The Red Page
The Armies
British.
Arthur Conan Doyle’s first volume of
his history of the war, The British Campaign
in France and Flanders, 1914 (llodder and
Stoughton, 65.), is admirably written. He
claims that his narration is approximately
true, in many cases authoritative, and very
precisely correct in its detail. Owing to
the general rearrangements recently made
in , the Army the restrictions which forbade
the mention of numbered units have now
been removed. The awful battering that
the little British Army received during its
retreat from Mons is revealed in all its
dreadful detail. “No finer force.... has ever
left the shores of Britain. It is a conser-
vative estimate to say that within four
months a half of their number were either
dead or in the hospitals.” The first British
soldier to be killed in tlie war was probably
an aviator whose shattered machine was
found to the north-west of Mons. The
destruction of that machine was of great
strategic importance; for that aviator
would have returned with the unexpected
news that the Germans were in force in
that region. Mons would, no doubt, have
been immediately abandoned and the whole
Army brought swiftly into a position of
strategical safety. The British had against
them “the chosen legions of the most won-
derful army that the world had ever seen—-
an army that represented the last possible
word on the material and mechanical side
of war.” French discovered later that
instead of two German corps he had been
fighting four, and that the French armies
on his right were already in full retreat,
while nothing substantial lay upon his left.
He had to retreat to escape being sur-
rounded.
The rest of the story is that of a small clot
of khaki surrounded on three sides by a
sea of grey, the clot slipping swiftly south
and with equal swiftness growing smaller,
but every now and then pausing and holding
up that huge ocean of grey. The losses were
only paralleled by the heroism of that
valiant band. “History has shown many
times before that a retreating British Army
still retains a sting in its tail.” At Le
Cateau the men were so exhausted that
Smith-Dorrien said to French, “The only
thing for the men to do when they can’t
stand is to lie down and fight”; and French
had to assent. So the men fought, being
too tired to walk. There is no doubt that
this decision of Smith-Dorrien’s was crucial.
“Had the artillery gone the Army would
have gone. Had the Army gone the Ger-
mans had a clear run into Paris It is
impossible to doubt that the Germans, in
spite of their preponderating numbers, were
staggered by the resistance which they had
encountered. In no other way can one
explain the fact that their pursuit, which
for three days had been incessant, should
now, at the most critical instant, have eased
off. The cavalry and guns staved off the
final blow, and the stricken infantry stag-
gered from the field....So ended the peril-
ous, costly and almost disastrous action of
Le Gateau.”
d*
Conan Doyle admits that the troops
were utterly exhausted, disorganised as
bodies, but still preserving their moral as
individuals. The units of infantry had
become considerably mixed, and depleted by
quite 50 per cent., and the abandonment of
packs, etc., gave their pursuers every evi-
dence of a rout. “In many cases the whole
kits of the officers were deliberately sacri-
ficed,” says Doyle, with unconscious humor,
as evidently the kit of a British officer is
sacred; and adds: “Many men were
delirious from exhaustion and incapable of
understanding an order.” Yet the troops
re-formed. And Joffre admitted that the
flank of the long line of the French armies
had been saved by the hard fighting and
the self-sacrifice of the British.
But though the German Army was finally
driven back and pinned at the Aisne, the
Huns had another army ready, and the
Allies had not. Hence the race to the sea
and the first dreadful battle of Ypres. How
the depleted British Army held Ypres is
almost a miracle. Doyle rightly terms this
battle one of the decisive moments of the
world’s history, for the Huns almost held
the Channel ports within their hands. “It
was the closest thing to a really serious
defeat whieh the Army had had since Le
Cateau.” French is reported to have said
that there .was no time in the Mons retreat
when he did not see his way, but there was
a. moment in this battle when he thought
himself to be at the end of his resources.
“The fact that three divisions of infantry
with brigades which resembled battalions,
and battalions which were anything from
companies to platoons, destitute of reserves,
barred the path to a powerful German army,
is one of the greatest feats of military his-
tory. It was a very near thing.... The
fact that the enemy did not recoil and that
there was no sensational 'capture of prisoners
and guns has obscured the completeness of
the victory A German force of at least
600,000 men set forth to reach the coast,
and was opposed by a force of less than
half its numbers who barred the way. The
Germans did not advance five miles in a
month of fighting, and they lost not less
than 150,000 men without any military ad-
vantage whatever. .. .If this is not a great
victory, I do not know what military
achievement would deserve the term.”
AUSTRALIAN.
Philip F. E. Schuler, war correspondent
of Melbourne Age, writes a lucid and com-
prehensive account of the A.I.F. and its
Anzac adventure. Curiously, Schuler omits
all mention of the contingent that captured
the Hun colonies in the Pacific. The author
left Australia on the flagship of the first
convoy, went with the troops into camp at
Mena, and though not allowed to go to
Gallipoli with the transports, he managed to
get to the scene of action in a Greek trad-
ing steamer, and then with other corres-
pondents watched the operations from a
motor-launch. That craft was soon ban-
ished by the British destroyers; but a
couple of months later Schuler got General
Hamilton's permission to visit the Anzac
front.
In Australia in Arms (Unwin, 7s. 6d.)
Schuler throws new light upon several
events. The delay of three weeks suffered
by the first convoy was due, he tells us,
to the Dominion Government refusing to
despatch the 10 M.L. troopships without ad-
equate escort, and the warships had to go
across and fetch the Maorilanders. Schuler’s
presence on the flagship enables him to give
a full account of the Sydney-Emden fight.
He points out the dramatic fact that the
fleet of 40 crowded ships which was so
anxious to avoid a meeting with the German
raider should itself catch the Emden red-
handed. The 30,000 Australasians in the
fleet had a narrow escape of taking an
involuntary part in that fight; for the
Emden crossed the path of the convoy, which
had every light extinguished, barely three
hours ahead. “One can only conjecture what
might have happened" had the lights not
been doused.” Schuler gives a dramatic
account of that thrilling encounter. Both
the Melbourne and the Japanese warship
Ibuki itched to go; but Captain Silver, of
the Melbourne, as senior officer, had to stqy
with the convoy, and also had to quell the
Ibuki’s eagerness to join the Sydney. To
her second signal, “I wish go,” Captain
Silver reluctantly refused his permission,
pointing out that her duty was to guard
the convoy. After the destruction of the
Emden, a typical naval message was sent
to these two cruisers: “Sorry there was not
enough meat to go round.”
Another fact divulged is that in the first
days at Anzac Army Orders issued for a
general advance, which, if successful, would
have opened the way to Constantinople,
were altered by a mere chance word. In-
stead of this general advance all that was
attempted was a mere straightening of the
line. This great wasted opportunity “was,
to put it plainly, faulty Staff work.” Gen-
eral Bridges took the responsibility upon
himself, fearing that its execution would
open a dangerous gap in the Anzac line,
and the Army Orders remained unfulfilled
and, strangely enough, uneaneelled. “That
there should have been a general advance
is recognised on all hands, and there is no
doubt in the minds of many Generals with
whom I have spoken that it would have
been possible that day, had proper provi-
sion been made in the original orders for
the filling of the very vital gap in the
centre of our line. The whole lamentable
incident must be put down as indicative of
bad Staff work—for thus it was that the
whole future of Anzac was changed by a
chance meeting of three senior officers on
the main ridge and General Bridges’ firm
decision.”
MAORILAND.
Captain Malcolm Ross, official war-corres-
pondent with the M.L. Forces, is the author
of a rather scrappy collection of war studies,
entitled Light and Shade in War (Arnold,
55.). The collection has no plan, and the
author ought perhaps to be called the
Malcolm Ross Family, since his son, Noel
(late lance-corporal with the Anzacs and
Lieutenant, Territorial Artillery, and now
of The Times), and, presumably, his wife,
have also contributed. Instead of a book
dealing solely with the M.L. Billjim’s mate
from Maoriland—a book that the world is
waiting for, the Ross Family offer us jour-
nalistic sketches of Malcolm Ross’s sciatica,
his various dug-outs, sketches of England
and Scotland, impressions of the British
Navy and other ephemera. However, there
is enough of the M.L. Billzac to interest the
reader, though the Ross Family is rather
the conscientious journalist than the brilliant
war-correspondent. Memorable, though, is
his description of the two casualties at The
Landing. “At the bottom I saw a wounded
man bleeding badly over one shoulder. He
grinned hideously with his shattered mouth.
‘Got it where the chicken got the axe,’ he
wheezed, and fainted as the stretcher-bearers
came up to him.... Two bullets lobbed into
the trench parapet and then the man next
to me stood straight up and fell back over
my legs. ‘Mafeesh,’ he said quaintly, the
Arabic for ‘finished,’ and then more slowly,
‘Money-belt—missus and kids —dirty swine.
dirty .’ Then a strange thing happened.
Dying, shattered beyond recognition, he
rose to his knees and dragged his rifle to
the parapet. With a weak finger he took
shaky aim at the sky and fired his last shot,
to collapse finally in the bottom of the
trench.”
Useful, too, is Ross’s definition of the
various batches of men. The first contin-
gent became known as “The Tourists.”
They were out to see a bit of the world, and
to do any fighting that came along. Then
came “The Dinkums,” followed by “The
Super-Dinkums,” “The War-Babies,” and
“The Hard Thinkers,” the latter having-
thought a great deal before they came.
Incidentally, Ross’s use of “Tommy,” or
worse, “Colonial ‘Tommy,’ ” for an Anzac
private will surely not please that bloke. It
is high time that the M.L. private christened
himself.
From an interesting description of the
modern raid we learn that officers and men
taking part are now supplied with chewing-
gum. It is to prevent the men, waiting to
go over the parapet, getting into a state of
nervous strain that leads to coughing. A
cough might cost you your life. You can’t
smoke; but you are told to chew. The
Maori is interesting in raiding. He
fashions billhooks into the shape of the
mere of his ancestors; and he practises blows
that his people brought with them from
Hawaiiki, especially the temple-hit, which
severs the temporal artery and ends with a
screw that sometimes takes off the top of
the enemy’s, head.
Interesting journalistic stuff; but doubt-
less Malcolm Ross is at work on a real
book dealing exclusively and comprehen-
sively with the Anzac from Maoriland.
. “Hen” ; Why does The Bulletin devote
time and space as to whether Billjim should
include the Maorilander? I’m just back, so
that I don’t know how the argument started;
but I trust that it won’t develop into the
sort of thing one sees in daily papers about
an Australian in France looking sad and
annoyed when called an Anzac. ' Ask them
what they are and they will say “The ——
mugs.” Amongst each other, those that
arent ‘Scotty” are “Digger” or “Nugget.”
I think I can assure The Bulletin that
what used to worry us in the line was when
we were going to be relieved; and when in
our billets the nearest estaminet at which
to get the least awful beer.
‘Copabella”: I protest. Dennis makes Ginger
Mick to say: “Per I might be King o’ Footscray if
wed ad more discipline.” In the name of the
Prophet, why Footscray? Is it not enough that we
nave been an overworked pantomime gag for years:
I s “ enough that the ignorant and unthinking
.nolU their noses at our name on account of the sins
of Kensington, across the river from us and never
withm our municipal jurisdiction—without having
fathered upon us the social misdeameanor of the
P, * ■ Know ye that within the memory of our
Oldest Inhabitant there has never been a push in
Footscray except the harmless necessary one that
does nothing more frightful than polish the culvert
handrails at the Nicholson-street corner with its
time-payment clothes, and fill the shop doorways with
sputum, cigarette butts and childish barrack. Know,
too, that spite of its 30,000 people and the fact that
they are wholly industrialists apt to take their
pleasures more strenuously than dwellers in Anaemia
and Neuritis and other “tony” suburbs, its police-
court statistics compare favorably with the toniest.
I have haunted the local court and police station in
vain for copy, and the only big cases in years have
been due to the aberrations of visitors from other
parts. What then have we done that we should be
butchered to make a poet’s scansion fit? Dennis’s
part in this conspiracy of belittlement of one of the
most progressive and clean-living cities in Australia
is no doubt unintentional, but the belittlement is
there—-the name “Footscray” is a byword and a gibe
far and near. We of Footscray, knowing the place,
raise protesting hands to high Heaven and ask:
Why?
r “Coriander” rebukes us: The Red Page '
(25/1/T7), in reviewing lan Macleod’s
novel, Hack’s Brat, says that the life in
the mining camp is admirably drawn. Well,
I should like the opinion of a jury of con-
tributors to the Aboriginalities and Wild
Cat columns on this. The camp is fifty
miles the other side of Yantabulla, and the
miners take up small claims (no mention
of any Warden’s Court or office) and pro-
ceed to sink through ironstone (the hero
sank his shaft singlehanded) to reach the
clayey soil which shows them they are on
gold. Then a company comes along and
crushes the “quartz reef.” There is no men-
tion of any water supply, but teams bring-
ing machinery are bogged in a swamp. It
is explained that this is in drought time.
The landscape is freely decorated with bush
fires as a matter of everyday happening.
The Brat, on page 60, expresses a liking
for death-adders and rattlesnakes. In the
pub they speak a sort of stagey dialect,
Cynthia uses the expressions “elevated ass”
and “piffle,” and the pub takings are kept
in a treacle-tin in the bar. Even after
John has lived at the camp for some time
he knows no better than to ride from Yanta-
bulla on his bicycle, 50 miles of scrub and
sand, with a suit-case in his hand.
_ Pa S e abjectly apologises for not being
The .Wild Cat Column. What it humbly meant
when it suggested that the life in the mining camp
was admirably drawn was that the atmosphere of
the mining camp was admirably suggested: and
though it noted various small errors in the Sydney
part of the yarn, it did not think them large enough
for notice. Evidently the author knows about as
much of mining as the Red Page does. Possibly she
knows more. It would be dead easy.]
" Tom Collins "
According- to Bernard O’Dowd, “in the
whole Bulletin family there is no more
sincere writer than ‘Tom Collins,’ and in
his particular corner, of the library I do not
think I am doing injustice to anyone in say-
ing that on the whole our finest production
of prose literature up to the present is Such
is Lifer Bernard O’Dowd is welcome to
that generous opinion; but The Poems of
Joseph Furphy (“Tom Collins”), collected
and edited by K.B. (Lothian, Melb.; 55.),
does not bear out the first part of Bernard
O’Dowd’s contention, while with Furphy’s
prose works we are not at present dealing.
“Tom Collins'’ was certainly a fore-runner,
and this page agrees with O’Dowd that: “It
will not take much critical acumen to trace,
when one remembers how long ago most of
his works were written, the paternity of
more than one recent school of Australian
literature to ‘Tom Collins.’ ” Tom Collins
was a pioneer; he has all the crudity, the
strength, and the weakness of the pioneer.
His “Fsalm of Counsel” is straight Aus-
tralian, spoken at a time where there was
much need for that variety of speech; and
for that fine, definite Australian creed this
nation should be grateful. But his verses, as
a whole, display little but shrewd sense
clothed in clever rime; and to the reader who
meets him thus “collected” for the first
time, “Tom Collins” will he welcomed not for
his creed—which has by now become a com-
monplace, and is threatening to become a
narrow curse—but for his fertility in riming
and his casual Australian humor. It seems
a pity, since the volume may have some his-
torical importance, that it is disfigured by
obvious misprints.
The inclusion of his fugitive verse in this
collection does not help the reputation of
“Tom Collins.” “Brahm” is a remarkable
effort, and almost defies classification. It is
casual and colloquial, and worries the
modern reader, like so many of the pieces of
“Tom Collins,” by the author’s pertinacious
search for rimes for the one syllable; but ii
gets its effect of impressiveness by these
very qualities. It is metaphysics in topical
verse. “The Gumsucker’s Dirge” and “The
Schoolhouse on the Plain” (too long for quo-
tation) are excellent “Abo.” verses, full of
humor and sentiment, done with the neatest
of touches. These extracts from his “Psalm
of Counsel” adequately show the poet’s
message and method :
Though some good folks may take it ill,
As trifling with parsonic frill,
Thus saith the Lord to Jim and Bill,
In admonition stern and straight:
Ye hold from Me the brightest zones,
The fairest realm this planet owns,
Guarded on every side by Jones,
And standing yet inviolate. ...
Australia is the unit, mind!
With bounds unchangeably defined;
A continent to you assigned —•
That is the primary postulate.
One “angry cloth ” to call your own;
One scorn for every brand of drone;
One slant-eyed menace- — yours alone!—
Involving each ingredient State.
The Commonwealth is paramount;
City or province merely count
A.s streamlets from that central fount —
Provided you are Up To Date....
Now take the tip of Holy Writ —
You won't survive unless you're fit;
And something more than honest grit
Must go to make a people great.
An Asiatic boundary fence
Is little better than pretence
Unless you're white in every sense —
Unless, in fact, you're Up To Date. ...
Touching your own forefathers’ case,
Ask History what has taken place
Since Dago legions made the pace —
Say, Anno Domini 78.
Thrice has the Motherland been lost —
Three separate times has she been bossed
By enterprising foes who crossed
The German Sea or Dover Strait;
While Bulldog Boys, with clods and sticks,
Failed to frustrate their knavish tricks;
Hence Freedom's show was simply nix —
Which came of not being Up To Date....
Look out for snakes among the grass —
The noisy parish-minded ass;
The paltry devotee of Class;
The preacher of sectarian hate. .
To give such pests an honest deal,
With justice to the public weal,
You may respect their narrow zeal,
But count them foes icithin the gate.
Should they as candidates appear,
Dispose them in their proper sphere,
That is to say, upon their ear —
Your statesmen must be Up To Date. ...
The point of honor is your crux;
Run always straight and chance the duckt;
For in this world of constant flux
The higher type must dominate.
All fetich forms you may neglect.
But vices that command respect,
And virtues that are least correct, -
You ivill do well to cultivate.
Confront the proud, sustain the weak,
And not for you shall Freedom shriek
Till falls your Kosciusko peak—
Assuming you are Up To Date.

Modern Chemistry
and the
Art of Shaving
At last Chemistry has
taken upon herself to
teach us the true art of

SHAVING CREAM

Australia Riddled with
DeadlyKidivey Disease
ALMOST EVERYBODY COMPLAINING.
Kidney disease is most certainly on the in-
crease. Thousands and thousands of people
in the towns and villages of this country are
in terrible danger of deadly kidney disease.
This insidious disease creeps 'on, gradu-
ally getting a tinner and firmer grip, until
when it is too late the poor sufferer discovers
he has Bright’s Disease. Have ’you kidney
trouble? Do you know how to recognise
whether you are suffering from this or not?
Here are some of the symptoms of kidney
disease: Aches and pains in various parts of
the body, nasty taste in the mouth first tliing-
in the morning, constipation, cloudy and bad-
colored urine, general feeling of weakness
and irritability, puffiness under the eyes—
these are all symptoms of kidney trouble.
And then people who suffer from backache,
rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone or
pains in the bladder, muscles and joints are
all suffering these pains through weak or
diseased kidneys, for these are the sure and
certain symptoms of kidney trouble.
All these things mean that the kidneys are
weak and diseased. They need strengthening
and making healthy.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Night-Ride.
Faster speed w r e through the bracken,
Catch me closer to your heart!
Clench the reins before they slacken
Best the frighted filly start!
Oh. the blazing pennons whirling
Hu by jewels on the grass
And the burnished blossoms curling
Into phantoms as we pass!
Dowm the slender tongue of tracking
Let her fly, she cannot trip!
Back of us we hear the cracking
Of the scarlet stockman’s whip.
He is rounding up his cattle —
Fiery steers and steeds of gold,
Crimson stallions; hear them rattle
Through the forest fold on fold !
He is groaning with his plunder.
Turn her head toward the creek !
Though his feet be swift as thunder
We will hear his angry shriek
As we gallop helter-skelter
Through the cool and plashing tide,
To the land of peace and shelter
On the safe and southern side.
On he follows. Nearer, nearer,
King his brumby’s brazen feet.
Clipping-clopping, clearer, clearer—
Death’s the fire we must defeat.
Keep your lips on mine, my darling.
Let the flame-flowers lick my hair;
Love can brook the angry snarling
Of their passionate despair.
Cross the creek —he cannot follow!
Love will ever conquer all.
Down we canter through the hollow
Safe at last from fear and fall.
* * * *
Thus I fancied we were speeding
Nil night long, with Love’s control,
From our Passion and its pleading
To the safety of our Soul.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
New Moon.
A tiny wind that stirs the leaves
Creeps rustling o’er the high hill-crest
To fill with scent of gathered sheaves,
The dim lands fading to the west.
Stars twinkle shyly in its wake,
Around it plumy moths arise,
And at its sigh in every brake
Flash fairy limbs and laughing eyes;
Flash slender limbs athwart the dusk
Where drone the beetles, booming, blind.
Mark ! the wee feet on blade and husk !
The fairy horns upon the wind !
Hark to the honeyed whispering tone
That wreathes with echoes delicate,
With fairy laughter lightly blown,
The portals of night’s opening gate.
Where bubble-like a lantern swings
Its fragile globe of lucent gold—
The thin, new moon that clasping rings
The glimmering circle of the old.
VICTORIA.
SYD O.
Blood Tryst.
Now who urtj, they would come to me
Down the night, down the night?
There’s a grave below the hawthorn tree,
And a broken wall at the hill’s grey knee,
And a stain of blood on the soul of me
For an hour’s delight.
They whisper long, the grey ghosts three,
Through the night, through the night—
Drey ghosts of all that a man would be,
Grey-ghosts of all that a man would see
A hen the soul of him broke in pieces three
For an hour's delight.
A rose she wore for all to see—
Sweets of night, sweets of night—
And she set her mouth on the soul of me
With the fire of her kisses throe times
three....
Oh. it grew too dark for my soul to see
In the dusk’s delight.
Now who was this would come to me
Down the night, down the night?
A bitter word. His sword went free.
And a bitter point at his throat would be,
And the blood of him red on her and me
For an hour’s delight.
Who dug a grave below the tree
All the night, all the night?
A lire was in the soul of me....
She clung and sobbed about his knee
And she cursed me black below the tree
For an hour’s delight.
Oh, fair she was to touch and see—
Soft as night, soft as night—
But the dead man’s eyes I could not flee.
Nor the black of the curse she set on me;
And the blood between us was red to see
For an hour’s delight.
They whisper long, the grey ghosts three,
Through the night, through the night.
The ghost 6f all that a man would be.
The ghosts of love and the shame in me
"When the soul of me broke in pieces three
For an hour’s delight.
Why will ye walk and come to me
Down the night, down the night?
There’s rest' in the grave by the hawthorn
tree
And the deaths I die are three times three
One death for you and all deaths for me
For an hour’s delight!
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. Gd. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, to any
address in Australasia, post paid.
Post free to any address abroad, £ 1 10s. per annum.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. tvhen
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 15, 1817.
“SCABBING” ON THE CHILDREN.
This is the record of the Commonwealth’s
performance as an importer and exporter of
goods other than gold during the last two
years:—
Imports. Exports.
1915 .. £68,599,914 £00,231,908
1916 . . 81,118,605 73,363,394
In the financial year 1915-16, July to June,
the exports were short of the imports by
£12,717,356; in the previous year by
£5.878,871. Having started the war by
spending about half a million a month more
on imported goods that could be paid for
with the products sold, proceedings have been
livened up until the margin between what
Australia is buying and what it is selling
is substantially over a million a month, for
on the figures of the past half-year it is clear
that the imports are steadily increasing.
Prior to 1912 Australia had for several
years a balance of exports over imports. In
that year the slide commenced. The rush
downhill is now on greased rails. Debt is
being built up at a rate which can have only
one possible result. Meantime it is an
illuminating circumstance that the more
frantically the nation borrows the more
monumental grows the trading debt against
the people—the greater the drain for interest
from a relatively lower production.
All this mass of imported material has to
be paid for either by goods or by loans; and
it is by means of the lOU that the foreign
bondholder and the exporter to Australia are
now having their demands satisfied. How
much longer the Commonwealth Parliament
proposes to sanction so gross an abuse of
the national credit this paper has not even
the ghost of an idea. Nor is it possible to
even guess what kind of devil’s broth Aus-
tralian public men imagine they are brewing
for the community. But there is nothing more
certain than that if the Commonwealth had
a Government worthy of the citizens’ regard,
some explanation would at least be given of
the grounds upon which it sanctions a policy
of ruin. And particularly would the Minis-
try explain how it imagines that the Aus-
tralian people are going to grapple with the
frightful debt of war, plus the burdens con-
tracted in previous years, if they go on
plunging into debt for imported goods at the
rate of a million a month. .It is the wildest
and most reckless gamble ever indulged in
by any country on earth. While flustered
time-servers are blithering about “winning 1
the war” the country's strength is being
sapped away. While phrase-mongers talk
about “developing our industries” they
pawn the public assets, and the proceeds are
used to give outside artisans aud
tu.rers a job. While the air is filled With
loud shouts about conserving out* strength,
the Customs Departmeht reports that in the
last six months Australia spent £12.705,475
on imported apparel—four millions more
than during the final half of the previous
year. In six months preceding January 1
the volume of foreign merchandise landed in
Australia was greater than for the whole 12
months of 1906.
* * * *
No excuse for this maniacal waste is
possible. To gird at the public for “private
extravagance” is futile. Individuals are not
to blame. It is the public policy that is
wrong—the evil habit, from which even war
has failed to purge our Parliaments, of
promoting a bogus prosperity by discounting
bills .payable the day after to-morrow. It is
easy for people with long tongues . and
feather-heads to say that the country will by
“increased production” make good the trade
debts of these later years—that our great
powers of productiveness are being enhanced
by our borrowings! for public works. Trash of
this sort long ago ceased to be believed even
by the people who employed it. Actually their
borrowings are for millinery and ornate
shoes and whisky and cigars; for all the
complicated stock-in-trade of t the Importer,
the effect of loans always being to load us
with manufactured goods to the value of the
money raised. When the politician borrows
a few millions in London the transaction
merely consists of postponing payment of a
sheaf of bills for embalmed fish and silk
stockings and musical instruments and so
forth, utilising the money to keep Aus-
tralians moving behind a pick and shovel or
running a shop where cash, really owing to
someone at the other end of the earth, is
taken over the counter in exchange for stock
procured on tick. And the ultimate fate pi
a country given to lunacy of this kind is
that in process of time it becomes inhabited
by a community spending most of its exist-
ence struggling to pay interest on the ship-
loads of boots and pants and potted aspara-
gus and motor-cars that in the distant past
its fathers trafficked with. This is what
Australia is deliberately preparing for now.
Increased production will not be for the
benefit of the people who produce. The
people who will not produce now, and who
are being incited by Parliaments to clothe
themselves in glad-rags, are “scabbing” on
their children. The cataract of imports is
the outward and visible sign of an economic
and industrial sabotage more sinister than
anything the wildest visionary of the I.W.W.
even dreamed of.
* * *
And while Australia has been importing
the products of oversea labor at a rate un-
dreamed of in days of peace, the Premier of
N. S. Wales is heard moaning to a deputa-
tion of Laborites that he cannot “provide
employment” for some thousands of men who
were employed before on the proceeds of
loans raised to keep people employed in
another country. The more Holman has
borrowed the more he has had to borrow to
keep the game going. The more people he
has employed the greater has grown his
danger and theirs. They have done nothing
towards the vital task of building a nation.
They have been working in order that a
greater variety of imports might be accumu-
lated. And the horrified Holman is now in
the position of a man called upon either to
borrow incredible millions or to explain when
the great increase of production that his
other millions were going to lead to _ will
happen. The circumstances for explaining
this are very favorable, for not only is in-
creased production wanted to fill up the hole
created by explosions in France, but it is
urgently required to liquidate the charges
involved in the importing mania. If by his
frantic distribution of lOU’s and dealings
with Norton Griffiths and Co., and by
the valiant achievements of six other
Treasurers, Australia cannot be brought to
a stage at which she can produce enough to
pay for imported clothes and food and
drinks, the question when she will, and how
the war is going to be paid for, is worth
thinking about. The people should be told
what views Holman and the rest of them
entertain on this subject. At present it
looks as if they are going on “all in” prin-
ciples, and damning the consequences. Mani-
festly this cannot go on for ever. The pro-
blem now is whether the Australian people
can produce enough to provide for their
necessities instead of producing an intoler-
able burden for the descendants. If they
can they ought to start instead of slouching
round praying that someone shall lend the
nation a million or two to buy production
from people somewhere else. These import-
figures yell their warning that in every
the policy of self-reliance,
industrial development and nationalism,
preached by the glib tongues of political
partisans up and down the Commonwealth,
is being strangled.
WAR WITHOUT BLOODSHED.
After all, Woodrow Wilson may be teach-
ing the world some of its biggest lessons.
If his enunciation of the principle of Peace
without Victory should be followed by a
practical attempt to conduct War without
Bloodshed, the Millennium might yet get a
start in a fashion that would surprise
the Professor himself. The plain fact, which
all men must admit, stands written in blaz-
ing cordite and red blood, that the whole
theory of war is a setting of the boot above
the brain and a minimising of the values of
all things which were previously regarded
as standing for the uplift of humanity.
Brute force, backed by everything noisy and
destructive, must settle the international
controversy; and pure intellect and nobler
emotion have very little say in it. Tom
Carlyle valued the works of Shakspeare
at a greater price than the whole Indian
Empire, and any person possessed of the
higher civilised intelligence will agree with
him; but Macbeth wouldn’t stop a German
attack on a single furlong of trefiches, nm-
could Hamlet turn aside the bullets of so
much as one Turkish snipei’. The finest
achievement of the wbrld’s greatest artist
can be destroyed by. the bursting Of a Single
shell; find the hOblest philosophic idea ol’
religions purpose has a poor chance of hold-
ing its own against the blind, mad butchery
Of a bayonet' charge. More than this, war’s
vile feature is that it reduces to a mere
futility the best that evolution has done in
the way of producing manhood. Some-
where in the Belgian ranks Maeterlinck
is serving his country. He stands for some-
thin? . infinitely high in the development of
his race, for a glory of mental achievement
worth whole generations of lesser men.
Yet war takes no account of all this; and a
Prussian swashbuckler with an intelligence-
which would have been in no way remarkable
in the Stone Age is easily his master in the
field. There is certainly a kind of intellect
required for handling vast armies and
planning the strategy of campaigns; but
great generalship, closely analysed, dues nbt
imply great manhbod. Some generals pf
lofty renown wefe thin-minded, hard people
with a capacity in one direction only and a
dullness of sensibility that made that capa-
city workable in the stress of battle. Othef
great warriors—like the late tk J. CiESAfi;
of Rome—Were, not such marvellous strat-
egists or tactieiaiiSj but by theit big intel-
lectual power Were magnificent headers bf
hiem The latter ate, however, tne excep-
tions. War dbek not make its appeal to the
brain. Its business is the boot and the fist,
and heads are cheap enough when the guns
shout their chorus.
* * * *
Necessarily, war, which is the black back-
ground of all international politics, has viti-
ated and destroyed not only personal values
but State values as well. _ A country is
considered great in proportion to the num-
ber of rifles it can fire and the Weight. of
shells it can throw. A small cbuhtry which
had riseii, to the highest leyel Intellectually
and morally and Which stdod aS the beacbil-
light bf mankind Would be of no account
befelde a vast horde of semi-savage fighting
men organised under a brutal and inflexible
despotism. There was a time when the
little State of Athens—a republic smaller
in area than the modern Montenegro—was
easily worth all the rest of the world put
together. Had everything else been sud-
denly wiped out with poison-gas or fire
from Heaven, the remaining race of men
could have been very little poorer and might
have been infinitely better off. But modern
war can take no account of such things;
for, as a fighting force, the highly civilised
nation would seem to be at a distinct dis-
advantage in arguing with a nation of semi-
barbarians, even if equal to. it in numerical
strength and material Wealth. It is simply
a, ease bf putting the skilled artisan and
the poet to hght the nigger pugilist and the
ship’s bliity; The more highly civilised men
may win by superior science, but the
chances are they have little enough know-
ledge of the thing that is called scieuce in
the realm of butchery. Every further ad-
vance towards the bigger dreams of man-
kind must aggravate this disadvantage; and
it is evident that humanity as 'a whole can-
not get far forward while the savage in man
is allowed to retain the power of destroying
all that has been achieved. Every nation of
modern Europe really repudiates the idea
of war and regards it with loathing and
contempt; yet every nation has by custom
come to regard the awful thing as one of
the settled conventions of humanity and, in
spite of its better sense, glorifies it by refer-
ences to past national achievement in the
shambles called battle. What we want is a
new custom, a new convention based upon
modern thought and feeling. That war is a
permanent feature of the universal man-
scape must be taken, as an axiom, but that
war need involve murder and bloodshed and
every sort of crime is utterly ridiculous.
There are far finer weapons than the sword
and gun, and far keener wounds can be in-
flicted than those made in the flesh of armed
peasants. In a word, war should be lifted
to the intellectual field and its ruthlessness
allowed play only by means of those things
which man possesses as man distinct from
the animal.
* * * *
Consider the possibilities. If, by a con-
gress of the nations, meeting, say, in 1900, it
had been decided that arms were no longer
to be manufactured by any nation, and that
only an international police force should
have military training, the trouble of 1914
would have been met by Germany and Aus-
tria declaring war on France and Russia.
At once the nations concerned would have
cut off all trading and social relations and
ignored each other’s existence. No French
thought, idea, manufacture or product would
have crossed the frontier, and Germany
would have strictly kept all her Ivultur,
dyes, chemicals and blowers on brass instru-
ments for home consumption or sale to
neutrals. Outsiders would have watched the
struggle with keen interest; and the nations
concerned would have used every endeavor
to produce things new, valuable and strange
in which the others could have no share.
German propaganda in England and
America would have devoted itself to the
task of making France look ridiculous in the
eyes of her neighbors, and Gallic invention
would have taken the same humorous turn.
There would have been loud mockery of
French fashions in Berlin and keen jest
at German table manners in Paris; and all
the while vague ideas of the beauty and
wonder of forbidden Parisian delights would
have disturbed Gretciien and irritated
llans, while French scientists wondered if
they were being left behind and French
socialists chafed at the notion that Fritz
was putting Marxian theories to a practical
test. No railway traffic across the frontiers
would have been permitted, no letters
allowed to pass between the rival countries;
and each would have done its utmost to so
work the world’s money markets as to
destroy the credit of the other. It would
have been real war, war to the last franc
or mark, but not a drop of blood would have
been shed. The fighters would have been
the financiers, inventors, manufacturers,
scientists, painters, poets and musicians—
in fact, the organised and embattled intelli-
gence of the warring nations; For a year or
tWo it Would have been impossible to tell
how siifch strife was going; then results
would have begun to , manifest themselves,
dhie natioh which could db least Without the
Other would have begun to waver. A feeler
for peace would have been thrown out and
possibly responded to; and the final result
would be Peace without Victory, of the
Wilson pattern. The mere statement of
the course of such a war looks mad to minds
accustomed to the frightfulness of our daily
meal of horrors; but it is sane enough. If
men must fight, that is how they should
fight—indeed it is the only way in which
they can fight as men. Up to 1914 the
nations fought as wild beasts; since then
the Germans have taught them to fight like
devils.
WAR ORPHANS AND THEIR WEL-
FARE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Fhe first principle of patriotism is national
integrity. War orphans, direct descendants
from tiie fittest of the race to-day, represent
the flower, in bud; of the race; and stand for
that integrity:
M'ecognising tfiisi; each fieliigefent is. filah-
iiitig for the preservation Bf these cliilateii.
A hateful analysis of the different methods
in, practice shows that France has devoted
most energy and shown most foresight in her
efforts; Britain, most advanced in many
social movements, has paid as little as any.
It is not, I believe, for lack of heart. It is
rather for want of thought. It is to stir up
thought that I put here on record what our
noblest Ally has done.
On August 2, 1914, close on the heels of
the mobilisation order, the following circular
Was issued throughout France i
Any child whose father i§ mobilised; if brbugiit
tB the People’s University of Paris, will bfe.imffle-
diately received without any formalities, and most
affeetibriately taken care of by friends of the Univer-
sity and housewives of the neighborhood. Fathers are
assured that, whatever happens, their little ones will
never be abandoned to vice and misery.
This promise, emanating from a popular
movement amongst the women of Paris,
noted philanthropists, litterateurs and public
men too old to take up arms, laid the foun-
dation of the Association Nationals des
Orphelins do la Guerre, which is to-day one
of the most complete, humane and system-
atic efforts that the world has ever seen.
The society proceeded to the root of the vital
problem at once. It received, maintained
and educated orphans, first at Its‘own ex-
pense, and later with the aid of national,
departmental, local and particular subsidies,
tjnder able administration its good Services
SOon spread over the length and breadth of
the nation, improving as they matured. So
Well had the matter in hand in the Republic
and its dependencies been mastered that,
when Italy opened hostilities against the
common enemy, it was decided to receive
motherless children of mobilised Italians;
and, later, when gallant little Servia was so
hard-pressed, the society organised an expedi-
tion to that hapless storm-centre to succor
what infants were fortunate enough to escape
the heel of the ruthless invader and the
rigors of the terrible retreat through
Albania. Incidentally, the privations endured
by this expedition are indescribable.
' The children thus succored live in colonics
which have been established in various parts.
Each colony, or settlement, is worked upon
a family system, based upon a selection of
adopted mothers widowed or disabled h,v
the ravages of war. Each one of these
resides in a separate pavilion or villa, and is
entrusted with the care of 10 orphans. In
order that they may enjoy the advantages of
social intercourse in addition to the comtorts
of a home thus provided, the children have
meals and play in common. So successful
has this system proved in the working that
the society has refused many generous offers
of palatial buildings as seminaries, -colleges,
etc., on the ground that such large establish-
ments would involve a return to the old
orphanage and boarding-school 03
maintenance and education, which they
abhor. .
“Illegitimates” come under no ban. iherc
is no stain upon the child ; and the society
will allow none to be put there.
The younger orphans are placed in child*
nurseries, where healthy foster-mothers,
chosen from widows of the w T ar and passed
by leading physicians, suckle one infant each
besides their own. Primary education is
undertaken by school-mistresses, appointed
and paid by the State, who reside amongst
their pupils. It is the care of the society
to give the wards an occupation and teach
them a trade, always recognising that the
revival of the economic life of the nation
alone will furnish a decisive character to
military victory. Hence advanced educa-
tion is entrusted to professional men crippled
in the war; while others unable to earn a
living at their trades have the satisfaction
and comfort of teaching them to the offspring
of fallen comrades. . »
The health of the orphans is assiduously
looked to, and huge sanitariums have been
erected in the healthiest localities in I ranee
for the invalids and weaklings.
As regards religious training, the wishes
of the dead father, which-the society takes
the utmost pains to ascertain, are strictly
carried out. All denominations mix in one
colony. The society sets its face against
parcelling out the future citizens of the
nation into separate orphanages according
to their religions. Instead, it endeavors to
show its respect for the dead by maintaining
among their descendants that principle ot
national unity for which the fathers sacri-
ficed their lives.
Through the courtesy of Leon Ciiarbon-
net, an eminent man of letters, and his
estimable wife, who officiate over the large
colonies in the south of France, I have been
able to visit these settlements from time to
time and watch their development, ine
more one sees of them the more one must
admire this organisation, which at once safe-
guards the young and finds congenial and
useful employment for the wounded ana
widows of the fallen; and the more one must
. thrill for that noble patriotism whicn
brought it into being. I have seen these
“families,” and never, even at home, been
among healthier, heartier and happier; and


the adopted mothers seem to be full of tender
affection. I have seen subscription lists
built sou by sou and franc by franc out of
the poilu’s meagre pay. I have visited, the
great workshops where the blind and maimed
teach the sons of the nation; I have watched
the huge sewing and cookery classes, and
the schools of nursing and domestic economy
where the young daughters are learning to
be of service to the State; and if the bright
faces of the youngsters are symbolical, as,
no doubt they are. of the France-to-be, it is
certain that the new Republic will be as
sunny-dispositioned as the old. But above
and before all France has kept her compact
with those who made the grand sacrifice, and
in keeping it has assured her most cherished
ideal —national integrity.
* * * *
And what of ours? We rank as one of
the most patriotic of nations. The huge relief
funds in hand testify to Australia’s gener-
osity. Our soldiers as fighting men have few
equals. They are the best paid in the world.
Our women have faced casualty-lists with a
silent fortitude and worked for the creature
comforts of their men in a manner worthy
of the highest traditions of any country. The
war pensions and amelioration schemes have
been drawn up liberally and thoughtfully.
And yet no scheme guaranteeing the future
welfare of every war orphan in the Com-
monwealth has been inaugurated. Our good
friends at home have looked to the needs of
the Australia of to-day and overlooked that
sacred heritage left by those of our kin now
sleeping in warrior graves somewhere in
France, and in that remote resting-place by
the waters of the blue Aegean.
FRANCE.
J. HAMILTON.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Uselessness of Platitudinising.
Thus Frank Tudor, making a few well-
chosen remarks at a Melbourne recruiting
rally the other evening : —■
Personally, lie believed that if the war had been
postponed for a few years it would have been
possible that the nations of the earth would have
become civilised enough not to submit any difference,
however great, to the force of arms.
Voices: No ! Rot!
Mr. Tudor: Of course, none of us thought to-day
as we thought two years and a half ago. There was
a blood lust among the people to-day. We saw red
and thought red, and were unable to reason as we did
two years and a half ago. When we read of a
number of enemies being “wiped out” and sent to
their Maker we rejoiced. We did not think as we
used to think. We needed, in fact, to think back.
There is no meat on bones of this sort. It
is the language of futility, of inaction —-of
the queer individual who, when his house is
on fire, sits down to moan and groan and
wring his hands and warn the firemen not
to turn the water on lest they capture a
cold in the bead. What does it matter to
anyone except the members of the Bung-
wunga Debating Society what might have
happened if the war had been “postponed
for a few years”? The solid fact is that it
was not postponed; wherefore the people
at whom the German leaped have to deal
with a war in full bloom, not an adjourn-
ment that no one moved. And what
advantage is it to speak in a regretful tone
about people rejoicing over enemies sent to
their Maker or any other address? War is
hideous. The horrors of it are beyond
human conception. It is murder in whole-
sale quantities. Its demoralising influences
are fearful in their scope. But if the enemy
is not provided with a passage over the
Styx our own men will be, and that is the
beginning and end of the whole atrocious
business. People can be profoundly grieved
by the slaughter, shocked past expression
by the poignant tragedy of the trenches, and
yet be morally justified in entertaining feel-
ings of satisfaction that their army looks
like giving the other side a whipping. At
the battle of Naseby it was a matter for
profound regret by Oliver Cromwell that
a man whom he detected doing great execu-
tion for the Royalists was not battling for
him and the Lord instead of for Belial.
Cromwell was a practical person who
wanted to win. If Tudor wants the com-
munity to “think back” he can remind them
of nothing more appropriate than that Ger-
many made war on Australia, that the Ger-
man is not yet beaten and has been sending
a considerable number of Australian soldiers
to their Maker without remorse. And hav-
ing done this it seems a fair thing that he
should help them to think forward a bit.
Exhortations to be careful not to “see red,”
to cut “rejoicing” out of the schedule, to be
sorry the disturbance started, leave matters
hanging in the atmosphere and the enemy
banging at the door.
Winning the War and Squabbling Over
Jobs.
Hughes talked and talked again last week
about his attempt to form a National Gov-
ernment. He made no such attempt. He
did not want to. And Cook was in the
same boat. The “invitation” extended to
Tudor was forwarded, after the Tudorites
•had been reviled for many weeks, with the
certain knowledge that it would be rejected.
It was part of the game. Both Cook and
Hughes knew that they were only making
a demonstration for the entertainment of the
public—that their negotiations have all along
been based upon frowsy considerations of
party and personal advantage. The yarn
that these two men have been engaged in an
honest effort to rescue the Commonwealth
Parliament from the swamp into which it
has wandered is too thin, their talk about
“winning the war” just so much shoddy.
Hughes has said he “earnestly desired that
the men and women of all parties should
sink their differences and join in concentrat-
ing upon the great problems of the war and
Australia’s participation in the struggle.”
Employed by Hughes in the circumstances
of the moment, with Parliament as it is, the
temper of the people as it is known to be
and his reputation as a spinner of empty
phrases so firmly established, these words
were as devoid of meaning as they were of
honesty. What Hughes did not say was
this :—•
I want the Libs and Labs to join the Ministry, on
„his basis —that I shall be Prime Minister; that I
shall go to London; that we shall arrive at a surrep-
titious scheme for working the outside machine; that
there shall be an understanding about candidates at
the general election; that we shall get the British
Parliament to amend the Constitution and postpone
the election.
But it is what has been in his mind. His
tricky retention of an office held in defiance
of every principle of Parliamentary Govern-
ment, has been a daily proof to' the people
that he would rather see the Australian
Parliament paralysed and the country de-
prived of Responsible Government than per-
mit the Legislature to perform its proper
functions. He is an adventurer, enabled by
the Party system to seize the Parliamentary
machine, and desperately trying to gull the
public with cackle about the war while he
struggles to save his bacon. Yet this paper
does not blame him for what he is. He is
merely the product of a cursed system for
which the blame rests with the people.
The Cocky’s Htfme and. His Missus.
Ax item of interest at the next Easter
Show in Sydney should be the competitive
plans for dwellings to house returned soldiers
and other settlers on the land. This comes
of a prize offered by the G.-G. Evidently
his Ex. in his wanderings through the bush
has been smitten by the appalling architec-
ture. There is a horrible failure in the
average bush home to study climatic and
other local conditions. Apart from the appal-
ling travesty of human dwellings—medleys
of bagging, kerosene-tins and old galvanised-
iron that alternately freeze and grill the
cocky’s missus and kids—bush architecture
literally shrieks for reform right through,
from the undiluted tin box, dumped down in
an atmosphere of sun and sand and flies, to
the typical “suburban villa,” with its three-
foot verandah, narrow passage and orna-
mental windows, which is mostly the heart’s
pride of some “progressive” cocky who has
done well and believes in “making himself
comfortable.” These latter horrors are
among the most melancholy sights to be
found in the bush, and in commonsense are
about a million decades behind the old
station homestead, with its generous veran-
dahs all round, its big halls and windows, a
shingle roof for choice, and thick stone or
pise walls. The first consideration should be
fresh air and escape from the heat; space
is a mere detail, as there is no “fifty-foot
frontage” to cramp the designer, and com-
monsense points to something in the shape
of an inner courtyard or unroofed vestibule.
All the time, in the evolution of bush archi-
tecture, the cocky’s missus should be remem-
bered. She has been overlooked long enough.
On plenty of farms that ooze prosperity, and
where the last things in machinery and toil-
dodging devices are found outside, the old
woman still battles alone- with the same lack
of equipment that furrowed the face and pre-
maturely bowed her big-hearted grandmother
50, 60, 80 years ago. Between regular
births she still carts water a hundred yards
or two, washes in the open in a few battered
tubs that have to be filled and emptied by
hand, and cooks and bakes alongside an
open fire in- an untidy skillion kitchen with
an iron roof and an imitation window.
Bred of stout stuff, with strangely few
growls she suffers these and a score of other
needless soul-killing details that never strike
the old man when he is busy scheming to
secure the latest thing in headers and corn-
shellers. In these days of telephones and
cheap oil- and gas-engines, the well-to-do
cocky’s home should be as comfortable and
labor-saving as any suburban residence:
yet tlie average city toiler's missus
would tear up half her’ suburb if asked to
endure what her comparatively-affluent bush-
sister has to take as a matter of course. It
is time the latter woke up and made some
direct remarks to father. There is great
and joyous room for a union of cockies’
wives—perhaps that is why the average
cocky is such a fervid non-unionist. Yet
hundreds of him would gladly do all he could
to help the old woman if he happened to
think of it.
Australia First.
The cry of G. D. Meudell
Two things stir me to appeal to all good Aus-
tralians to consider how we ought to act unitedly
to prevent our country being entangled in foreign-
er shall we say, exotic? —policies and post-war busi-
ness arrangements. The one was the Paris pact or
Conference and the other the invitation to take
part in an Imperial Conference.
Parliamentary Governinent here and in. England
has broken down. The English people are asking
for new economic alliances to perpetuate old economic
heresies, and they arc crying out for another Parlia-
ment. So they invite us to join them in this fresh
adventure. Put frankly, Australia does not need
any more parties or Governments or Parliaments;
it badly wants to rationalise, not to nationalise, its
existing political and governmental system. Any
new movement ought not to be, like the projected
National Federation, a mere reshuffling of party
political aims and actions. It ought to be a universal
demand for honest finance, both Commonwealth and
State. That is the keystone of the national arch.
Good finance means good government, and no govern-
ment can ever be good in Australia which does not
administer an adequate scientific, comprehensive,
Protective tariff. We want an enlightened leader, a
champion born and bred in Australia who loves this
country and puts it before any other. We want a
McKinley, whose tariff re-created the United States,
to make permanent a system of Protection which
will secure our industrial independence. Last year
Victoria imported 11 million pounds more of goods
than she exported, mostly iron and woollen goods,
wearing apparel and paper. Australia is really an
inferior agricultural country, and her obvious destiny
because of her coal, iron and metals is to become
a gi eat manufacturing realm. The only way to bring
our coal, iron, oil, tin, copper and all the others
into full use is to become determined to strengthen
and expand our industrial life continuously and
strenuously.
But not by sinking our individuality and losing
our independence by blindly joining England in a
Freetrade Zollverein or Customs union of the Allies.
That’s why every good Australian must distrust
W. M. Hughes and all the other political personages
born elsewhere who do not and cannot by nativity
love this country and therefore understand its ideals
properly like we Australians. A great many
worthy people will object to the claim I now
make that we Australians shall take a bigger share
of the work and duty of managing the public busi-
ness of Australia. Yet the native-born number 82 per
cent, of the people, those born in the United King-
dom 13 per cent, and the foreign-born 5 per. cent.
If we want Protection for ourselves, for our indus-
tries, for our country in full measure, we must
reject the George Reids and William Hugheses, and
all the other exotic politicians who have worked
themselves to the front. We Australians can carry
all elections, can appoint all members of Parliament
and municipal councillors, by overwhelming force
of numbers; and if the present political crowds in
the seven Parliaments, saturated with anti-Australian
ideas, do not give us honest finance and proper Pro-
tection, then we ought simply cast them aside and
elect only Australians who will.
-V —
“C.G.”: For the coming fierce economic
war “efficiency in commerce” is the watch-
word. Now, efficiency can come through
nothing else blit education, and how does
the N. S. Wales education system conduce
to efficiency in matters that are going to
count? The recently-published leaving-cer-
tificate examination results, which cover
over 70 secondary public, denominational
and private schools and colleges, stand out
in illustration. Some 850 candidates sat.
Of these (presumably the cream of the
schools) fourteen took “business principles”
—the most tabooed of the whole of the sub-
jects. Even then only five passed, one alone
being an “A.” Which again gave the lowest
percentage in the whole bundle. Economics
tempted 18 and 14 passed. Yet there were
S5O candidates in all! It may be a possible
consolation that Greek beat either. French
had 632 supporters and Latin 548. Botany
and geology were also well up in the run-
ning. Thus while we are turning out our
Greeks and Romans and rock-scratchers
and botanists the business community
screans for the Efficient Young Man. He’s
reported missing. At any rate, the schools and
colleges haven’t got him. The fact that
the technical colleges and business colleges
cater for the practical young man isn’t
any palliative. These come in too late
for 90 per cent, of the youngsters. Mean-
while the exhibitions and prizes dangled be-
fore the University door are made the goal
of our secondary-school system; and all but
the very few who land there are abandoned.
This writer has had direct personal experience
of the system, and is positive that for
every one useful kid turned out of the
secondary schools a dozen are allowed to
drift. We have excellent educational
machinery, but it wants to be suitably
adjusted and “tuned-up.” It may be dis-
tasteful,_ but we’ll have to follow the Teu-
ton again. . Before the war Germany was
peppered with schools in which a big crowd
of up-to-date commercial subjects had biffed
the traditional “classics.” It was Wilhelm
himself who told a Berlin conference that
the duty of the country was to turn out
young Germans, not Greeks and Romans.
Even England has awakened to similar
advice. In the future we will have to view
education more frequently from the utili-
tarian point of view, with the requirements
and usefulness of the Kid in the foreground.
It’s all live politics. Efficiency is the affair
of the State, and we’ll have to get it or go
out. We haven’t much to spare in this
round, and there’s another one coming.
The story of death and suffering from
Antarctic cold told in connection with the
Shackleton expedition has caused a shud-
dering sympathy throughout the world ; and
in due course, no doubt, honors will be
showered upon the survivors. Yet 1 olar
expeditious are exactly on a par with
the performances of the daring idiot who
handles deadly snakes at the street corner.
Crowds gape at the horror; but any
man in his senses knows, that the
tiling has no scientific value and that
such performances ought to be a crimi-
nal offence. The same is true of the Pole-
hunting folly with its trimmings of death
and misery. Frost bites as badly as a
death-adder; and, on the statement of a
prominent member of the Geographical
Society, the whole Arctic and Antarctic field
of exploration is not of sufficient scientific
value to be worth the sacrifice of a life.
But because Polar adventure provides fine
daily press sensation and brings trade to
their mart, newspaper proprietors can
always be found to support it. Were the
commander of an expedition in which lives
are lost put on his trial for manslaughter
and compelled to show the good he reason-
ably expected to achieve by the performance,
the business might be viewed in proper per-
spective. Hudson, Boss and older voyagers
had plenty of excuse. The theory of an
open Polar'sea was firmly held, and they
might have added to the world’s treasure by
their discovery of ice-free lands fit for
human habitation. Now that dream has
utterly gone. It is time, therefore, that
science itself protested against these garish
showmen’s tricks being practised in its name.

About a lately-dead Maorilander: —
Robert McNab’s political life was not without one
hig accomplishment, or at any rate something that
looked hig when he did it. Like most Maorilanders
Me Nab felt in his bones that to the people there-
abouts the land question was the greatest of all
political issues. Yet he only half grasped the pro-
blem and had been confused by the sham fight about
the leasehold and freehold which raged in the days
of McKenzie and Seddon. When lie joined the first
Ward Ministry as Minister for Lands the time was
dead-ripe for a Land Bill, anil one was produced.
On this McNab said he would stand or fall, Ward
made loud remarks about “nailing his colors to the
mast,” and in the end both of them bolted. The
main object of the Bill was to chop lumps off the
holdings of the big owners, and it frightened six
months’ growth out of them. Tn detail it was
hopeless and as a measure of genuine reform really
worth nothing. But incidental to it was a proposal
to stop further alienation of Crown land by throwing
the whole of the land then leased or unoccupied into
a State Trust for the benefit of education, this to be
known as the National Endowment. The idea was
to remove from the political field the inflamed con-
troversy that had been going on about tenure, and
the Ministry had enough courage left to sec it
through. The effect of this legislation was to sot
aside 7,000,000 acres of Crown land as a permanent
endowment for education and old-age pensions. Pro-
vision was made for throwing another 21000,000 acres
then in occupation on lease into the pool. The
value of this endowment is a matter of opinion. Tha*
it will stand is problematical. Already the principle
of the thing has been nibbled at by an amendment
of the Act four years ago permitting the “exchange"
of endowment land for other land. Recent legisla-
tion by the Masse r-ites has practically killed the
old leasehold controversy, and land problems in
Maoriland will have to be approached from a new
angle. But McNab’s effort to save the remnant of
the public estate had some elements of greatness about
it. He was also the author of the renewable lease
by which an attempt was made to compromise between
the demands for the freehold and the old leasehold
for 999 years.

Last week Tiie Bu.letin urged that a
Commission should he appointed to inquire
into the whole financial administration of
the Defence Department. Even if it did
no other good it would show what individ-
ual Commandants have done to improve
matters here and there. As The Bulletin
said last week, thousands of pounds’ worth
of equipment has been sent to the destruc-
tor instead of to the cleaner. This scandal,
it seems, continued in N. S. Wales until
October or November last, when an organisa-
tion was set up in Sydney to go through
all discarded clothing, boots and bedding,
patch and purify what could be patched
and purified and dispose of the bal-
ance. Under the State Commandant Dr.
Storey, politician David’s lieut.-colonel
son, has had charge of this business; and
as the other States don’t appear to hav«'
anything quite like it, the sooner news of
such improvements is spread the better. The
suggested Commission would supply the
machinery for doing it.
“W.D.Y.” : Here is another of the amazing
performances of the Defence Department.
About three months ago a returned soldier
I know very well received his discharge as
unfit for active service. As he felt no ill-
effects from his six months’ training in
Egypt he went back to his pre-war job as a
farmhand, and was toiling cheerfully when
he received a notification from the Pensions
Board that he had been granted 30s. a week
for six months, and that his case would be
reviewed again in six months’ time. Now he
had never asked for a pension, and did not
honestly think he was entitled to one; in
fact, the idea of applying had never entered
his head. It is a fair thing to grant pen-
sions to returned men if they have sustained
injury ; but there will be a big enough bill
to meet for the war without making money
presentations. The experts who are to over-
haul the Pay Office should be directed to
glance at the system of the Pensions Board.
The Victorian Department of Agriculture
suffers from an unconquerable optimism.
Take bee-keeping, for instance. The only
sensible way to become an apiarist is to
start a cow-farm and get a few hives going
when the cows are bringing in an income.
Once the cows begin to provide his living the
persevering producer get a few pounds
extra out of bees to pay for tobacco and
hair-restorer. The man who starts by try-
ing to make bees keep him is merely a
gambler. Yet the Journal of Agricul-
ture, now being eagerly examined by re-
turned soldiers and the War Council for
traces of suitable occupations, contains
page after page of enthusiastic bee-articles,
telling all about the lovely prospects.
Edward Penglase, who bee-keeps at Fern-
bank (Vic.), published his opinion on the
matter the other day and it is worth past-

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Seen from afar she seemed not terrible;
Seen close, her pallid face and hairless head
Was foul with every foul disease of llell;
Her clothes, a few torn rags of tawdry red,
Veiled not the ghastly whiteness of her
limbs —
The white of marble where pale moonlight
swims.
She spake. Her words had all the tragic
woe
Of all the multitudinous blasted lives,
The myriad unborn babes; each word fell
slow,
As when the speaker with a strange tongue
strives;
Each heavy with a more than human an-
guish,
They seemed to cause the very air to lan-
guish :
“I am the blight that blights the marriage
bed,
Defiling love and blasting all its fruits;
Where Fever, Phthisis, count their thousand
dead,
I count as well my thousand living brutes;
’Tis I, and only I, have power to doom
The unborn infant in its mother’s womb.
“I lull my victims to security
As false as it is vain; maybe a span
Of years may pass, and still he harbors me
Unknown, then loves and marries—foolish
man!
The torments of the blackest Hell are mild
To his when he beholds his stricken child.
“Another score of years and once again
Within the victim’s veins I wake to life,
Distilling my black venom through his brain,
And helping fierce unreason win the strife;
The helpless body broken and defiled
In some asylum joins his idiot child.
“Majestic monuments, asylums, jails.
All testify the measure of my might;
Although I only thrive where knowledge
fails,
And perish where she sheds her cPansing
light,
Dull Ignorance and Cant are my good
friends,
False Piety the best protection lends.”
She showed her victims, drove on drove
they-came,
From old age to the blighted day-old child,
Some blind, some paralysed, some halt,
some lame,
Some mindless, but all loathsomely defiled ;
They passed, all silent, stony with despair,
Save one who mumbled curses mixed wiiii
prayer.

The reports on bee-keeping issued bv the Agricul-
tural Department should be taken with a grain of
salt, as only the bright side is shown. Having kept
iees in I ictoria for 26 years, I can say that it is
? n . e ° f t the most uncertain industries. In this dis-
trict the last five years were total failures.
The War Council should keep its eye firmly
fixed on Strawberry as the most depend-
able rural money-maker in sight. There are
a few successful bee-keepers, very patient
experts mostly; but the successful Straw-
berry-keeper who isn’t much of an expert
IS quite numerous —numerous enough, any-
way, to give a plain lead in the right direc-
tion.

On the Verge of a Fresh Round.
There is a fine lack of detail about the
effects of Germany’s latest frightfulness.
V ilhelm is reported to be satisfied with
results so far, and Britain is alleged to con-
sider that the operations do not seriously
threaten isolation. The fact may be that
both sides are posing, neither being able yet
to tell how the thing is going to end. If the
construction of vessels in British shipyards
is speeded-up and the armed merchantman
can make good shooting practice, it seems at
least reasonable to hope that the German
will have to think out something else. Mean-
while there was no news last week of the
“overt act” which might provoke Uncle Sam
to get properly riled, and a deep suspicion
existed that Sam was going to a lot of
trouble to avoid being riled any more than
he was before. The German is in all like-
lihood just as anxious as the Yank to dodge
overt acts —until he gets a line on what liis
torpedoes can actually do. If he sees that he
cannot succeed in a contract limited to Brit-
ish vessels and the shipping of European
neutrals, there would be nothing gained by
adding the U.S.A. to liis troubles.- If, how-
ever, he comes to the conclusion that commit-
ting an overt act will help him to victory,
Washington can rely on the deed being done.
Victory at the price of offending the U.S.A.
would certainly be cheap to the German, for
he is bleeding now far more than is good
for him, with the horrible certainty of hav-
ing soon to fight as he never fought before.
East and west of him are enormous armies
waiting for General Frost to take a holiday
—armies that are well-gunned, confident and
resolute. Months of a carnage more terrible
than any that has gone before are
ahead ; for if the past affords any clue to the
future it pan be taken as certain that the
German will fight on the conquered land he
bolds until he can fight no more. There are
overwhelming political and military reasons
for this; for the day the German armies
retreat to their own frontiers —if they ever
do—the German nation will know that it is
beaten. But the war is a long way from the
German frontiers yet; and words will not
drive the enemy thither, nor will armies that
are short of reinforcements. All the talk
in the world about “winning the war” won’t
alter that fact.
When the plaint of the clerks was opened
before .Justice Hodges in the Vic. Court
of Industrial Appeals recently, his Honor
opined that equal pay should not be given
for equal work as between the sexes. It
was put to him that a differential wage
meant the replacement of men clerks by
women. “And a very good thing, too.” he
answered. Possibly. It might be a 'good
thing if wages were so modified that women
displaced men as solicitors, barristers and
judges; also as parsons, politicians, drafts-
men, importers and salesmen of many sorts.
The war has. shown that woman can do
a whole lot of things. But nobody has yet.
advanced any sound reason why she should
be encouraged to keep on doing them. The
point is that while women can do a lot of
man’s work man cannot do woman’s. Her
exclusive job is that of wife and mother, and
no system is good, which tends to make it
more difficult for a man to support a house-
hold. so that woman may do her exclusive
job in reasonable comfort.
Eleven of the 52 aliens naturalised in the
Commonwealth during January were Ger-
mans. Four were over GO, and two were
Australian women whose German husbands
have died. So far so good. But the other
five were naturalised as “special cases.”
Why this lack of publicity about the enrol-
ment of enemy subjects as good Australians
these nerilous times? It is fairly up to the
Government to let everybody know who and
what are the enemy aliens now being re-
ceived into the bosom of the Commonwealth
as special cases. The Railway Commis-
sioners in most States can’t even carry a
bit of freight at less than the usual rates
without publishing their reasons; and surely
the admittance of Germans to citizenship
just now is quite as important a departure
from public policy.

Playhouse ~Feb. 17) McMahon’s Benefit
The Taits have made a bright musical-
comedy start at Sydney Palace with “Very
Hood Eddie,” a well-dressed lightweight
American show which is passed over the
footlights by a co. headed by Barry Lupino,
who, as well as leading, has also attended to
the staging. The Taits have provided a
dainty cradle for their first baby by
having the somewhat dingy Palace refur-
bished. Among the improvements there is a
new curtain of stone-colored silk decorated
with trails of hand-painted roses. A big
first-night audience wasn’t disappointed with
the frivolity which the raising of this glad
rag unveiled. “Eddie” has the advantage of a
plot which is only lost sight of occasionally
and never absolutely falls in its tracks. It
is also a rest from the stereotyped form of
musical comedy which stars the dear Duchess
of Whitehall, the young women of title who
marry the Wrong belltoppered lcnuts, the
frivolous elderly gent who wants his nephew
to enter the diplomatic service, and the rest
of the familiar Punch and Judy show. The
American importation doesn’t bring one
titled character in its Matilda. Eddie him-
self is a lisping little Englishman who has
been abducted in holy matrimony by a sort
of she-colonel, and the plot begins to work
when a Hudson River excursion steamer
starts unexpectedly and carries off Eddie and
the baby doll-bride of a tragic American who
has gone ashore with Eddie’s missus to see
after some vagrant luggage. The marooned
pair don’t catch up with Eddie and the baby
doll till the second act. when they find them
living the blameless life in the Rip Van
Winkle Inn, where the comedy clerk is the
whole staff, and says he can’t be sacked
“because the boss committed, a crime and I
was the only witness.” It looks the sort of
show that could be speeded-up if necessary
to a 60-mile-an-hour gait; but it gets along
comfortably like a family motor full of virtu-
ous week-enders.
* * * *
Barry Lupino, in the uame-part, gets his
laughs quietly and never forces the pace.
Lilian Tucker, a singing American beautv-
blonde, has the same agreeable quality of
restfulness. She is the stage beloved of old
friend Andrew lligginson, who used to pursue
“The Merry Widow” as the pettish Prince
Danilo. Andrew has a hint of the mellow-
ness of approaching inidddle-age about him
now. Fayette Perry does the baby doll’s
work prettily. John Beck (another new-
comer) manages a big slice of the second act
as the hotel clerk, a slouching, dictatorial
“THE ORCHID A AT SYDNEY
