Meakin (Phil Smith) to a duel.
country buck in loose garments. Emily Fitz-
roy towers as Eddie’s massive bride. Alfred
Bristowe earns overtime as an excited stage
Frenchman; and Daisy Revette seems to
float on whenever the music becomes brisk.
Jerome Kern, a U.S. composer, is respon-
sible for this music. Several of the airs have
tlie valuable haunting quality, and will pull
music-lovers back for a second hearing. A
good orchestra under Robert Keers gets the
most out of the 17 numbers, and the dress-
ing of the show compels tin- respectful ntton-
tion of fashion-critics. The size of the
Palace stage doesn’t allow much room for
display; but there is room for a compact
company of good-lookers, and Mrs. Scott
(sister to the Tait brotherhood) has person-
ally seen to it that their frocks are a credit
to them and the family. The Clints collect
credit for the scenery, which includes a heavy
second-act set.

being nightly demonstrated at Sydney Cri-
terion by Elma Royton, while the composi-
tion of the morning-after antidote is lucidly

panying lecture, “How to Be a Husband to

Evidently the intrusive moth is slain at

The three-hour film “Intolerance” un-
wound its 13,000 feet of architectural won-
ders, gorgeousness and horrors to a packed-
tight Sydney Royal. The plot is woven of
four threads, each a world-story of the
havoc wrought by man’s intolerance, tem-
pered only by the grace of love. New York.
Paris, Babylon and Jerusalem are the cen-
tres of action. Though the picture is well
worth seeing the agony of three of the
stories is too drawn out; one’s spine be-
comes jelly from the succession of thrills.
But the story of Calvary—that greatest
tragedy in the world’s history—conveys
no sense of reality. Perhaps the murder
of that “Just Man” is beyond the power
of film factory to comprehend and portray.
Smoke is now pouring from the chimney
of the vaudeville factory known as Sydney
Tivoli, and Fred Bluett is officially described
as “The One Comedian that Nobody Ever
Tires of.” If he ever has an argument with
the boss about salary, he should take that
cutting along with him and read it aloud.

the halo bestowed upon him by Melba, and

hayseed. The Togos are falling off the bill

Part-singing having Ivon so successfully
featured by the Dandies during their Syd-
ney season at the Garden Theatre, the co.
that succeeded them the other night, the
Smart Set, caught the wink and followed.
A very pleasing follow-up it was, too. On
the whole, the show is much on the lines
of that of the Dandies and loses nothing
by comparison. Change of programme
occurs weekly, but prices of admission and
the managerial smile of welcome are fix-
tures.
One difference between President Gar-
field, of U.S.A., and showman T. H. Eslick,
of Sydney, is that the former went from
log cabin to White House while the latter
proposes to go from White City to log-
rolling cabin. Eslick is to stand as a Lib-
eral for the Paddington seat in the Assem-
bly at the next elections in N. S. Wales.
Meanwhile the carnival frolics continue to
be a feature at the big amusement park
and the side-shows are at the old address.
In “The Misleading Lady, - ’ at Melbourne
Loyal, two Yankee writers of the telephone
and ‘auto. school of fiction have joined
their limited faculties to provide an improb-
able niglit-out for a very ordinary young
woman. Proceedings open with the usual
preamble wherein the young person, Helen
Steele, instigates a proposal of marriage
from honest John Craigen. It seems that
she has frivolously lured him to offer his
heart and fortune for the sake of a rather
brainless wager; but when she discovers
that John is dead-earnest, she realises that
something special in feminine contrition is
needed to soften the blow. Thereupon she
gives a movie show of Shame and Remorse,
which becomes articulate and expressive of
Shocked Amazement when the affronted hero
grabs her about the waist and hurries her
per motor to a lone place in the mountains.
Act 11. affords a vision of abducted Helen,
sorely in need of a powder-rag and com-
posure. in Mr. Craigen’s Adirondack shack.
The humpy is eminently suitable for the per-
petration of polite masculine brutalities,
wherefore John starts to demonstrate that
his second name is Petruehio, while he tries
to escape a web of farcical melodrama in
which the worried authors are now attempt-
ing to enmesh him. Follows a mad farrago
of incidents recalling the absurdities with-
out the thrills of “The Seven Keys to Bald-
pate.’’ While Craigen is immersed in humili-
ating Miss Steele, only to be howled at.
thumped and finally adored by that way-
ward female, irrelevant motors whiz* out
of the night, telephones buzz, rain and
thunder riot in the background, and an
escaped lunatic, posed as Napoleon, heads
imaginary legions through the scene of
activities. More than once Bonaparte saves
the play from meeting its Waterloo, and
-there is no doubt that he has been specially
created to hold the stage while the dramatist's
are plotting further embarrassment for Helen
and John. The so-called comedy ends in
anti-climax and mental exhaustion, with
Helen and John fastened firmly together and
an inexplicable rival scouting somewhere in
the snow of Little Bourke-street in hopes
of salvaging his missing love.
* * * *

definite object in the piece is the disgruntled
rival, Henry Tracey. Played by Leslie

homicide in the sardine class. Frank Harvey
is no less resolute as .Tack Craigen, and

A revival of “The Merchant of Venice,” at
Melbourne Princess’s, brings Allan Wilkie
forward in one of the most convincing of his
characters. This Jew seems about right in
all main essentials. Miss Hunter-Watts re-
peats her confident impression of Portia,
without suggesting that a heart beats under
either the satin of the lady or the' red robe
of tbe Venetian advocate. Miss Hunter-
Watts in some attitudes is just a piece of
elegant dress material. The Prince of
Morocco, formerly a well-spoken potentate
by Marcus St. John, is another of the halt
and maimed which the inexperienced Fred
Kelioe has to add to his list of dramatic
casualties. Walter Hunt’s Bassanio is
stagily conventional, and Vivian Edwards
(Gratiano) is stronger in voice and cruder
in decorum than the previous impersonator,
Robert Faulkner. If Shylock fails to obtain
his pound of Christian, his daughter, Jessica,
gets it in the puny Lorenzo of David Bel-
bridge. It is impossible to believe that
Jessica’s roving eye could be captured by
this fragment of a lover. John Cosgrove’s
stout Antonio nearly obliterates the fore-
ground as before. Edward Landor is a new
Launcelot Gobbo, with a quaint style of
foolishness that implies more animal cun-
ning than mental obtuseness. Miss Lorna
Forbes, quite unsuited as Jessica, practi-
cally administers the coup de firace to a
character which previously was sadly mauled
by Miss Elwyn Harvey. Miss Marie Ney is
Nerissa; and the only reproach that this
gallant journal can utter against her is that
she looks far too comely and coquettish for
a “waiting woman.”
“The House That Jack Built” at Mel-
bourne Majesty’s looks nothing alongside
the houses the Firm gets. This is the eighth
week of the show, and the theatre continues
to be packed with parents who have
brought their children and children who
have had their parents thrust upon them.
Spectacularly the matinees are the great-
est success. Every one of them is an over-
whelming conglomerate mass of kid, ice-cream
and lolly.
Allen Doone’s Tom Moore at Melbourne
King’s remains the same theatrical myth
that The Bulletin saw last week. The
play, however, is kinder to the Irish min-
strel than to other historical personages
who appear therein. Beau Brummel, for
instance, is represented as a johnny who
mostly suppresses the Beau and seems to
regard Brummel as a variation of Brummy.
Sheridan is even more severely dealt with.
He is depicted as a burly, bovine person
with long arrears of temperance to make
up. Frank Cullenane plays this bibulous
effigy. Next Saturday “The Parish Priest”
will be revived, and on Wednesday (21st)
“In Old Donegal.”
The Passing Show arrived at Melbourne
Tivoli on Saturday, revealing front and
back views of shapely girleens, two or
three scenes of decorative fancy, and the
partially disguised Strollers headed by
Sydney James. Sydney and his colleagues
reappear in nondescript garments in which
the previous Stroller characteristics are not
utterly suppressed, though they have to
struggle hard for notice. Also the little party
has abandoned its Stroller form of humor,
and has become more Amurkan and ragtimey
in conformity with the artistic canons of King
Mclntosh. James is the director of frivol-
ities, and whistles, warbles, or ventriloquises
with almost equal success. Connie Milne,
Madeline Rossiter, G. W. Desmond and
George Graystone all do good work, and
Gwen Lewis has a piano and a scene all
to herself.
Annette Ivellerman continues to be the
sport of cruel Fate at Melbourne Audi-
torium. During a busy evening she is. stolen
by bandits and sold as a slave, then sen-
tenced to become an alligator’s breakfast
and frizzle 1 over a martyr’s lire. After
dodging those modes of extinction, she is
tossed about in troubled waters and deposited
by the waves on a tropical shore, only
to be pelted by gnomes who are appa-
rently upset by her thrilling charms. And
it should be remarked that Annette really is
something to shock unsophisticated gnomes.
She shows herself among them, as she
appears fi’eely elsewhere, with no more
wardrobe than her flowing tresses and a
couple of hankies for winter wear.

Conspicuous on the Dandies’ programme
at St. Kilda (Vic.) last week was a scrap
of concerted frivolity called “The Musical
Stores.” In this item Harry de Robeck
indulged in low-comedy obeisance before a
finical customer (Ida Newton), whilst
other members of the co. figured as the
dukes, duchesses and other members of
the nobility who are found in hordes be-
hind rag-shop counters.
This week Hoyt’s (Melbourne) are pic-
turing Diamond Daisy, a lady burglar who
inserts herself into apparently invulnerable
establishments and performs very prettily
with the jemmy and bit. The title of the
feature is “The Wager,” and Emily Stevens,
an old screen favorite, is condemned to go
wrong as the female crook. Of the other
Hoyt film, “The Darkening Trail,” all The '
Bulletin need say is that it contains Wil-
liam S. Hart and a gun, and-every Hoyt
supporter knows that that means gore.
The well-earned benefit to Gregan
McMahon happens at Melbourne Playhouse
on Saturday night. The bill is Act I. of
“The Pigeon,” followed by the three-act
comedy, “The Two Mr. Wetlierbys.” Gregan
will be seen in both items and reproduce
two of his cleverest bits of portraiture.

Adelaide Tivoli gives a week-end to its
Andersonian dramas, skipping easily from
the naval “Sailor’s Sweetheart” to the mili-
tary “Life’s Revenge.” Cross and Fitz-
herbert are a fine, dashing pair of leading
men, and Blanche McPherson fights a duel
with Ida Gresham to the satisfaction of the
public. .Tack Cannot and his Follies are to
come to this house. The Majestic counters
with a “Follies of Pleasure” team of its
own. Its turns are strong on the athletic
side, with Dubois of the swift tennis-racquet
topping the bill. The Steele-Paynes’ bell-
ringing almost ranks as an athletic feat too.
The Royal has gone back to pictures—
William Farnum. West’s produce H. B.
Warner per film; Wondergraph has Valen-
tine Grant (and dog) : and at the Pav. C.
Chaplin is a clever fool on skates.
The reporter had been sent to interview
the latest imported star actress, and was
duly impressed by the announcement that
she had brought with her 11 trunks of
dresses. “If I remember rightly, Ethel Irv-
ing brought only five,” he remarked. “Oh,
yes,” was the reply, delivered with cold
scorn, “but she relied upon her acting!”
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Fax”: Melbourne Herald stated, among
other misrecollections of his career, that the
late Henry Bracy “discovered” Florence
Young when he recommended her for the
character of Casilda in the first Melbourne
performance of “The Gondoliers,” which, by
the way, was its first Australian production.
The fact is Florence got her chance months
previously at Melbourne Opera House, where
Musgrove was running the Nellie Stewart
Opera Co. under the nominal management of
the MacMalions. She figured on the “Boc-
caccio” hill as “Miss Young (first appear-
ance).” On “The Gondoliers” hill at the
Princess’s she was “Miss Florence Young
(her first appearance at this theatre).”
“M.P.A.”: Annette Kellennan, the Aus-
tralian mermaid now topping the bill in “A
Daughter of the Gods,” owes her position to
two men. First, her late father, a tireless old
battler, despite the heart trouble which
eventually took him off. Papa kept her nose to
the water, so to speak, in the exhibition swim-
ming and diving business. Annette’s turn
was really a trick diving show in a canvas
tank let into the ground. Hot or cold, the
mermaid had to face the music. It was at
Princes Court, Melbourne, playing on a per-
centage basis, and at the Bendigo Easter
Fair, that old-man Kellennan got a lift that
enabled him to take his girl to London.
Prior to her departure for the Big Smoke
Annette, then a brown-faced girl in a red
tam-o’-shanter, with a weakness for chewing-
gum, swam five miles down the Yarra, fol-
lowed by a boatload of pressmen, visiting-
swimmers (Kieran, Wickham, Springfield
and others) and a few photographers. After
this big swim Annette got a fine boost in the
dailies. Then the second man to help took
a hand. This was ,T. A. Sears, the well-
known Melbourne outdoor camera man, who

“Monkbarns”: In one of the interesting
pars, on Bracy (B. 8/2/T7) it is said he
was here in the Julia Mathews time; but
Julia had left Australia a good many years
before Bracy came. Her era at the old wooden
matchbox Princess’s, with Fawcett Rowe,
was between ’6O and ’62. She was a dumpy
little woman, with soft, _ putty face, and
singing was her strong point. She married
one Mumford, of Maorihuid. Her stately
father and her lynx-like mother had been
desperately keen janitors. Rowe _ also left
Austral’a about ’62. In America long
afterwnuls he wrote “Fun on the Bristol,”
for Sheridan; but before that ho had
done well, as a star, in England. His
Micawber was reckoned the best ever seen
in London. Sam Emery, afterwards known
in Australia, and Winifred’s dad, was also
grand as Peggotty. So was J. L. Irving,
Ethel’s father, as Heep. Titheradge had racy
anecdotes about Rowe’s cheek in persisting
to act characters like Macbeth and Richard
111. Titli. was Macduff, while the whole
text of Macbeth was ladled out to Rowe by
prompters on both sides.
“Peter” : The advent of the Taits in the
theatrical business means increased employ-
ment for some worthy Australian mummers.
The brethren are not going to let Australian
talent lie idle, and have already made a good
beginning by signing on Lizette Parkes,
Eileen Sparks and Nancye Stewart for their
“Turn to the Right” show, which is to be
staged at Melbourne King’s on February 24.
Lizette will be the nice little heroine, and
the other two will be accommodated in minor
parts. The Taits are only importing one
lady—a specialist in wayback motherliness
for the role of old Mrs. Bascom.

Died in France, William TO. Pike, lieut. of
Royal Engineers, aged 24. Young Pike, who
came from Strathfield (N.S.W.), was a
product of the N. S. Wales State-school
system, and won a scholarship in engineer-
ing when he entered Sydney Uni in 1910.
lie got first-class honors through his course,
and climbed to the B.E. degree in 1914 with
the Uni. medal as the most distinguished
student of his year. A research scholarship
followed, and a travelling fellowship; but
meanwhile the Devil had broken into Bel-
gium, and Pike got the Uni. Senate to post-
pone his fellowship and nominate him to a
commission with the Engineers instead.
Of the eighteen Senators who go out for
election in April or May—three from each
State —eight are Labor men, four Liberals
and six (W.A. and S.A.) followers of
Hughes. If seven Laborites go back their
side will have a majority, for there are 11
Labor Senators with three years still to go.
The chances of a Phewsion getting a Senate
majority through the election are thus not
cheerful, and they are not improved by the
prevalent idea that a win for Hughes and
Cook would be at once employed to disfran-
chise the people by prolonging the life of
the Reps, for a year or two.
S. Aus. State Bank, which was the pio-
neer of its kind in this continent, has cele-
brated its 21st birthday by a luncheon.
Fred Holder, the State Treasurer, after-
wards Commonwealth Speaker, who financed
it in its infancy, is long dead ; and 11. M.
Addison, the first chairman, is the only sur-
vivor of the original Board. Catt and
Gordon (now a judge), who piloted the
scheme through Parliament in 1895, are still
on deck; and G. S. Wright, the bank’s
inspector-general, has held that post ever
since it started. 11. D. Gell, a local man
who mixes accountancy with the best teach-
ings of the young men’s class in the Sunday-
school, is chairman to-day. He came in
because it promised to supply young people
with money to build homes at 4 per cent. —
and for a good while did it.
The Ist Light Horse Depot (28 Moore-
street, Sydney) has a scheme. It has made
arrangements to buy biscuits, fruit and
vegetables (tinned, of course), sardines,
cigarettes, tobacco, etc., at rock-bottom
wholesale prices; and it is forwarding
7s. Gd. and 15s. Easter parcels to any man
in any unit on active service. Send the
7s. Gd. or the 15s. and Billjim’s address, and
the Ist Light Horse Comforts Committee
will do the rest.
THE PENSIVE ADVANCE AGENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Although exposed for weeks to rain,
And sun and wind and vandal hand,
Your message still is fairly plain,
Old daybill on a wayback stand l
My paste and bills I do not heed,
Rut, meditative, pause and read.
De Vere’s All-Star Dramatic Co.
It was your lot to advertise,
And let this sleepy township know
Here was a show to patronise.
And thus you estimate its worth:
“The Greatest Company on Earth.’’
“East Lynne,” of course; and you proclaim
Your leading lady, Dora Dell
(I do not recollect the name),
“The World's best Lady Isabel.”
Your Archibald Carlyle, Guy Fane,
Came here “Direct from Drury Lane.”
Yet shoddy ’twas in ev’ry sense,
This venture of the bold De Yere,
“Produced regardless of expense.”
The clothes were dim with age, 1 hear.
Although you state, with shameless swank,
“New Scenery by Splodger Blank.”
De Vere and Co. have come and gone,
(The 2 a.m., I’m told, they caught),
But bleached and torn and frowned upon,
You still, can stir a faded thought.
In pity I will hide from view
Old lies, grown commonplace, with new.
N.S.W.

Thank the good Lord all the brains of the
Defence Department have not been sent to
the Front. There are still some for home
consumption. A sample turned up in Syd-
ney last week when a hefty lad eager to do
his bit was prosecuted for making a false
declaration of his age. lie had lined up
for his rifle and kit and bluffed it out that
he was 18, whereas the scoundrel was then
only 16 years 9 months! The outraged chief
enrolling officer, as soon as he discovered
the crime, ran the young blackguard to the
police court, where a reluctant magistrate
fined him 50s. If he hasn’t got 50s. he is

You never know how it is going to hit
you. A month ago an elderly Sydney draper
took off his boots and waded into Leich-
hardt Bay to gather cockles. He came out
of the water with a blister on the ball of his
left foot, and remarked to his wife that he
must have scratched it on a cockle-
shell. Next day his foot was sore; but he
was able to go to work. lie sold his calico
steadily for a fortnight, never guessing that
time was nearly up ; then one morning he
complained of sore throat, stiffness of the
jaws and a pain in his stomach. The doctor
hurried him to the hospital with tetanus,
and lie took a whole month to die of it. The
only safe man with a wound is the soldier
who lies on his stretcher in France with his
forehead decorated with the indelible pencil-
mark which .signifies that anti-tetanus serum
has been injected.
That mark on the forehead recalls a queer
thing you see in Java. Especially in the
days when malaria is most ferocious, mothers
streak their babies’ foreheads with the sign
of the Cross, using a mixture of rice-flour
and gin. If the baby isn't safe then it never
will be, for the Cross is the natives’ idea of
white-man’s magic, rice is the greatest thing
in the world (the native is personally sure
on that point), and gin is the wonderful
drink distilled by the Dutchman, who, being
the native’s ruler, stands for civilisation. So
the wliitey streak on the little brown face
stands for magic, life and all earthly power
and knowledge—and a mother can do no
more for her infant than invoke that Trinity.
“O’Pliimerty” : The union-of-churches idea
got an advt. the other day in Tassy on the
spot where an R.C. and an Anglican ceme-
tery are side by side, with a stone wall
between them. An old man and his wife
who belonged one each to these churches
desired to be united in their death. They
therefore bought two grave allotments, one
on each side of the wall, and the graves were
dug obliquely so that they met underneath.
Now the two coffins rest together, and every-
body is content.

“Itedivi”: I suppose they are stricter in
France with regard to non-regimental embel-
lishments, but on Gallipoli it was a
habit with the boys to adorn the bands of
their slouch hats with the name of the town
they came from. Some had just one name
showing, some two or more, but I saw one
bloke with: “CAIRNS. COONAMBLE.
BATHURST, WOl )ONGA, ECIIUCA.”
This roused the spirit of sarcasm in his
cobber, who came to light next day with:
“THE BLANKY BUSH.”

STAGE-MANAGER: “But why he so cut up over the loss of that little shopping-hag?”
THE ACTRESS: “ ‘Shopping-hag’ he hi owed! I had six changes of costume in that bag!”
“Longy”: If Billjim has caught special
attentions in England it isn’t because he
has chased them. Here is a typical instance.
At an English hospital where I was a cus-
tomer there were about a dozen Australians
among 120 patients. King George and his
Mary came to see us one day, and spoke to
every man, wisely making, no distinction
between Tommies and Australians. But on
the quiet he spread himself, telling the
Anzacs what he thought them. While the
Queen was talking to three Australian ser-
geants the matron (an Englishwoman)
beckoned to two of us who were seated with
three Tommies out on the lawn to come over
to the Queen. The Tommies of course read
the signal, too. My mate Bill swore a bit
and did not heed the beckon. When the
matron gave us the office again, by holding
up two fingers and then waving, Bill swore
a. bit more, and we stuck there with the
three Englishmen. The Queen, “dropping”
to it, came over and yarned to the five of
us. When the matron spoke of it later Bill
told the lady that we reckoned no difference
should be made between the Tommies and
ourselves. She let it go at that.

“Duanrats”: The hard case, on leave
from Broadmeadows, arrived at Memourne
station without a ticket. Bustling through
at the barrier he thrust into the hand of
the collector a cigarette card. The aston-
ished man exclaimed in an injured voice:
“Here, that’s no good to me.” “Well, tear
the damned thing up,” cheerfully replied
the hopeful of the army as he melted into
the traffic.
* *• * *
The British Government is making an ex-
periment. It is withholding the ration
ticket, and putting the people on their honor
to eat no more per week than 41b. of bread,
2ilb. of meat and Jib. of sugar. This may
turn out all right. But the chances are that
it will not, and that John Bull will have to
line up at national refreshment-rooms. One
Australian training-camp has provided a
curious illustration of what is likely to
happen meanwhile. As a piece of economy
the men from various small camps have been
drafted to the big camp, and instead of the
food being sent out for division among a
few, it has to be scrambled for by as many
as 40 or 50 men. The consequence is that
the early-comers, knowing they will probably
be short to-morrow, as they may have been
the day before, take an over-generous help-
ing, and the late-comers must then go short.
It is a wasteful system; and the failure of
it in Australia on a small scale is prettv
sure to indicate what the result will be
when the same plan of help-yourself-but-
don t-take-too-much is adopted on a national
scale in England.
“The Tiger” : Last year the Government
was so badly treated oyer the wheat-buying
scheme that it has been found - necessary to
issue a regulation that no grain will be
received by the pool unless the bags are
stamped with the registered brand of the
owner. Thousands of bags of wheat were
received last season without the slightest
examination, and carelessness on the part
of the agents developed in Hayseed a sur-
prising originality. Bags filled with sand
were an item in the programme; stones also
found their way to the stacks; and second
grades were passed off as first. But the
Brigand Chief was discovered at Wyalong
(N.S.W.). He carted his wheat to the siding
in a two-horse dray. On his way thither
a fine horse, weighing nearly a ton, was
always between the shafts, and a quad, a
few hundredweights lighter in the lead. On
the weighbridge everything was weighed ex-
cept the leading horse. After taking the
wheat off the farmer would unyoke his
horses and feed them, and later, in order to
arrive at the net weight of the load, the
dray was scaled before the homeward
journey was commenced. Then, however,
the smaller horse was always between the
shafts. The robbery was not noticed till
the culprit became over-talkative in a con-
vivial moment.

THE BANQUET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I gowned myself with anxious haste
The joys of Pleasure’s feast to taste.
Swiftly my napkin I unwound,
And nibbled at the roll I found.
The service runs with ease and state—
llefore me still an empty plate.
The many courses come and go,
Nor taste of any do I know.
And still to taunt my thirsting eyes
The wines in near-by glasses rise.
So far am I beloyv the salt,
No slave will by my settle halt;
Soon will the hostess catch our eyes
And from our places all must rise ;
Starving, to dry old age I’ll creep
Mv longing eyes ton tired to weep.
•loyt will you spare me not one crumb,
Is this of Pleasure’s feast the sum—
A napkin round a slim French roll,
An olive and a finger bowl?

IN CAT SOCIETY.
“I shudder when I think of my thirtieth
birthday.”
“Why? Whatever happened?”
Thus Melbourne lawyer Marshall Lyle on
lawyers:—
As regards the intellectual powers of lawyers their
principal defect has been stated by one of them,
Lord Tenterden. “Those who had been bred to the
law,” he said, “were rendered dull, by habit, to
many of its defects.” Charles Dickens explained
this dullness of lawyers (and especially of judges) :
it is due to the tendency of men to contract a
liking for those things which they have .studied at
great cost of time and intellect. This tendency
might be corrected by studying other things, but it
does not seem that this succeeds in Australia, or any-
where else. Except such lawyers as Bacon, - More,
Blackst'one and Romilly, the profession has always
been remarkable for its unintellectuality as regards
law reform, and the profession is much to blame for
bamboozling the stupid people who look to lawyers
as leaders in law reform. No profession has said so
many silly things against reform. It was Ellenborough
who said that England would be ruined if capital
punishment were abolished for stealing ss. worth
of wet linen from a bleaching-ground. The Lord
Chancellor in 1813 prophesied that every poor man’s
house in England would be broken into if the death
penalty were abolished for stealing ss. worth from
a shop. Lord Eldon, in tears, shuddered in the
House of Lords at the thought of what would happen
if the death penalty were abolished for forgery; and
Lyndhurst and Wynford agreed with him. And if I
were to quote some of the very stupid things that
Victorian and N. S. Wales judges and lawyers have

UN[?]IE (who has just given s...all nephew a new knife): “I suppose you’ll carve your
name on the furniture with it?”
SMALL NEPHEW: “No Gar! I’m going to keep this one for best.”
said during the last 30 years I .would be only show-
ing that, no matter whether in Old World or New,
the same defect is apparent, viz., dullness produced
by too much legal study, i may add that the late
Lord Russell of Killowen once warned me not to
study the law too much. “Don’t study too much ;
know your facts and the human .beings you arc
dealing with.” This was his advice, often repeated,
and he was right. He was brilliant, because he
had the advantage of not being clogged and
oppressed with legal lore. Some day someone will
rise and demand the suppression of all law reports.
But meanwhile lawyers will continue to worship
them. Just think of the silly things judges say in
Australia 1 And just look at the rubbish in their
libraries 1
“Red Tape” loosens up:—
When war broke out a respectable number of
N. S. Wales public servants enlisted, and some of
the departments were denuded of eligible single
men. Most of the Service, however, is married and
a lot of it is old. But whatever they were they
all were loud in their talk of helping the Empire.
In a burst of patriotism it was decided that incre-
ments in the higher ranks should be suspended,
increases to top-storey salaries should stop alto-
gether, that all hands should work until 5 p.m.
instead of knocking off at 4.30, and that annual
leave should be given up. The patriotic mood
lasted three months. Then a bombardment of
medical certificates broke down the annual leave
barrier. Next a section clamored successfully for
their increments, and, naturally, the usual crop of
increases to the big men joined the procession.
Now the extra half-hour of duty is to disappear
on April 1, to re-appear (it is said) on November
1. To-day the Public Service is operating under
pre-war conditions, the departments pursue their
old unruffled way, and but for an occasional “Roll
of Honor” there is nothing to show that war is on.
The only sound that breaks the summer’s peace is
the pursuit of the skirts of influence up the back
stairs and the wail of “Backsheesh!” from some poor
soul in its agony.
The suspicion that the average State-
school teacher is a discontented toiler was
confirmed last week. The N. S. Wales Edu-
cation Department required six truant-
inspectors, and restricted applications to the
service. The Minister was shocked to learn
that 90 per cent, of the male teaching staff
had applied. The jobs carried £2OO per
annum, increasing by £25 annually to £3OO.
Apparently all teachers receiving less than
£2OO applied, but there were also many appli-
cants from teachers in the higher grades. As
the Department is admittedly short of male
teachers, and the duties of the truant-chaser
only require long legs and an eagle eye, why
not go outside the service? The supply of
returned soldier is not yet exhausted.

“Homagen”: For cheerfulness in a big
scrap 1 the Australians will do me. In August
last at Pozieres we were holding the
front line in our turn.
This ' trench had been
made in a hurry, and was
only 30in. wide. There
was a man every three
or four yards, lounging
about to be as comfortable
as possible, his rifle _ some-
where near him (if it was
not lost in the mud), his
pockets bulging with
bombs, and if he wasn’t
smoking a wet cigarette he
was trying to borrow one.
By day and night a run-
ning fire of conversation
went backwards and for-
wards from man to man
for about a mile. To let
a despatch-runner or
stretcher-bearer pass, each
man in turn would lie
down and let the traveller
walk over him, then get
up, scrape, himself and call
himself Sir Walter
Raleigh. There was a foot
of mud and slush at the
bottom of the trench, yet
while the chaps were in it
I never heard it alluded to
as anything but a good
subject to joke about.
That’s the Australian way.
They’re born grouchers
whenever grouching
doesn’t really matter. But
when the job’s on, and
grouching would matter,
they forget everything in the delight of
action and responsibility.
* * * *
“8.1. D.”: My friend Brother George
(known after the flesh as George Cook)
enters a mild protest against the par. (B.
3/2/T7) about the Ingathering of the Israel-
ites of the House of David. George says
that the real difference between the Chris-
tian Israelites looked after by Shadrach
Hertel and his kind isn’t a matter of hair:
that is only an outward and visible sign.
The Christian Israelites, he says, are under
the law of Moses, and the David Israelites
have come out of the bondage of the law into
the light of the Gospel. Nor does he agree
that Australians are not admitted to mem-
bership of the House of David till all their
dross is paid over. There are two kinds of
Israelites. Those who feel the call to
separate themselves from the world go to
the sects’ headquarters at Benton Harbor.
Michigan, U.S.A., an establishment presided
over by Benjamin and Mary, and live the
community life. If they have any filthy
lucre (and some of them have had ,a fair
wallet) it has to be turned over to the com-
munity. But those who are not called go
on living in the world. Brother George has
been in the House of David for 12 years, but
he has never been to the U.S.A. and doesn t
expect to go. In the community, he says,
they neither marry nor are - given in
marriage, and those who are man and wife
live as if they were not. As George says,
this is the more perfect way, but is not for
everyone. Still, if it is left to the uncalled
to do all the multiplying and replenishing,
those who live the perfect life will never be
any fewer than they are, because they
achieve immortality here on earth. If a
member dies the explanation is easy; he has
gone astray somehow. Not that that matters
as much as you might think, for there is no
Hell.

“Copabella” : The best thing at the A.N.A.
Exhibition in Melbourne was the remark of
a Footscray woman. She had just rescued
her five-year-old child from under the feet of
a rushing, struggling mob of women fighting
to get to the official feed at about 5 o’clock
on Anniversary Day. “Heavens!” she said
to an attendant. “Whoever are they?” “The
ladies,” he replied, “going to the luncheon.
Mr. Hughes is to speak.” “Don’t ladies get
enough to eat when they’re at home?”
queried the woman from Footscray. “They
do seem hungry.” The poor woman wasn’t
used to it. There aren’t any “ladies” in
Footscray.

“F.C.”: That Hun criminal in a recent
Bulletin who objected to fight because it
wasn’t in his sentence, finds a mate in one
of Victoria’s indeterminate eonfinces who
had been promoted to the comparative free-
dom of the tree-planting gang at French
Island. After a brief trial of healthy,
pleasant work he demanded to be returned
to Pentridge. Living in a camp, he said, was
not in his sentence: he wanted the simple
life where he was well looked after and had
everything done for him.

IN OLD MAN’S LAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Do you still remember, Sweetness, do you care still
to remember,
That sweet night of early summer, just about a
week ago,
When we sat upon your window-sill and watched the
garden grow ?
With exceeding scented fleetness —ah, the warm, kind
mid-November!—
The golden moments galloped towards the holy hour
of parting. . . .
And still my shins are smarting with what happened
when I went.
Happiness so soon is spent,
And I’m wearing, for remembrance, every sort of
bump and dent.
While your Pa reclined serenely, his remote cigar-
tip glowing,
Safely on the far verandah, nodding early off to
sleep,
We—we held each other’s hands, and, oh, the per-
fume wide and deep
Of the springing catmint greenly underneath your
casement growing!....
Down among the fragrant bashes wriggled Little
Brother, spying,
With extreme disfavor eyeing saucy Sister where
she sat.
Pa would shortly settle that
When good Willie woke him up to tell what Miss
Pauline was at.
No more then did Father “slumber, snoring rudely
there, staccato ;
Loudly snorting, up he started —started up in
wrath, alas!
And I went, like summer lightning, flashing through
the garden-glass.
Mashing up the young cucumber and the tender,
green tomato,
I fled flashing, smashing, crashing, with the mad old
bloke pursuing.
What the blazes was I doing in his garden, like t’
know.
Though our parting was a blow,
Pa’s language was so dreadful it seemed best for me
to go.
Yes, I went without delaying—your fatheaded father
willed it —
Right across the dewy evening, crushing greens
beneath my boots
And retreating at the double as I heard his crazy
hoots.
Oh, good Lord! What he was saying! Down the
lovely night he shrilled it
Like a barmy-brained old blighter.... I was sorry
for you, darling,
When I heaid that sudden snarling, but of course I
had to clear
When your dear old Dad drew near,
With an uproar like an army closing in upon my
rear.
VICTORIA.
O. L.

HOW TO STOP PAIN IN STOMACH.
THE VALUE OF MAGNESIA AND
HOT WATER.
Generations of people have employed hot
water as a soother of pain in the stopiacli,
but modern physicians and specialists have
discovered and used successfully an addi-
tion which renders the hot water trebly
efficacious. Hot water is still recommended
in all cases of stomach pain, from whatever
cause arising, hut still better results are
obtained if a little bisurated magnesia is
added. Hot water attracts blood to the
painful part and the bisurated magnesia
neutralises the acid, which is usually the
underlying cause of the pain. That is why
such excellent results follow combination of
the two. Readers who are subject to pain
in .the stomach, especially after eating,
should try this hot water and magnesia
recipe. It renders dieting quite unneces-
sary, for by slowly drinking half a glassful
of hot water containing half a tea-
spoonful of bisurated magnesia immediately
after meats, the dangerous stomach acids
are destroyed, and food remains bland and
sweet until perfectly digested. The bisu-
rated magnesia, which is extensively used
in hospitals, can readily be obtained from
chemists everywhere; but to avoid confu-
sion with the acetates, citrates and oxides
of magnesia or equally unsuitable, mixtuies
of bismuth and magnesia, it is important
that bisurated should be asked for a ery
distinct! y.—Advt.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Re the use of the great Australian slan-
guage by our German friends in the trenches.
Will Dyson writes of an Anzac who took
three German prisoners on his own. and
who explains: “My idea in gettin’ at them
was to go right through ’em with the
bayonet; but the first one put fight light out
of my mind. ‘I surrender,’ he said. ‘Fair
do, old bloke.’ ”
REJECTED.
THE DRUNK: “’Strut! (hie), you reject
me (hie), do yer!!—Orlright (hie),
reject me, an’ you’ll blanky well lose
the (hie) war.”
Last week Basil Watson flew from Warr-
nambool to Hamilton (Vic.), 614 miles by
the line he took, in 29 minutes. Basil is
home-grown—one of the Bendigo Watsons —
and so is his aeroplane. lie made it himself
of all-Australian materials.
One State Governor has told his friends
that lie will resign if called upon to sign a
death-warrant. The genial Carmichael while
in Victoria lived in dread of having to sign
away a life; and Ilopetoun, after completing
his first document condemning a man to the
rope, fainted dead away, and didn’t revive
until he had given everybody around a great
scare. Lieutenant-Govs., being mostly legal
gentlemen, are all contemptuous of this
undignified weakness.
Captain George Rankin and Lieut. Nor-
man Itae, of the Rochester (Vic.) irrigation
• country, have been promoted Lieut.-Colonel
and Major, respectively, in Egypt. In the
days of peace both were eoc-kies with a
fondness for horses and monthly Light
Horse parades. They enlisted at the out-
break of trouble, but were soon sporting
stars again. Norman was the “boy” presi-
dent of the -,ochester A. and P. Society
that year, and Rankin was looking forward
to a hot time harvesting—though not in the
Fields of Death.

WEBSTER,
Postmaster-
General.
Melbourne Paunch intended to say:
"William Hughes is to-day the most active
force in Australian politics.” But a comp,
who knew better made “farce” of it.
Dick Sleath, whom old-man Parkes fav-
ored among all the early N.S.W. Labor
members, resurrected himself at the first
meeting of the “National” party in Syd-
ney last week. Time hasn’t mellowed the
old strike-leader and “conspirator,” and he
was only heard protesting against the
wretched dozen or two present presuming
to elect a permanent committee to run the
concern.
Judge Rutledge, of Queensland, who died
last week, was one of three brothers who all
went into Methodist pulpits and all but one
dropped out again. The father came this
way nearly 80 years ago and settled at
Castlereagb. near Penrith. He belonged to
a sect known in England as “The Shakers.”
The eldest son, Arthur, left the ministry,
studied law, practised in Queensland, and in
his old age was made a judge. Another left
the Methodies and died an Anglican. The
third, Woolls, after running a small paper
at Grafton, joined the Methody brotherhood
and stayed there.
Two Australians who wear Anglican
gaiters in foreign parts are Arthur M. and
11. J. C. Knight, sons of that “Parson
Knight” of whom old hands on the N. S.
Wales South Coast still tell yarns. Back in the
’so’s and ’6o’s he circulated the only Angli-
can collection-plate in that part, with head-
quarters at Bega, where the two bishops
were born. Tlreir mother is buried in the
local cemetery. Dad went to the Cold
Country after her death, taking the boys
with him. Both got “double firsts” at Cam-
bridge. The younger son, Arthur, bished at
Rangoon (Burma) from 1903 to 1912, but
is now head of St. Augustine's College for
training missionary priests at Canterbury
(Eng.). His elder brother was Dean of
Corpus Christi, Cambridge, till 1911, when
he was made a sort of episcopal ganger to
keep the Anglican chaplains in southern
Europe up to the .collar. Another Aus-
tralian, 11. Iv. Archdall, now Dean of New-
castle, succeeded him in his Cambridge job.
As Anglicanism claims to be part of the
Church, it doesn’t appoint bishops
in R.C. countries or set up churches there
except “chaplaincies” for the convenience of
English residents and tourists. Knight is
consequently known as “Bishop of Gibral-
tar,” that being a British possession which
doesn’t happen to have an R.C. prelate on
the premises.
E. J. Watt, pastoralist and racing man,
who has been held by a Maoriland magistrate
to be exempt from military service there on
the ground that his “home is in Australia.”
owes everything to the other country, any-
how. and still has considerable interests over
,\under. Watt was born in Maoriland, in-
herited a valuable slice of it. and is one of a
family that never bothers about an over-
draft. His father was a merchant-squatter,
from whom young Watt inherited the valu-
able Longlands estate, Hawkes Bay, most
of which was subsequently sold on sub-
division. But he took more interest in
racing than anything else outside his bank
account, is best known in Australia as the
owner of Mountain Knight, and has had one
string of horses in the Commonwealth and
another in Maoriland. One of Watt's sisters
is the wife of George Lowry, proprietor of
the famous Desert Gold. Another married a
Walters, of the London Times clan.
Maoriland got a bargain when John
Harper, just dead at the age of 90. landed
with the Canterbury Pilgrims from their
four stout ships. The Harper family first
lived in a cave at Heathcote, then in a sod
hut. John ran a coach and tilled the soil,
and the family multiplied and flourished.
As a legacy to the country that had treated
him well the old man left six sons, four
daughters, 60 grandchildren, 72 great-grand-
children and two great-great-grandchildren.
Died at Sydney, aged 72, Mark Breach,
ancient mariner. Breach came first to Aus-
tralia in 1860 as master of the barque Jeru-
salem. and for many years traded between
London and Sydney in charge of clippers
under the Aberdeen White Star flag, his
last command being the Patriarch. When
he left the sea he hung out his sign as a
marine surveyor.
Captain W. W. Dove, who at 22 has won
the Military Cross in Flanders, is a nephew
of now-deceased Norton Dove, one time S.M.
at Broken Hill. Young Dove went to Samoa
with the Maoriland expeditionary force in
the first month of the war, and after helping
to kick the Huns out of the group moved
on to Egypt in time to see the curtain rung
down on the Gallipoli tragedy. His M.C.
came to him for gallantry at Flers.
A send-off from the big timber: —
Re Reg Lindsay, just dead in France, and the
family gift. I came across Reg when he was assist-
ant-ranger in the Bet Bet forest country. He
camped at the township pub and kept its boarders
amused with lightning sketches of local characters.
One day there was a timber-getters’ row in the pub
bar and a man fell through one of the highly-omate
futurist windows. To replace the glass was easy
enough—the store stocked everything—but the
harassed publican was in despair at the thought of
having'to get an artist out from Bendigo to restore
the coloring. “The new forester’s a bit ’v a drorer,”
suggested one of the bar council, and the “drorer"’
was duly approached. Reg agreed, without a smile,
and in a few days the new pane flamed out 'with a
bold black BAR, lined with signal red on a pale
greenery-yallery ground, own brother to the existing
window. In addition to memories of a good com-
panion one Backblocks township at least will have
a tangible memorial of Reg Lindsay.

HARRY
JULIUS,
caricaturist,
shortly leaving
for U.S.A.
A returned soldier named Sam Brown
got up to speak a piece at a meeting at
Walcha (N.S.W.). He said that he had
10 brothers at the Front, and that his father,
aged 58. was also fighting. Ilis father-in-law
had died in his boots for all those present.
What did the meeting think of that record?
The meeting showed him. It gave him three
hearty cheers. But it didn’t give him half
a recruit.
The efficient woman doesn’t seem to find
the struggle so hard as it used to be. Miss
Elizabeth McNeill, who has been town
clerk, valuer and collector to the borough
of Ararat (A ic.) for some time, has just
been given the billet of secretary to Stawell
shire. The position was previously held
by civil-engineer Angus Clarke. Now
Stawell is to have an engineer at an
engineer’s wage, and the secretary's job is to
be a distinct one.

From Cranbourne (Vic.), to which quiet
little town the news of the murder of Edith

Awarded D.S.O. last month for work in
Egypt, Major Andrew J. Dwyer, of the
sth Pioneer Battalion. He got his earliest
military training in the State School Cadets
at Carlton, and was a bugler in the first.
Battalion of the Victorian Rifles. He
bugled later for the 3rd Victorian Bushmen in
S’Africa, where lie won the Queen’s Medal
with five bars. After that he had many years
as an assistant at the Fairfield State School
where he trained the cadet team that won
the Sargood Challenge Shield Competition.

Commons, free of election exes. And once

A notable Maoriland guide and explorer
has dropped out of the team, and will take
things easy for the rest of his years. No
more will Donald Sutherland ferry tourists
across Milford Sound to rest, under his hos-
pitable roof. The Government has planned
a hostel to stand alongside the old accommo-
dation-lmt. so the awed stranger will still be
able to hear the story of the taniwha that
Sutherland saw and see the prooTs lie
possesses that the Maori devil is no myth.
Charles Broughton Boydell, the just-
retired Clerk to the Australian Senate, is a
grandson of William Grant Broughton, who
in 1836 was the first-appointed Bishop in
Australia. 11 is spiritual jurisdiction covered
the continent and Maoriland. Eldest-daugh-
ter Mary Phoebe married William Boydell,
a settler on the land at Paterson. Their
son, Charles Broughton, worked in the N. S.
Wales State Assembly from 1873 till April.
1901. when he was appointed Clerk-assistant
to the House of Reps., and from there
passed to the clerkship of the Chamber that
has Prime Minister Hughes stung to death.
Another prominent N. S. Wales unionist,
F. T. Turner, general secretary of the
Enginedrivers and Firemen's, is getting into
khaki. Turner believes that the army must
h.ave men even though they be husbands and
fathers. A wife and five children will pray
for his safe return. But lie was always a
fighter. When H. A. Mitchell battled to
organise a scattered body of men with a
very small idea of the value of organisation,
the Enginedrivers and Firemen's union was
practically Mitchell and his adjutant Turner.
By .getting awards and agreements—many
obtained by strong bluff —the pair tremen-
dously improved conditions for the men
around the fires and made the struggling
organisation a success. Then, when their
reward was about due, they found the men
they had battled for breathing suspicions
about the “good thing” they had on with
the Ass. Mitchell, wearied, got out and
in came industrial advocate for the big
Broken Hill Prop. Turner, who took his
place, is now off to a fairer fight.
THE [?]VABLE CHARACTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
i long for the streets, but the Lord knoweth best,
For there I am never a saint;
There are lovable characters out in the West,
With humor heroic and quaint;
And, be it Up Country, or be it Out Back,
When I shall have gone to my Home,
I trust to be buried ’twi.vt Uiver and Track
Where my lovable characters roam.
There are lovable characters drag through the scrub.
Where the Optimist ever prevails ;
There are lovable characters hang round the pub,
There are lovable jokers at sales
Where the auctioneer’s one of the lovable wags
(Maybe from his ‘“order” estranged),
And the beer is on tap, and the pigs in the bags
Of the purchasing cookies arc changed.
There were lovable characters out in the West,
Of fifty hot summers, or more,
Who could not be proved, when it came to the test.
Too old to be sent to the war:
They were all forty-five and were orphans, they said,
With no one to keep them, or keep ;
And mostly in France, with the world’s bravest dead,
Those lovable characters sleep.
1 long for the streets, but the Lord knoweth' best,
For there I am never a saint;
There are lovable characters out in the West.
With humor heroic and quaint ;
And, be it Up Country,, or be it Out Back,
When ,1 shall have gone to my Home,
1 trust to be buried ’twixt River and Track
Where my lovable characters roam.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The movement of the Commonwealth
public debt has been this way:—
June, 1911. . £15,580,771 June, 1915. . £37,428,830
„ 1912.. 16,020,296 „ 1916.. 101,252,949
„ 1913.. 17,079,398 Dec., 1916.. 140,730,768
„ 1914.. 19,182,333
Incidentally the States have been doing their
bit at another rate of speed :
June, 1911. .£267,127,283 June, 1915. .£342,925,669
„ 1912.. 277,124,095 „ 1916.. 356,337,064
„ 1913.. 294,472,486 Dec., 1916. . 367,436,823
„ 1914.. 317,598,788
There is £30,000,000 or so to be added to the
Commonwealth debt in connection with the
note issue; but, leaving that aside, since
1911 the increases have been:—•
Commonwealth . . £125,150,597
States . . • . . 100,309,54,0
Between them they have made Australia
proprietor of a blister nearly twice as big
as it was before, the comparison being:—
1911 .. £282,708,060
1916 . . . . 508,167,591
And each inhabitant of the Commonwealth
now owes £lO5 instead of the paltry £62 he
hadn’t paid in 1911. In the latter year the
estimated value of production from all in-
dustries in the Commonwealth was
£188,581,000; it may now be about
£215,000,000. In neither year was it big
enough to keep the country put of debt even
if the war bill is omitted. As the latter can-
not be omitted in actual practice, is leaping
up by over a million and a half a month, and
will certainly leap for a long time yet, The
Bulletin asks whether any member of Aus-
tralia’s 14 Houses of Parliament, the P.L.L.,
the Liberal caucus or the Democratic Federa-
tion has thought for five consecutive
minutes on how, when and where production
in Australia is going to catch up.

The Australian Bank of Commerce
(Sydney), formerly the A.J.S. Bank: —
To Re- Re-
Profit. Dividends. serves, serves.
June, 1910. . £11,670 3 p.c.= £8,441 £3,229 £3,229
Dec., 1910.. 18,421 34 „,. 18,103 318 3,547
June, 1911.. 14,957 24 „„ 14,550 407 3,954
Dec., 1911.. 12,268 24 „„ 14,850 1,372
June, 1912.. 24,249 24 „„ 14,936 9,313 10,685
Dec., 1912.. 25,863 3* „„ 17,929 7,934 18,619
June, 1913.. 28,312 3 „ „ 17,932 10,380 28,999-
Dec., 1913. . 28,808 3 ~,,17,932 10,876 39,875
June, 1914.. 26,145 3 ~,,17,934 8,211 48,086
Dec., 1914.. 28,094 3 „ „ 17,934 10,160 58,246
June, 1915.. 27,241 3 ~„ 17,937 9,304 67,550
Dec., 1915.. 29,471 3 ~ „ 17,942 11,529 79,079
June, 1916.. 29,124 3 „ „ 17,961 11,163 90,242
Dec., 1916.. 31,202 3 „ „ 17,980 13,222 103,464
The evil that men do lives after them; and
just about the worst that any man can do
for his country is to supply it with artificial
conditions through lavish foreign borrowings.
Artificial conditions were produced in this
country 25 years ago, and this bank lent
very largely on the boom values then created.
\yhen the boom stopped, the values crashed ;
and this bank —or, rather, its predecessor,
the A.J.S. —could not pay its depositors. The
struggle went on for weary years; and then
a scheme was hatched by late general-mana-
ger Stewart to clean up the wreckage. After
twisting things this way and that, there
seemed no way out but to capitalise one-
fourth of the old debt; and that was so large
that, with the bit of old capital left, the
total share capital is £1,198,679 —all now
fully paid. Another one-lialf of the old debt
was converted, into 4 p.c. inscribed deposits;
and, as £15,000 of these have to be redeemed
each half-year (though not necessarily out of
profits), this lot lias been reduced to
£1,555,605. The capital is very large in pro-
portion to the total funds. All the other
money at the disposal of the bank—deposits,
etc. —is equal to only £1 9s. for every £1 of
the share capital and inscribed stock com-
bined. In these circumstances a large divi-
dend on the capital is at present out of the
questionq and the 3 p.c. to which the direc-
tors have thus far limited themselves is not
attractive.
But little by little both the borrowing and
the lending public will see that in this case
the small dividend, instead of being a sign of
weakness, as it often is, is a sign of strength.
For it means that thei*e is an overwhelming
amount of money fixed in the business to
safeguard the deposits. The bank owes the
depositors and other creditors who can with-
draw their money £3,955,640. Deduct
£2.013,399, the cash and liquid assets, and
£1.942,241 remains; and against that debt
to the public there are advances £4,444,898,
with £15,000 set by to purchase
stock and £358,132 in bank premises; and
bank premises and the advances taken over
from the old bank in 1909 were written down
hard. If a depositor wants assets against
his little bit, he can’t hope to find a bank in
Australia that can show him better figures.
But perhaps the bank has had more
trouble in finding satisfactory borrowers. In
the weary years of liquidation the public
didn’t exactly rush the A.J.S. with its best
business. The prudent borrower wants a
bank whose future is so sure that it can be
trusted not to call up the loan at the most
awkward time; and the A.J.S. wasn’t that
sort of bank. So moss grew up around it;
and the A. 8.0. has had to wear a new path
to the doors. It has been a slow process, but
none the worse on that account. Going back
to the December before the war year, there
have been these changes, and they are all for
the better: —
Dec., 1913. Dec., 1915. Dee., 1916.
Paid capital . . £1,195,447 £ 1,196,109 £ 1,198,679
Inscribed deposits 1,672,738 1,595,334 1,555,665
All other debts . . 3,099,067 3,487,563 3,955,640
Liquid assets .. 1,735,315 1,818,776 2,013,399
Advances, etc. . . 3,914,038 4,187,098 4,444,898
■Premises. . . . 360,706 355,153 358,132
Thus in three years the new bank has won
public confidence to the extent of £856,573
of new deposits, etc., and it has increased its
lending business by just over half-a-million.
Meantime, naturally, profits have steadily
improved; and, provided there is no catas-
trophe ahead of us, they ought to be at least
as good as long as money is dear. For dear
money suits the shareholders admirably.
With cheap money there isn’t much profit on
the million and a half of 4 p.c. inscribed
deposit stock; but 1 p.c. improvement in the
net earnings of that money is equal to very
nearlv 14 p.c. extra profit on the share
capital.

June, ’16.. 13,909 134 „ „ 14,081 55.880
Dec., ’16.. 14,360 134 „ „ 14,081 279 56,159
How those on the inside of this.concern must
smile when they see the pack chasing the
Sydney Ferries and barking about its mon-
strous charges in comparison with those of
the Manly Co.! As a matter of fact, Sydney
Ferries has been and still is a poor, struggling
journeyman compared with this finished artist
in money-making and stock-watering. The
Manly Co.’s paid capital fog many years was
only £25,000; but there were also at one
time £25,000 of debentures, which had been
issued to buy out a rival co. In 1907 the
capital was watered and the £20,000 of de-
bentures then left were swapped for shares.
No other money has been got in. That is to
su.y, the capital put into the co. was
£45,000; and £3OOO of that represented the
purchase of the goodwill of the bought-out
Manly Co-operative Co. Shareholders have
thus supplied £42,000 of the assets of the
co.; the nublic has supplied the rest.
The old £25.000 of shares and £20.000 of
debentures have now been watered up to
281,616 shares paid to 15s. = £211,212, carry-
ing a 134 p.c. dividend.
And things are still going pretty well.
Three years of war, during which the public
is supposed to , have been gradually learning
to practise economy, have given this Tesult:
Dec.,’l3. June,’l6. Dec.,’l6.
Assets other than steamers,
freehold and plant .. £70,737 £108,973 £110,697
Liabilities 2,247 1,918 1,790
Surplus liquid assets. . £68,490 £107,055 £108,907
So in these three years, the co., besides pay-
ing the gorgeous dividend and keeping up the
fleet almost to naval form (in all the world
there are no better-kept steamers), has
dropped £40,417 into its stocking—almost as
much as the capital actually put up by
shareholders. And that is a mere trifle laid
by in three years to provide for renewals and
cover contingencies. If Sydney Ferries could
show figures like that it probably wouldn’t
mind if the public did bark at it. The differ-
ence between the two is the same as the
difference between Jhe Silverton Tramway
Co., which can make 60 p.c. or 80 p.c. divi-
dends, and the ordinary railway system,
which finds it difficult as a whole to show 5
p.c. or 6 p.c. The Manly Co., like the Silver-
ton Tram Co., doesn’t do any pioneering.
Unlike the Sydney Ferries it has no new, un-
profitable lines. It picks up all its freight
at one whftrf and drops it at another. And
it keeps in excellent odor with the Manly
resident by giving him a ticket for next to
nothing. The tripper has to pay all of a fair
thing: perhaps a bit more. But if he does
howl, what does it matter? One tripper
howling at Leichhardt and another at Surry
Hills wouldn’t make any politician take
notice. And it is from the politician—State
and municipal—that this co. has most to
fear. That and a tremendous bill for com-
pensation after a collision some day.

The co. changed its name during the year,
finding the old one too local now that busi-
ness has slopped over to the North Island,
starting at Wanganui. Possibly as compen-
sation to shareholders for having to part
with an old friend they are given an extra
1 p.c., making 12 p.c. for the year. Still
this is a case in which shareholders deserve
a good dividend ; for in proportion to profits
the disbursements used to be small, with the
result that to-day, while the share capital is
£173,254, the visible- reserves are £195,168.
Besides these visible reserves there is pro-
bably some contingency fund. At any rate,
a very large amount has been written right
out of the balance-sheet during the past 10
years, this money being spent on additions
and renewals of works, ostensibly as a set-off
against depreciation.
This shows what has been done with the
disclosed profits of the past 10 years:—
Dividends.. .. .. .. .. £163,142
Written off (spent on additions and
renewals) , . . . . . . . . 162,349
Added to reserves .. .. . . 140,831
Total profit . . £466,322
Note, by the way, that during the past two
years no amount is shown as written off.
Apparently, realising that discretion is the
better part of valor, the management now
cuts down the visible profits by whatever
amount it thinks necessary to cover wear
and tear. The effect is to throw the first
column of figures in the table out of step. It
is pretty certain that the co. hasn’t changed
its policy, which was declared in 1911 as
follows:—■
This meeting of shareholders endorses and ap-
proves of the policy as indicated in the directors'
speech in regard to the. conservation of surplus
assets so as to provide for depreciation and create
funds to work the business and eventually form
gold reserves.
If the management is still pursuing that
policy, but deducting something before dis-
closing profits instead of afterwards, then
perhaps, in order to make the first column
uniform, 20 or 30 thousand pounds should be
added to the declared profits of each of the
last couple of years. As for the past year
something unspecified is also frankly provided
for “taxation and sundry contingencies.”
A further view of 10 years’ history is .
given hereunder: —
1906. 1910. 1915. 1916.
Debentures ..£173,500 £150,000 £150,000
Other liabilities 91,319 £81,619 307,385 496,723
Property, plant,
etc 369,647 297,723 273,522 464,042
Liquid assets . . 128,368 99,891 542,587 564,641
Liquid assets in excess of liabilities other
than debentures totalled £37,049 10 years
ago; now they total £67,928. Meantime
£23,500 of debentures have been paid off, but
£7334 of share capital has been got in. Here
you have it then: in 10 years net liquid
assets have been increased by £30,879 and
debenture liabilities reduced by £16,166 out
of profit; in addition, profits have paid for
all the additions and extensions of works.
And that, of course, is on top of the divi-
dends, which have run up from 8 p.c'. to 12
p.c.

“There has been a slight decrease in the
demand for some of the co.’s products,” the
directors say, “owing to the curtailment of
operations in many local industries”; yet
Australia last year imported goods to the
value £81,923,955, or no less than
£12,435,036 more than it did in the preceding
year. Naturally there is a howl for more
public loans in order to “provide work ”
What we actually do with the loans is to
provide a few odd jobs at dirt-shifting, and
so on, for our own people and a great deal
more work of a superior kind for the people
of other countries. Having the hides and
tanning barks (though not nearly as much
of the latter as we should have) we ought to
be a very large manufacturer and exporter
of leather. But the boom conditions that
have been created by bad borrowing and
worse spending have made it difficult for
leather-makers to compete with foreigners
who are not nearly so well situated with
regard to raw materials. And we won’t do
much good until the evil effects wear off. In
particular we will have to root out the para-
sites who now make the cost of living so
outrageously dear in a country where it
ought to be as cheap as in any white-man's
land, in the world.
Meantime, J. (’. Ludowici and Son rely
upon their old-established reputation, and by
specialising in some lines for which they
have more than local fame, manage to show
fair results on a moderate capital of £56,644.
One advantage of this business is that it
requires very little plant; hence, though the
co. has its own freehold, and though stocks
are now up to £40,789, and book debts are
£8546, it manages to get along without going
further into debt than £4192. Apparently
buildings and plant are kept well written
down ; and generally, after a rather stormy
career for some time, the co. seems to be in
calm water. It has no goodwill or other
ballast to carry ; and if Australia gets sense
it ought to be a busy and profitable concern.

Builders who insist on a roofing that is water-
proof and lasting should specify Adarnax Rooting.
Climatic changes do not affect it because in making
it superior quality fibred woollen felt is thoroughly
saturated with pure mineral asphaltum. It is then
heated and a surface is deposited on it, which
makes it proof against the elements. It contains
no tar, fish-oil or resin, which are all affected by
weather changes. Therefore, it will not perish or
crumble, it is also proof against sulphuric acid
or chemical fumes, and is thus very suitable for
mine buildings for either roofing or floor covering.
Adarnax Roofing is cheaper than galvanised-iron,
costs less for freight, and gives better service. Send
for specifications and prices now to William Adams
and 00. Ltd., Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Perth
and Adelaide.— Advt.

Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Boiler, so univer-
sally used where the economy in steam-raising is con-
sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-
tion and excellent facilities for cleaning, the heavy
lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with.— Advt.

get half the capital without the £27,493. For
of the capital of £112,500 no less than
£102,728 is represented by “plant, mains and
expenses of installation” ; and the value of
that lot depends upon the profit it can earn.
That the mains and other plant are well pre-
served is of course important. On this point
the directors say -
Questions are sometimes asked as to the life of
the co.’s principal asset, viz., the mains, and occa-
sionally there are opportunities of thoroughly examin-
ing these pipes. Such an opportunity occurred within
the last few weeks, when, owing to a land subsidence,
a fracture of one of our mains occurred. We are
able to show to-day the pipe, which has been down
about 27 years. There is practically no sign of
corrosion inside or out, and this is reassuring evidence
of the great longevity and value of the co.’s mains.
It is good evidence'that the mains have a
long life. But not everything that has a
long life is of value. That depends as well
upon the profitable use that can be made of
the thing. At one time it looked as if
hydraulic power was losing its grip. Deduct-
ing the expenditure for material and labor
used in lift-repair work in the last year or
two (previously the co. did not do this
work), the gross revenue for the July-
-1 >ecember period has been :
1909 ..£11,430 I 1913 ..£11,856
1910 .. 11,852 I 1914 .. 11,551
1911 .. 12,129 11915 .. 10,336
1912 .. 12,089 I 1916 .. 10,911
Meantime there has been a tremendous ex-
pansion of building in Sydney; but the co.
not only got no benefit from it, but hardly
held its old connection. During the last year
or so, however, there has been a stirring
among the dry bones, and the co. has begun
to fight for business. No doubt it will get
some ; for there are purposes for which hy-
draulic power is superior to any other offer-
ing. But there are not going to be any very
fat dividends. For one thing, the electric
power with which this co. has Jo compete is
supplied by the City Council, which is not
out after profit; and in any case the co. has
to share divided profits in excess of 10 p.c.
with the City Council.

Tins eo. works wholly on its own funds; its
total liabilities on December 31 were only
£2lOO. Hence the higher interest which can
now be got for loans is all to the good of
shareholders. On the other hand high in-
terest means generally low value for pledges
and other securities; though thus far,
owing to special circumstances, there has
probably been no shrinkage. Later on, how-
ever, there will be; and then it will depend
on what the management has been whether
there will be losses on realisation. That
is the danger which confronts'not only this
co. but every trading concern with a large
stock secured at war prices; and the co.
which puts aside substantial sums out of
the present profits to cover contingencies is
the one that is most likely to feel comfort-
able in the future. Unfortunately one can’t
tell from published accounts which are the
wise and which the foolish cos., because con-
tingency accounts are very rarely disclosed.
What one can say, so far as this" co. is con-
cerned, is that it has had nearly 35 years’
experience, and maintained its 10 p.c. divi-
dends even in the black years of the early
’DO’s. Later on, in 1899, it had to drop
them to 7-J p.c., but they were back to 10
p.c. in 1903. As the co. has added £22,000
to its visible reserves during the past 10
years, and as most of this money is used in
actual trading, only about £7OOO having
gone into freehold additions, it is in a better
position now to maintain the dividend.
Certainly the capital has been increased, and
it isn’t easy to earn 10 p.c. on new capital;
but not only have the reserves grown abso-
lutely, but also in proportion to capital they
are now greater than they were in 1903.
* * * *
For years past the English-owned Mount
Boppy, Canbelego, has been the most im-
portant gold-mine in N. S. Wales. To the
end of 1915 the Records show that £1,526,766
had been produced from 849,421 tons ore.
During 1915 costs averaged 225. 5d., and the
extraction, after elaborate treatment of tail-
ings and slimes, was barely 305., and the
dividend was only Is. per share on ordinaries
and 10 p.c. on prefs. Since then costs of all
kinds have been rising. The higher quota-
tions for cyanide* to a concern that uses a
large quantity in the treatment of tailings
and slimes at a narrow margin of profit, were
particularly trying; and explosives have been
dear and sometimes very scarce. Mount
Boppy carries 121,000 ordinary and 30,000
pref. shares, and the modest capital of
£151,000 has been returned many times over.
In its palmy days the co. paid a pretty con-
stant 40 p.c. per annum. A feature of the
mine is the large quantity of ore that has
been discovered in lateral development, so
the close-down isn’t permanent.

The 12-year-old Cope’s Creek Central lias
had one of its sick half-years. The sluicing
plant these days has to be fed with a lot
of money to keep it jigging, and costs ran
this way : No. 1, ll.Sd. per cub. yd.; No. 2,
10.15 d.; No. 3,14.9 d. Floods, certainly,
were in part responsible. The half-year
showed £4O net to the bad; but by closer
boring and dodging poor places the co. ex-
pects (as usual) to do better in the future.
* * * *
A scheme of development has been decided
on by Bodangora (N.S.W.). Mitchell’s
Creek shaft and the Horseshoe shaft on
Dick’s reef are to be sunk deeper. For the
six months to the end of November 4619
tons, nearly all from Dick’s reef, gave a net
profit of £3121, or about 13s. per ton. One
sixpenny (£1250) was paid during the term.
The gross value obtained from the 4619 tons
was £13,005. averaging over 565. Reserves
are estimated at 7258 tons below No. 6
level, worth 625. 6d. per ton, and the battery
should recover 50s. There are also 2060
tons above No. 6 level valued at 405., and
at Mitchell’s Creek shaft the ore developed
above the 100 ft. level is estimated at 1500
tons, worth 525. The south drive at the
100 ft. level is in 435 ft., and the face has
been showing 13in. of stone, valued at 80s.
But there are many blanks, and just before
getting this patch of good stone 10ft. of very
poor ground was passed through.
* * • # *
Broken Hill Prop., which must be coining
money, like the others, out of its silver, lead
and zinc, has nevertheless had to pass a divi-
dend. The accounts to be published pre-
sently will show that the iron and steel
works are wolfing money; and the financial
results are falling far short of estimates.
Places are extra-good; materials are all
right; but. costs are said to be miles ahead
of the estimates based on U.S. experience.
There ought to be a plain statement on this
subject, backed by figures. On the question
of Australian labor costs compared with the
labor costs of foreign competitors we have
had nothing but vague and generally most
biassed moans and shrieks. We ought to
have figures to show us exactly where we
are.
* * * *
“Olio”: Before the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment considers putting up works at La-
trobe for the production of shale oil, why not
take into account the possibilities in N. S.
ales, where the largest oil shale deposits
exist and plants are already erected and
have been worked or are working? The
deal which the Speck’s House of Dodder and
Drift turned down was projected on the
recommenqation of Wade, Commonwealth oil
expert. He may be a fine geologist, but that
does not necessarily make him an expert in
getting shale or refining oil. The way the
figures of expected production were set out
was not such as to impress anyone
acquainted with the details of this industry.
Why the Commonwealth Government, or
the Speck Government for that matter,
should go nosing round for information and
reports from anyone but actual experts in
the business would be hard to understand
if the Australian public had not already
got accustomed to that sort of thing. The
Bulletin's suggestion (8/2/’l7) is to
dll in George Georgeson, of the Metal
Exchange, on the ground that his experience
with the Commonwealth Oil Corporation
should not be wasted. It is pertinent to
ask what qualifications he has. When he
was appointed secretary to the Exchange
some papers gurgled about his “wide experi-
ence m mining,” but did he know anything
of mining when he came from Ireland to
the C-0.C.? And was his job in the 0.0 O.
a technical one requiring knowledge of oil-
distillation and refining, or was it as a
salesman that he moved and had his being?
These questions should be answered if there
really is any intention of emploving this
man, who. anyhow, has his Metal Exchange
job to look after.
* * * *
Two old Victorian fields which have done
nothing for the last 20 or 30 years are now
buzzing again. They have been roused by
the call for antimony for munitions. Cloii-
binane, near Kilmore, which closed down 25
years ago, because go much antimony turned
up that the amalgamation of the gold was
prevented, is now being reopened by the
Clonbinane Co., mostly financed in Mel-
bourne. Antimony is the main objective,
with the gold as a sideline. A concentration
plant has been installed on the site of the
Apollo, along the line of the old Golden
Dyke. About 6000 tons are already out,
worth loz. gold and 17 per cent, antimony,
and a prospecting cut across the dyke
has located six lodes carrying gold and
antimony within 250 ft. of the surface. At
Costerfield, near. Heathcote, the old anti-
mony and gold mines are again working, and
during the last 12 months produced 12,382
tons of ore which yielded 3300 tons of con-
centrates. Some 300 men are already
employed and £60.000 in wages were paid
during the year. The concentrates were sent
to the St. Helens Smelting and Refining Co.,
England.
* * * *
Cliiltern (Vic.) is a sick field these days,
but it is claimed for its one still-alive mine,
the Cliiltern Valley, that it showed the way
to the world in using electric power for
haulage underground. They use a 6VIO h.p.
motor on a 16in.-gauge track, and it hauls
50 or 60 trucks and sometimes as many as
100, each holding over j-ton of dirt.
Before they shifted the plant further down
the lead some of the stuff used to be hauled
as much as two miles. They have already
worked down the lead about half a dozen
miles, and reckon to keep on going till they
reach the Murray or the sea or some other
obstruction.
• * » •
Attempts are being made to bring Pad-
dington, near Broad Arrow (W.A.), back
to life. The township once had two brew-
eries, six pubs, several stores and a mayor;
now all that remains are a few weather-
beaten lean-tos tenanted by goats. Several
shows were worked in the neighborhood, the
biggest of which was the Paddington Con-
sols, owned by the London and Globe Cor-
poration. The. show, however, did not come
up to. expectations, and finally went under.
Big piles of residues are dumped on the sur-
face of this derelict, and a syndicate is erect-
ing a plant to try to extract whatever gold
there is in them. Other parties are busy
testing adjacent has-beens.
* * * *
Ivalgurli (W.A.) doesn’t expect to last
much longer. Last year it won over £50,000
less than in 1915, and this year will pro-
bably earn a good deal less. The mining
position is still shaky, and is certain to go
from bad to worse. The balance-sheet
shows a surplus of assets over liabilities
equal to 10s. per share, which will be pro-
bably held and distributed among share-
holders when the end comes. This, according
to official estimates, should be within two
years. It may come much sooner than that,
as the. Board has decided to stop the moment
operations become unprofitable.
From an old mining man at Westonia: —
Edna May’s last half-yearly report states that the
amount of stoping done in the various parts of the
mine can be ascertained “by 'a perusal of the longi-
tudinal section enclosed.” No such section, how-
ever, is attached to the report that reached me. A
poor co. might plead economy, but that plea cannot
be advanced by one distributing 365. per share per
annum in dividends. It is time cos. were com-
pelled to publish sections showing their ore reserves,
compiled by qualified surveyors. Under present condi-
tions a Westralian estimate in nine cases out of 10
is only an expression of opinion.
A lot of misstatements have been made concerning
Stuckey’s cementing process, which has proved the
cheapest and most successful method of shaft sink-
ing in heavy water. Scoffers have alleged that the
Deeps never had the water pressure Edna May has;
while Deeps supporters claim that Stuckey’s method
could be used just as effectively in stoping
as in shaft-sinking. Undoubtedly the process is all
right in sinking; but the cost would be quite pro-
hibitive in stoping; and the danger of collapse and
disaster would be very great where large faces were
secured in this way.
