LETTER
Sydney, February 12, 1917.
My Dear Arini, —
A new verbal disease is on the lips of the
community—“l couldn’t say,” sometimes
varied with “I couldn’t tell you.” Replies
to the cross-examiners in the Howell Price
case are 50 per cent. “I couldn’t say,”
instead of “I don’t know,” or “I’m not cer-
tain.” The other day an elderly priest, a
passenger by a suburban train, dashed back
to his compartment just after he bad left it
and asked anxiously of a group of women,
‘‘Did you notice who took my overcoat? I
left it on the back of this seat.” One of the
women replied, “I couldn’t tell you.” The
old. gentleman’s face took on an amazed
expression. He stepped on to the platform
remarking to a friend: “But that’s a bold
one!” He clearly thought the woman knew
who took the coat, but wasn’t going
to tell. Although the shibboleth is not
ungrammatical it doesn’t express the mean-
ing intended. There must be a phrase-genn
—a very infectious microbe with which the
commonest book-worm would scorn to
acknowledge relationship.
The head of the Antills died at Jarvis-
held, near Picton, last week —an old lady
of 86. The Antills are a connecting link
with the Macquarie era. The Major who
founded the Australian family came in the
suite of Macquarie as an officer of the 73rd
Regiment. Jarvisfield, his grant at “Stone-
quarry,” subsequently Picton, he named after
a Macquarie estate in Scotland, and it was
there the old lady died. The eldest son
now owns Jarvisfield; the second son is
Brigadier-General Antill, on active service.
The wife of Brigadier-General 11. B. Las-
setter is a daughter, and G. F. Antill,
of the Commercial Bank, Warren, is the
youngest son. The Antills of old had a share
in the American War and in most other
oubles that have had gunpowder in them.
Captain Hollis, Marine Superintendent of
lie Commonwealth Dominion Line, must be
'an anxious man these days. His wife and
two children—the latter infants (one in
arms) —were on the Port Adelaide when she
was torpedoed a week or more ago off the
Irish Coast. Cables say no lives were lost,
but crew and passengers were in boats for
some time in the bitter weather. The Hollis
trio were cn route for Sydney, and lost
everything in the way of valuables and per-
sonal effects. Captain Hollis had been
commander of the Port Lincoln (late Maka-
rene), which was mined in the English
Channel.
The first notable score in the contest
between the Williamson people and the
Taits has been made by “the Firm,” which
has secured Marie Tempest and Cyril Maude.
When the Taits took the field there was a
rumor, which seemed authentic, that they
would give us some of the better-class Eng-
lish comedies, dozens of which have been
put on of recent years but have not reached
this backwater. There have been unmistak-
able signs of late that “crook” melodramas
and farces acted through the nose are giving
playgoers the tired feeling. To turn
to English drama was therefore a good move,
and, if time has not withered them and they
bring the stuff with which they scored their
chief English successes, Marie Tempest and
Cyril Maude should do something to raise
the standard of theatrical fare in this
country.
Maoriland and Brisbane trippers, cn route
to the Cold Country, have had a very jumpy
time of it over the passport restrictions.
Isabel Moffat, of the northern city, had her
precious booklet taken away from her by
one official and thrust back upon her by
another until she was giddy. But finally she
got it for keeps, and departed to sample the
various new perils of the seas. Mrs. Robertson,
of Toowoomba, promptly cancelled her berth,
and will remain here, hoping that her
General may get a trip over to display his
new decorations.
Recently Miss Joan Newton, who for
years has been secretary to the Home Mis-
sion Society, surprised her friends by doing
such an ordinary thing as marrying Ser-
geant George E. Hall, who has since left to
do his bit at the Front. One day last week
Mrs. Hall entertained a number of her
friends in the dreary Chapter House before
leaving for England. Here the cup that queers
the complexion was drunk to the accom-
paniment of farewell speeches, and the little
dark-eyed hostess remarked that so worried
had she been in case she did not get away
after all, that she wouldn’t feel quite liappj
until she was steaming out of the harbor—
even though the sea at the other end may
swarm with submarines.
Another passenger by a recent outward-
bounder, Mrs. Denison, wife of Commander
Denison, was kept on tenterhooks over her
passport until the last moment. For her last
few weeks in Sydney Mrs. Denison has been
the guest of Mrs. Edmund Playfair, who gave
her a farewell tea at the Australia on
Friday afternoon.
A Military Cross has been Avon by Dj*.
Weston in France. He may be remem-
bered as an American who became natural-
ised in Australia directly be learnt the
news of the sinking of the Lusitania, and
soon afterwards enlisted. He married a
Miss Cosgrove—a sister of Mrs. Arthur
Joavies and Mrs. Jack Toohey.
Mother can never tell. The young (and
unmarried) fire-eater Avlio has just been
elected to the Assembly by the I.W.W. vote
at Broken Hill wears the front name of
Percy. It always sounds like buttered
scones. Yet this Percy’s immediate object
in life is to burst open the prison door and
let all poor I.W.W. incendiary martyrs out.
His other name is Brookfield; and that
sounds like the Young Lady’s Journal,
too. Perhaps he has been goaded to it.
The squad of 20 English women-carpen-
ters who went to France the other Aveek
to build huts for the soldiers learnt their
trade in Surrey. They Avere apt pupils and
the contractor reckoned they would be
skilled tradeswomen in a feAV months. The
work they have to do is rough joinery, and
consists chiefly of nailing boards to battens
or crosspieces. The h~ts are the portable
sleeping ones which can be taken to pieces
and remade very rapidly. The women first
learnt to make all sections, sides, roofs and
floors, their forewoman being a neat little
person from Maoriland who arrived with
her reservist husband at the beginning of
the Avar. In the second week of their train-
ing they learnt how to bore holes for the
bolts which join the sections, and later do
the wood-splitting and sawing. No woman
mistook her thumb for the nail after the
first time.
Dr. N. W. Markwell, medical officer at
Thursday Island, and his family were cooling
off in Sydney last Aveek before venturing fur-
ther south. The family is in the fledgling
stage yet, but the latest addition has nothing
to hlame its tropical origin for. After enjoy-
ing a few Aveeks’ shiver by the Yarra, the
party will return to its habitat near the
Line for another grill.
Professor Gil Murray, the Australian-born
Oxford don and brother of his Ex. Hubert,
of Papua, can’t get a taste when he goes
home to see mother. Rosalind, Countess of
Carlisle, whose daughter he married, sits
verv high on the water-waggon, and in
November she had a large hole dug near-
Castle Howard and the contents of her
wine cellars poured into it. The cellars had
been sealed for 30 years, and contained some
choice stuff; but the 1500 bottles were just
splashed into the pit and the earth thrown
over the glorious puddle. The . wine would
have been most acceptable in military hos-
pitals, but Rosalind would rather have gone
out scrubbing by the day than “poison” Eng-
land’s gallant sons.
Major Jack Massie, son of Hugh and
grandson of fine old Tom Dibbs, writes from
the trenches that “the conditions in France
are immeasurably superior to those of
Gallipoli,” in spite of the fact that “you
have to dip your socks in Avhale-oil to keep
them from freezing as hard as boards.” The
weather makes him thoroughly appreciate a
big fur rug that Dad recently mailed him;
and as for the noble Christmas cheque for-
warded by grandfather—well, Jack’s ci'OAvd
knows something about that!
The rug reminds me of the remark of
another fine young Sydneysider, Emra
Burkett, who when the war started avrs
doing a curate’s job in the NeAvcastle dis-
trict. He soon went off in an ordinary Bill-
jim’s khaki. Later they made a chaplain of
him. “The principal difference betAveen the
two jobs,” he wrote in the freezing weather,
“is that the chaplain gets an extra blanket.”
If the desperate mothers don’t want them.
Australia does. Two girl-babies were found
last week. One was left on the doorstep
of a Si dney hospital by a Avoman Avho rang
the bell and then ran for her reputation.
The other poor mite was evidently flung
from a country train, and, of course, killed.
Under our terrifying system, the police
are as busy trying to find the parent of the
one child as the murderer of the other.
Why can’t the State take a lesson from
clear-eyed France and quietly Avelcome the
young human asset? It is regrettable that
every baby can’t have a cot and a comfort-
able home; but if it is to be a choice betAveen
the railway line and the kindly door-
step, for goodness sake let us provide more
doorsteps. Melbourne once found an Angli-
can archbishop on an English doorstep.
A man can be pulled up and heavily
fined for working a horse with a sore back ;
but the Law doesn’t make the slightest move
when an obviously careless or incompetent
mother takes a child shaken with whooping-
cough to a picture-show. You meet her pretty
regularly in the suburbs. It may be her
own, or some confiding friend may have
left it in her charge as sick nurse while
she has had an evening off to buy a hat.
The concentrated scorn of an indignant and
distracted audience occasionally drives her
out and home; but a large methodical
policeman should first take her name and
address for three months’ compulsory train-
ing in the State School of Hygiene—the
institution that doesn’t exist yet, but should.
The latest matrimonial advt.: —-
Gent like correspond Protestant Lady, bom sth
September, 1888, no other date. “Zodiac,” P.O.
More of the horrors of war! In these days
I can’t imagine any marriageable Gertie
cutting out all eligible mankind that doesn’t
happen to have been born inside one small
square of one year’s calendar.
From a friend in the trade: —
The “shiny taffeta silk” which you recently
praised for its “steady wear and good behaviour
in not ‘ragging’ ” isn’t really a patch on the
genuine thing. Taffeta splits sooner than tussore
or shantung, because the taffeta is “loaded” —that’s
what makes it so beautiful and shiny—and when
the adulteration drops out there is only a skeleton
of silk to take the wear and tear of being sat on.
The test is to burn a piece of each. The tussore
will “boil up” like burned wool into a shapeless
mass; but the burned taffeta w ill appear as a neat
black framework, which is the loading of metal
remaining after the silk has gone up in smoke.
The truly remarkable thing is that the inferior
article invariably brings a better price than the
pure goods.
Tlie death of Mrs. Michael Studholme re-
moves one of the best-known women of
South Maoriland, where she settled with her
husband—the first white man in Waimate —
in the uncomfy days of pioneering. Before
that her name was Channon and England
her home. An only sister married .Tames,
one of the gifted Moorhouse men. Of Mrs.
Studliolme’s six sons only three survive her.
One, Carlisle, passed out in Melbourne last
gala week in his early racing career.
Charities and church claimed the greater
part of his fine old mother’s attention for
more than half a century.
There was an evening wedding at St.
Philip’s last week, when Mabel Bowman, of
Orange, made the usual rash promises to
Reginald Clive Treweek, also of Orange.
The bride was a- picture in soft white
ninon and taffeta, with the usual veil and
diamond trimmings. Dorothy Glasson, in
cream taffeta, was the bridesmaid. Recep-
tion at Petty’s.
Eileen Ryan, of Hiawatha, Bondi, was
married on a recent Thursday to Leslie
McClelland, of Sydney. The ceremony was
performed in the Mary Immaculate Church,
Waverley. The bridal frock was of white
taffeta, trimmed with pearls, and the veil
was arranged with cap effect and the usual
blossoms. The two maids were Queenie
Ryan and Alma Meacle, gowned in blush
pink topped with black tulle hats carrying
long black velvet streamers.
“Midge” Giles, who had got as far as
Melbourne on her way -to marry Jack Chis-
holm (on active service in foreign parts),
must be an exasperated bride-elect at tins
date. A pretty trousseau packed, a senti-
mental bloke waiting, and a passage taken,
and then comes this verboten thing: women
and children not accepted as passengers.
Mrs. George Vivers had fixed her departure
for March 15. Now she is amongst the
blocked. Her husband (Dr. George) enlisted
as a private when he got to Blighty, but in
four months had his commission in the
swanky Blues.
Miss Dorothy Wilson was married to Cap-
tain Charles Lindburgh, of Hongkong, at St.
Philip’s Church on Saturday afternoon —a
very quiet affair.: The nice-looking bride
was in an ivory-tinted cloth coat and skirt
and a smart white French sailor-hat with
the modish high crown. Elsie Martin was
bridesmaid, and wore white relieved by a
pink hat and posy. Reception at the Went-
worth.
Lion-hunters at the Hydro, Medlow, have
shot down a fine bit of game. They have
discovered “Peter,” the author of “Trench
Yarns,” alias Captain Hudson, who in com-
pany with Captain Dawson is recuperating
after severe wounds received in France.
Major J. S. Anderson, M.C., whose family
keep the home fires burning at Armidale.
has captured in London Molly McArthur, of
Stanhope Gardens, S.W. The bride-to-be is
pretty and clever and a daughter of the
McArthur who was for a time Liberal Whip
in the House of Commons. Mrs. Leonard
Dangar. is a sister of the happy man.
Mrs. Robert Chadwick, widow of a tim-
ber magnate, had booked by the last mail-
boat to join her family on the other side.
Then Defence Department’s ordei’S stopped
the journey. But in the case of her step-
grand-daughter, Miss Osborne, who was
to accompany her, the ticket was not can-
celled ; the Department couldn’t be adamant
in her case, for waiting in L-ondon is a
bridegroom who is attached to the Admi-
ralty. And tucked into her luggage is a
cheery legacy of some hundreds per year
left her by her grandfather.
Mrs. Peter Rabett and her daughter, Miss
Binns, have just arrived home again, after
sampling war-work in London. Miss Binns
is showing our earls what a munition hail-
crop looks like, and, as the mode is becom-
ing enough, we may expect an outbreak of
it within a few days.
A small comfort in the shape of a little
son was given- to Mrs. Roger Hughes last
week. It seems but the other day that the
Hughes family rejoiced in the marriage of
eldest-son Roger to his pretty bride, who
within little more than a year of the wed-
ding is a war-widow and a mother.
Mrs. William Collins, of Nindooinbah,
Queensland, brought back her little brood
from Maoriland last week. She has been
visiting mother at Dunedin. She dropped
into a party one evening, given by Mrs. de
Burgh Persse, fellow-Queenslanders, look-
ing the last word —so to speals-r-in black
tulle, jet and pearls, the latter calculated to
breed envy and all other known vices of the
cat world. Host, hostess and guest leave for
Tasmania this week. Another Northern
visitor, bound south to Hobart, is Mrs.
Brown, who takes daughter Eileen for a
much-needed change after a strenuous
V.A.D. campaign.
A daughter of ILanbury Davies, who has
been responsible for almost as good work
with the Red Cross in Egypt as her father
has been doing here, will return to Sydney
shortly. She was looking towards London,
but that is a prohibited area for civilian
shes just now.
This comes from one of the boys:—
Thanks; but' don’t throw any more good coin
away on costly “watertight” matchboxes for use in
the trenches. We’ve found that the very best holder
for vestas is a small bottle. Even if Billjim gets
torpedoed and has to swim the Channel he is then
able to depend on his “lights” being dry should he
feel like a “fag.”
I’ve just been nibbling at the very exclu-
sive pasture of The Ladies’ Field. The
Lord High Penwiper, in giving a mention
to Sir Acland Hood, says he was once Gov-
ernor of Victoria. The tall, stoopy baronet
and his wife paid a long visit to the Earl
ILopetouns when the latter governed Vic-
toria in the early ’nineties, the ladies
being sisters. Acland Hood is now Lord St.
Andries. Perhaps it serves him right.
By the way, the same paper says that
“hospital nurses are marrying off at an
alarming rate, and it is worth while notic-
ing that the brides most in demand are not
the amateurs who have taken to the work
in the absence of social engagements of an
exciting nature, but fully-qualified nurses.”
General McCay has had a bean named
after him! It lived, twined and had its
podding under the title of the Zeppelin
Bean, but naturally had no luck under such
a name. As the General McCay its spon-
sors hope for the best—-and nine beans to
the pod.
Messages from U.S.A., where the Notes
come from, tell of the stage success of
actress Tempo Piggot, who made her Aus-
tralian hit with Harry Plimmer’s co. in
“Lovers’ Lane.” When I heard, she had a
touring job as Mrs. Ghichister in “Peg o’
My Heart” with Roscoe’s company. W. R.
Hunter, born in M.L. and staged in Sydney
—he was in “An Englishman’s Home” —
plays Aleric in thq same crowd.
The old-time Brown family I wrote about
the other day. in connection with the death
of the wife of the Parramatta doctor, seems
to be next door to immortal. That para-
graph has brought me the information that
of the Colstoun Browns, of whom the late
lady, was one, three are left. One, H. H.
Brown, Avas . father of the N. S. Wales
Assembly in the days when the present oldest
inhabitants of the House. Avere almost neAV-
comers. Tie is still in Sydney. Another is
Nugent Brown. He lives at Sandgate, in
Queensland, and left off being a pastoralist
many years ago. The third is Walterus
Brown; and Walterus Brown was police
magistrate in the Far Western _ country
before the discovery of Broken Hill made
things out there look different. He is camped
nowadays at placid Rooty Hill.
Talking of the Far West, that is Avhere
Grace Scobie. the most militant of all the re-
cruiting speakers, comes from. She is a
daughter of Robert, M.L.A., who settled in
Menindie a feiv years after Burke and Wills
made that their kicking-off place; and he
stayed there till the WentAvorth electorate
thought it Avas time Sydney kneAV the family
and heard a bit from father about another
Scotchman named Burras.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, February 12, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
i n< iulges in the real heather
walk these days. Whenever there is nothing
to open and the going’s good he takes his
stick and sets off for the horizon at a tine,
swinging stride. Last Sunday morning lie
tramped along the Esplanade, and marched
np St. Kilda-road with his eyes fixed stead-
ily ahead on the future.
Miss Macarthur Onslow, who returned
from Tasmania with little Lady Doris Black-
wood and has been the guest of the G.-G.
and his super-energetic better-half, booked
for and had a flying trip with a well-known
aviator before returning to Sydney.
Mrs. George Chirnside is in the limelight
again, and is now to be seen daily in the
company of her old pals, Lily and Alice
Fisken, going strong from morn till eve in
‘‘war work” of the most arduous and compli-
cated nature. This is right into her hands.
Her strong-willed daughter is occupying her
spare time in taking damaged Anzacs for
country .joy-rides in a new car. Percy’s
golden-haired wife is also doing her bit
valiantly—giving drag-picnics, high teas and
picture-show parties to wounded com-
patriots. The Fisken girls some time ago
attempted munition-making, but the job
proved too horrid and had to be abandoned.
Now, in addition to their other war work,
they are taking a hand in the British
Prisoners’ Relief Society.
Ex-Senator Simon Fraser and his wife
are revelling in the ocean breezes that blow
around their castle at Queenscliff. Mil
Fraser, who was 84 last birthday, preserves
the slim figure of his youth and takes a
daily four-mile walk by way of constitu-
tional. About once a week the pair trip in
the war-like Mars to Portsea, where Simon,
junior, and his young wife spend their morn-
ings teaching the two small boys (Simon and
Peter) to do nearly as well as Annette
Ivellerman.
The young globe-trotters, Mr. and Mrs.
George Green, cousins of Lord Nortlicote,
who recently landed here, and while on a
visit to Wirlgiewa were pitched out of the
Otway Falkiners’ car, are suffering a peace-
ful recovery from broken collarbone and
minor damages. Their breezy entertainers,
having arranged a trip to Rotorua, were im-
plored to go ahead with it—which they did.
Being quite recently mated, the Greens felt
they had enough conversation to go on with.
This is being passed round town as a
profound secret, so please don’t tell a soul.
Lesley Madden is going into business in
Collins-street, and will sell the most lovely
undies you ever saw. The shop will be
feahfully exclusive, and Lesley will design
everything. She is an expert needlewoman
and has found that the fascinating trifles
she hand-made for patriotic bazaars went
off like hot cakes or cold beer. That’s how
fhe got the idea. Australia Will Be There!
The vivacious Helena Rubenstein—now
Mrs. Somebody-or-other in England—is
making a thoroughly domestic visit to Aus-
tralia to see her people and show off her
two little sons, one of whom is six and the
other four. She is travelling this way via
New York and San Francisco and is due
in Melbourne in a few weeks.
The beautiful young Russian doctor who
is visiting here has quite a history. His
name is Klatchko (or some sneeze like
that) and he was for a time a prisoner of
the Huns. He was recovered in some way
during a naval set-to and was taken by
a British boat to Egypt. There he was
working in British hospitals for a while
till he was knocked over by the heat and
invalided here.
Major Rosenfield is homeward-bound
after a strenuous time in Egypt. He was
for a time the only eye-and-ear specialist
on military duty there, so he got all the
work that was going. Mrs. Rosenfield is
fluttering about town waiting for her man
and worrying about possible submarines.
She has not seen her husband for nearly
two years.
Louis L. Smith, son of the departed Dr.
£. £., is doctoring at a French military hos-
pital, and has won much professional praise.
Surgery is his specialty.
__ Long, lean and popular Stewart McArthur,
K.C., is home again in his chambers looking
over briefs. He went to England about a
year ago with his son Gordon, who is now
in the Artillery, and his daughter, Mrs. Billie
Knox, who wished to be near her soldier
husband. Back again on the home circuit, he
was welcomed back by his Melbourne Club
friends with some of the Club’s oldest port.
The Jo Clarke widow, mother of the
portly J.W.T.C., had the bad luck to break
her hip whilst summering in Tassy. The
poor lady fell out of bed one night and
groaned in anguish for hours before she
was heard. However, the doctor when he
came made a good job of it, and she is
recovering splendidly.
The Clarke sisters wailed on hearing of
the edict forbidding women sea-gadders to
lift a handbag. When one has acquired
the expensive travel habit it is hard to sit
still and keep out of the way. Sisters Roma
and Nesta had planned an exciting round
of war-time diversions in England with the
Norman Hodges pair.
Adelaide’s interjection
The Governor came back from Tasmania on
Saturday and on Monday graced the coming-of-age
anniversary luncheon of the State Bank. The guests
were mostly ex-M’s.P. who had been concerned with
the legislation which established the Bank —
amongst them A. M. Simpson, W. Copley and Alfred
Catt.
Miss Joyce Waterhouse is back from England;
which reminds me that the embargo on passports
for women and children is upsetting a good many
local plans. The wonder to most people whose
plans have not been upset is why it wasn’t imposed
months ago. War work in England is the com-
monly-pleaded excuse of trippers, but —well, I don’t
wish to be spiteful, my dear.
On Sunday the Chief Justice unveiled a Roll
of Honor, with 18G names, at Magill, which is only
a small suburb. Five sons have gone from one
family and four from each of three others, and
none of them waited for any special recruiting cam-
paign. The new campaign, by the way, got a good
start here on Tuesday night and the public shows
some faint signs of waking up. The trouble is
that it is the wrong public that is waking up.
Glenelg has embarked on a week’s carnival, with
the Red Cross, Trench Comforts and Belgian Funds
as the beneficiaries. The suburb has already proved
itself a generous supporter of patriotic funds, and,
with summer visitors to help things along, the car-
nival should boom cheerfully.
A. E. Kinnear has got the secretaryship to the
Public Service Commissioner. He started in the
Crown Lands Office in 1882 at £5O per annum, and
in 1901 was appointed secretary to the short-lived
Public Classification Board.
The northern Anglican bishopric is' to move its
headquarters from Petersburg to Gladstone, which
town is more convenient for Dr. White’s work than
the big railway centre.
At last the vexed question of the directorship of
the Botanical Gardens seems to be. settled, and the
billet goes to J. F. Bailey, of the Brisbane Gardens.
His father was the first S. Aus. Government Botanist
and planted the garden in Hackney, between the
Botanic Park and St. Peter’s College, which still
bears his name. His son was born in August, ’66,
and in ’B9 was appointed to the botanical branch of
the Queensland Agricultural Department as assistant
to his father. llis directorship of the Brisbane
Gardens dated from 1905 to 1915, when he succeeded
his father as Government Botanist in the northern
State.
Miss Hattie Knight, a bright young
writer who has contributed many smart
paragraphs to The Bulletin and has
lately been social recorder for Sydney Sun,
is to leave her job for a position of greater
importance. W. J. Leckie, member for
Benambra in the Kookaburra Parliament,
and a threatened opponent of Parker
Moloney for the Federal seat of Indi, is
feeling somewhat lonely and has induced
the little pen-woman to share his income
and solitude. The wedding is to happen in
Melbourne- in April, and every effort will
be made to keep it quiet. The organ will
be fitted with a silencer and the parson
will be shod with rubber boots.
St. _ Patrick’s Cathedral and the Oriental
were in festive rig on Wednesday last, when
Nancy Moynane—daughter of the Irish
solicitor-—changed her name to Forrest. The
young man she has promised to love and
obey comes from Bananaland and is the
son of Dr. Forrest, of Charters Towers. At
present he is stationed at the Caulfield Mili-
tary Hospital, but got a few days’ leave for
a honeymoon at Mornington.
It is quite possible that H. Y. McKay
dropped a few extra pounds into the War
Loan on behalf of a brand-new grand-
daughter, who has just arrived at Brighton,
consigned to the Sunsliiner’s only daughter,
Mrs. Clive Kidd.
Great rejoicings in the house of Willy
Robertson over the arrival of a long-hoped-
for heir. Colonel W. St. Leonards Robert-
son has a high place on the Instructional
Staff, and is in charge of the selection of
Remounts. He is the elder brother of popu-
lar Colonel “Jack” Robertson, Commandant
at Queeenseliff, whose stalwart son Donald
did so well at Pozieres. Mrs. Willy is a
sister of Lady Grey Egerton and Mrs. Ken-
neth (Muriel Cumming) ; they make a trio*
celebrated for their good looks.
The Russian-Australian Bureau of Com-
merce and Information bounded into exist-
ence last week at a big meeting in the Town
Hall. Hughes took a central position on
the platform and bestowed his blessing. Cook
bestowed his apology. Gaud-Mayor Hennessy
took the chair, and Russian Consul D’Abaza
sat at his strong right hand. Premier
Peacock wore a startling check tie and
thundered out his approval. G. H. Knibbs
looked like a young Father Christmas with
his white hair in startling contrast with his
sunbrowned face. Lieut.-Colonel Farr, who
was appointed secretary of the Bureau, sat
modestly in the_ background. He was Pay-
master-General in Egypt during the Anzacs’
stay. The committee includes a Baillieu,
Hugh V. Mcivay, aowett ana xcussxan-aauer
Myers. The visiting Russian doctor, in liis
loveliest uniform, spoke as a Russian in
Australia. He started last, just when the
whole meeting was ravenous for its lunch.
Even Hughes kept one bloodshot eye on the
clock. But the Russian had carefully learned
his piece and nothing would stop him.
Russian Consul-General D’Abaza’s father
is a Judge, now sitting on the Inter-
national Court in Egypt. D’Abaza himself
is a clever linguist who speaks English with
the precision of an orator, and he is keen
to encourage the learning of Russian in Mel-
bourne. He says that, since the war, the
teaching of English has been made compul-
sory in Russian schools. He scarcely hopes
Australia will reciprocate to a like extent,
but he has visions of general classes being
formed, and says the Consulate will encour-
age the idea to the fullest extent. Airs.
D’Abaza has been in the hands of the doctors
ever since her arrival in Melbourne. She
was ill on the journey across. The brief
glimpse Melbourneites got of the lady showed
them she is remarkably handsome.
The Praliran Red Cross works on regard-
less of the fact that these are the dog days
and Toorak is holidaying. Mrs. H. A. A.
Embling, the president, rules coolly and
methodically in dainty white .and her own
perfect complexion. She is local Mayoress
for tlie second consecutive season, her hus-
band being an energetic Worship who is
particularly keen on flowers, grassy reserves
and trees.
Over in Perth they have been indulging
in a Child’s Welfare Conference, a move-
ment initiated by the Governor’s lady before,
she became ill. Prof. Dakin, of the Uni-
versity, was one of its pushful spirits. So was
the young, shy-looking Le Couteur student
—he who carried off our Prof. Sugden’s
daughter to the altar. The Forrest lady was
also keenly interested, but her recent rushes
to and from Melbourne have taken up much
of her time. The Bungalow’s most hospit-
able gates are open now but spasmodically.
. The Ambrose Pratt family have re-
versed the general order of the dav and
have jumped from flat life to ground-level
housekeeping', and the handsome matron is
studying a cookery book in the kitchen of
a Toorak villa. They have lately been
travelling round viewing mountain and cave
scenery for the mental refreshment of the
versatile Ambrose.
The Celtic Club had a shamrock night
with Allen Doone at the King’s last week ;
and when Lesley Madden was escorted to a
seat in the front stalls the orchestra played
an Irish jig. Lesley had put a Paddy : green
fillet of beads round her hair, and with her
was Airs. Barry Thompson, with her black
locks also banded in green. Airs. Thompson
was an Irish girl before she became Aus-
tralianised, but the Irish home she was born
in was called “The Pah,” after a property
her father had in Maoriland some 80 years
ago. Allen Doone sang his swatest to the
two daughters of Erin, and they passed a
vote of confidence in his voice.
I-larrie Ireland, who has been ill so long,
caused a stir at the Theatre Royal one
night last week when she entered a box
with the Bland Holts to see Florence Rock-
well in “The House of Glass”—her first
visit to a theatre for well over three years.
The auburn-haired Harrie looked radiant;
she can manage without any support now
except a rather smart stick, and is hoping
for a complete recovery very soon and a
triumphant return to work.
Gladys Baker, a J.C.W. footlighter and
daughter of our old friend Walter Baker,
has been engaged to appear in the Alatri-
mony pantomime at an early date. Her
leading man is Lieut. Elgar W. Opie, now
learning the German Destruction Act at
Royal Park Camp.
The Overseas Club entertained the Allan
Wilkies last week, and the Hennessy dame,
as president, led the handshakes. The Gaud
Alayor came in like a hero about tea-time;
he is as cool as an iceberg among hosts of
women, and has practised ground and lofty
tea-drinking. The Peacock and Weedon
dames came along; also Airs. Hagelthorn.
who made a first appearance after her ill-
ness. Allan Wilkie and Frediswyde, etc.,
etc., gushed appreciations of Australia and
said they intended to stay here.
A Melbourne bachelor had a unique
theatre party the other night. He invited
75 children to the - pantomime, stipulating
on the invitations that the little guests should
be sent without grown-up escorts. Collecting
the youngsters by ordinary motor proved so
slow that he hired a char-a-banc and picked
up his guests by platoons. In the front seat
with the chauffeur was a huge suit-case
stuffed with boxes of chocolates. It was left
half-open to make the eyes of the children
goggle before they reached the party. The
young man achieved quite a sensation when
he entered the theatre with his family string-
ing after him, and a small, pink-frilled baby
prancing alongside. The veteran of the
party was a boy of eight. The host is
treasuring his answers to invitations. One
mite said she would be delighted to come
and see the vegetables walk. “But,” she
added, “my two front teeth is looth, and if
they fall out I can’t come. I’m trying to
hold them in.”
“The Alisleading Lady” filled the stalls
and boxes at the Theatre Royal oh Satur-
day, but the circle suffered from bald, spots.
Some Government House visitors were boxed
alongside the stage, but the - G.-G. and his
wife were not amongst them. A haughty
lady wearing grey silk and grey hair was
attended by a stray gent. help. Florence
Rockwell kept the same frock on all through
the play—through storm, rain, snow and a
motor ride. She only shed her gold brocade
dippers in order to help the plot. The dress
was very little but tulle, inset with pale
green silk and silver lace. It was trans-
parent from the waist up and from the
knees down, w hen her not-so-savage lover
took her off in the first act he bagged her
in a rug. Beatrice Holloway, making her
Australian farewell, wore an adorable cover-
ing of cinnamon-brown tulle rucked all
round. One of her house-party pals wore
peach silk, and another got into an outfit
which included blue silk stockings and gold
shoes.
The Italian Opera people are returning
to Melbourne soon, and their constant host-
ess, Mrs. Dougall, has been stocking the
larder in readiness. She entertained them
lavishly when they were here before and
knows what they like just as well as if she
kept a free restaurant.
A Japanese soldier who enlisted in the
British army in Prance has been winning
military honors. He is called Private Harry
O’Hara. Are the O’Haras in Japan as
well as in Ireland? The O’Briens have
turned up in the Mexican war that W. Wil-
son can’t settle; one of them is the prickly-
pear country’s most poreupiney leader.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

BILL: “Well, Jacky, what did the Germans
tell you?”
JACKY ( arrived hack with prisoners) : “No
fear! Not a plumy one speak English.
This lot all wild fellers.”
“Leo Lear” : H The Austins, of Barwon Park
estate, near Geelong (Vic.), have been cursed by
thousands of Australians for introducing rabbits to
this continent; but while they may have brought
over a few of the bunnies, I can lift the blame for
the original importation from their heads. A copy
of Chambers's Miscellany, published in 1847, has
recently been unearthed in Melbourne. Therein is
an article, “Li'e in the Bush,” by a lady, which
tells of the landing at Point Henry (Port Phillip)
iu 1839 of a man and woman from Tasmania, whose
luggage included cattle, horses, pigs, poultry, goats
and rabbits. They tramped 120 miles inland before
settling down, so no doubt the cottontails had the
innards of Vie. well explored before Austin landed
a consignment. Apropos of pests, as far as I know
there is no law preventing anyone landing a shipload
of tigers in the Northern scrubs to-morrow. An
Act might be passed in a hurry to meet such a case,
but after our experience of animal and vegetable
pests what we want is an absolute prohibition of
everything that grows, crawls or flies until it is
proved beyond doubt that it will be a benefit and
not a curse under our climatic conditions.
“Nymsie”: The circus strong man can
take off his hat to the venomous little black spider
with a danger signal on its back. In the kitchen
of the Express premises, at Rochester (Vic.), a
mouse was scampering about. A little later it was
seen to be in difficulties, and examination showed
that Red Spot was winding his web round and round
its body. A few moments later the hunter had, by
some marvellous system of haulage, suspended his
victim about a foot off the kitchen floor by strands of
web fixed to the table legs. For a day and a night
he kept close to his prey as though sucking its
blood in a prolonged debauch. He waved his legs
threateningly when touched with a wisp of the
house-broom ; but when the mouse was ripe we
squashed the little red terror. We were afraid lie
might attack the cow.

“H.H.8.”: If I was to mention his
name he’d come down and tear the office up by the
roots. But he was president of the shire council,
and at a meeting one of his fellow councillors
roused his ire. Rising to his feet he shook his hand
and bellowed: “If it wasn’t for bein’ a Jay Pee
I’d punch ye in the nose, I would. To Hell wid
bein’ a Jay Pee!” ,
* * * *
“Peri Winkle”: In Northern Victoria,
where a wet season has been experienced for a won-
der, the “drake” pest is badly in evidence and
local wheat agents are annoyingly suspicious. They
have found two grains of drake to one of wheat
in some bags. And the stuff is quite useless. It
resembles a wizened barley or anaemic rye. Whence
it comes and why it comes even the longest-whis-
lcered resident can’t explain. Somebody told us that
every pest has its uses and that the best brand of
whisky was made from drake, but somehow I think
he was a Hun publicity bureau.
* * * *
“G.M.D.” : Throw out that pear-tree carted
in by “The Hum” (B. 11/1/T7) from Warren River
(W.A.) and stick this really big one in its place.
Its home is Kerrisdale, D. L. Doolette’s property on
the King Parrot (Vie.). I do not know the age of
the old warrior, but it is still strong and heart}', and
at present carrying an enormous crop of fruit. It is
52ft. high, has an average spread of branches of 43ft.
and a butt 9ft. Bin. around. Unfortunately it is of
the Jargonelle variety, its fruit rotten to" the core
when fully ripe, but very useful for . rabbit poisoning.
* * * *
“E.M.W.”: The Darling Ranges of Wes-
tralia produce a great number and variety of car-
nivorous plants. Commonest among these is one
called by the children rainbow-plant. It is a
creeper which grows to a length of about 18 inches
and has tiny sundew cups on short stems at irregular
intervals. There is only one other sundew, this
being of the ordinary flat-leaved type. A very common
fl}-catcher has a small four-petalled flower with a
hammer which springs up and imprisons the adven-
turous insect. It occurs in six or .seven forms—in color,
white, cream, pink, yellow, blue and heliotrope. The
well-known spider orchid is carnivorous. Two small
fringes close from either side on the insect, which
is then slimed and absorbed in the usual wav. At
a spot in the Darling Ranges about 20 miles from
Perth I counted on one day in January 106 different
varieties of wild flowers. This though the full
flower season occurs in October. The far southern
portion of the State contains a greater profusion of
flora, but, I believe, much less variety.
* * * ' *
“Bad Australian”: Out on the debatable
ground which forms the frontier of Bolivia and
Brazil I was starting in to entertain the local
cacique or Lord Mayor and his frowsy satellites on
methylated spirit and canned opera when I found
that I had lost or mislaid my supply of “record
ticklers.” I had just offered the best apology I
could in the circumstances and had opened another
bottle of pop, when the old cacique rose slowly ip.
his place, and solemnly draining the heel taps which
remained in three battered pannikins, took a long
pull at the newly-opened bottle, kicked a way through
the multitude of dogs and naked nippers which
always infest an Indian camp, and strode moodily
into the wilderness. Now, when a savage suddenly
rises from the festive board and strides moodily into
the wilderness or the adjacent camp-meeting of the
Sons of Temperance, it is a bad sign for the company;
but while I was busy trying to figure out the betting
on the final result, the noble red (or yellow) man
strode moodily back again, and, drawing his heavy
“Solingen” blade, nipped the points off a bunch of
cactus spines which he had garnered from the wilder-
ness hereinbefore mentioned, and handed them to me
with an interrogatory grunt. I tumbled to the scheme
at once, and, fitting one of the points in the socket,
touched the gramophone off and scored one of the
most brilliant successes of an exceptionally brilliant
career. The spine of the S. American giant cactus
is about as long and thick and nearly as hard as a
knitting-needle, and in many places is used as a
substitute for that useful instrument. This success-
ful experiment suggests that Billjim need never be
cornered for a “tickler” for his phonograph as long
as there is a “bony” bream or “yellow belly” in the
“crik,” a porcupine in the nearest ant-bed, or any
other hard thorny circumstance of suitable dimen-
sions within cooee.

“F.W.W.” : The inland N. S. Wales cocky
has not been wildly enthusiastic over the Agricul-
tural Department’s scheme of building silos for him;
and there’s no reason why he should. Cocky’s best
plan of conserving ensilage is the “pit” system,
which costs nothing but a few days’ hard yakker.
Any cocky with a team of horses, plough and a
“tumbling tommy” can root out in a week a hole
capable of storing a couple of hundred tons of
■ensilage and 40 acres of a green wheat crop will
fill it. The needful pressure can be got by drawing
the waggon through the pit and by building up the
greenstuff to about 10ft. above the surface and
piling on top the earth he has scraped out. The
weight of the earth will press down the contents of
the pit to land level.
“J.M.H.”: Talking about balolo. One of
the most interesting sights of the Pacific is the
crowd of canoes, boats and launches gathered off
Toko, near Levuka, filled with a laughing, chatter-
ing crowd of all colors collecting the fish; the sea
around alive with barracoota and others gathered for
the, feast. “Joe Bagley” (B. 21/12/T6) says the
fish “remains up until sundown” ; actually it rises
before grey dawn and disappears at sunrise or imme-
diately after. The balolo flotilla leaves Levuka
before 4 a.m. and returns about 7. The long,
wriggling, worm-like objects, no bigger than the
lead of-an ordinary pencil, emerge in a thick mass
‘from holes in the coral reef, but ' only at a few
well-known spots, and are scooped out. of the sea
with small dippers like butterfly nets, made of
muslin or mosquito netting. The writhing mass of
green and brown worms in the bucket or tub is far
from appetising in appearance, but cooked in cocoa-
nut milk (vaka viti) or fried in batter (vaka papa-
hoji) they are excellent, with a haunting flavor of
the reef.
“F.W.W.” : Re “Pick's” (B. IS/IT7) re-
marks on loss of horses from eating musty ensilage.
Mustiness is merely bacteria which sets up -bowel
irritation, very- often with fatal results. I knew an
old Riverina squatter who lost a thousand pounds’
worth of rams before he jerried that musty ensilage
was the murderer. Properly-made ensilage is never
musty, and it doesn’t matter how wet the green
crop is when put in. The whole secret of ensilage-
making is to keep moving, pack the material well
and leave the atmosphere outside, not inside, the
silo. Incidentally, after the ensilage is made my
experience is that it is not advisable to feed horses
on it if any other tucker is handy.
“Agricola”: The cause of the death of
“Pick’s” horses (B. 18/I/’l7) was mould poisoning,
and is a subject of immense importance. Pro. L.
11. Pammel, of lowa (U.S.A.), says that horses there
have died in numbers through eating affected corn,
and Dr. Cameron, of the Vic. Agric. Dept., investi-
gated the subject in 1906 when horses were dying
in the Malice.. He found that spinal meningitis is
closely related to the forage poisoning. A toxin is
caused through the activity of the fungi and im-
paction paralysis, or costal disease, (which has been
so widespread and little understood), is produced.
Native grasses seem to be extremely liable to the
attacks of moulds and the low content of lime and
phosphoric acid in many areas seems to be a pre-
disposing cause. Pammel, in Feb., 1915, said that
the maize fungus was fusarium.

“Lord Toothpick” : Billji in remains calm
and unmoved in the whirl of London. One of Eng-
land's noblest young sons was driving through Picca-
dilly when Bill lurched on to the roadway, holding
up his hand as a signal to stop. When the carriage
came to a standstill Billjim produced a match, and
striking it on the wheel lit up a bumper. “Thank
you, old sport,” he said heartily, and went on his
way to the nearest place of worship.

AGAIN!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve gone upon the land and tried all sorts of things
to grow ;
I’ve lived upon my wealth until the hole began to
show ;
I’ve had a fly at many things. I’ve bartered, bought
and sold,
But now I'm baclc with pick in hand upon a bit of
gold.
I’m boss and I am employee, no strikes disturb my
rest,
And I can graft or I can loaf just as it suits me
best;
Let others talk of city life, of trams and pubs and
noise,
I wouldn’t swap, my prospects now for all their
city joys ;
Of idle days and genteel ways I’ve more than had
my fill—-
It’s good to be at work again with hammer, gad
and drill.
Bright hopes go ringing through my brain as ring
the hammer blows,
And every bit I dolly up a half-ounce prospect
shows;
Yet I am only on the cap, the rubbish on the
crown—
Lord knows what sort of stuff will be a few feet
further down !
Just twenty yards along the line Mick Geelan got
his dab,
And fifty ounces was the kind of ore that Michael
had ;
And further south two hundred feet, down where the
gully runs,
Light hundred ounces was the yield from Rowe’s one
hundred tons.
In every dish the tail of gold gives me the old-time
thrill—
It’s good to be at work again with hammer, gad
and drill.
What matter rough and blistered hands and sun-
burned arms that ache,
When I can dream the miners’ dream about the
“punch” I’ll make!
What matters if it duffers out! For other spots I’ll
try,
And when I strike bonanza Stuff just think of all
I’ll buy!
Again I’ll see the stampers fall upon my golden ore,
And bet upon the yield ’twill give, as in the days of
yore;
Again I’ll hear their friendly voice, and swear I
hear them say:
It s two-ounce tack, it’s two-ounce tack,” in chorus
all the day.
The work is hard, and I am soft from easy times,
but still
It’s good to be at work again with hammer, gad
and drill.

“Word o’ Truth” :
“When that Pommy said as Bill wos tellin’ a
lie, he got " wot he wos looking for. Any-
ways, a cove wot couldn’t use his hands
better nor that cove should keep his gate shut.”
It was Jim o’ the Pond that wos wordin’ us.
“As I wos tellin’ yous blokes,” he says, “I wos
missing me rabbit-traps somethin’ surprisin’. Tork
about ’poss’ snares doing a get—they jest seemed to
walk away and bury theirselves! I know’d yous
coves hadn’t shook ’em. So I goes round one
night extra early; and where my first trap ought
ter have been I finds a blanky gohanna. All six
foot he was, and took no notice o’ me. When I
belts him with a sapling he just does a reach with
his mouth and spurts out me rabbit-trap. Then he
done a sprint for the roughest stringy-bark an’ made
faces at me. I thought I had me trap, but I
hadn’t; he’d half melted it away in his innards.”
“I had wuss luck nor that,” said Old Bill, “when
I give the rab. brusher. Found two foxes at one o’
me traps. One had his tail in the'trap trying to
pull it away, an’ t’other wos diggin’ out ther peg.
Just got it free when I arrove, an’ he picks up the
chain in his teeth an’ the two of ’em scoots and
climbs a gum-tree.”
“Why, foxes can’t climb trees!” said that Pommy.
“Not Pommy foxes,” says Bill. “But ours have
Tamed by climbing up arter the rabbits’ nests.”
It was there that that Pom spoke too often.

“Jobber”: This is how to deal with a
jib. Stand the nark as far back in the breeching as
possible, then fasten his fly-switch to the axle with
a stout piece of tough string (not rope). Then per-
suade the horse to stand -up in the traces a bit, and
if he doesn’t go like a Jew from a porkshop I’ll give
up soldiering and get work.
A DISTINCTION.
JONES AND BROWN (at garden shivoo ): “Can you tell us, Miss, if there's a bar any-
where near?"
ATTENDANT: “Oh, you can get lots of light refreshment here."
JONES AND BROWN: "Oh, thanks. But we’re not thirsty—we merely want a drink."
“Warrego Bill” : Let me have the floor for
a while to debate the question of the mysterious
illuminations that “Will o’ the Wisp” (B. 7/12/’l6)
and “N’importe” (B. 25/l/’l7) have been talking'
about. Did you ever hear of the lights of Changkat
Asah? I don’t suppose you have, but Changkat Asah
is a mountain in the Malay Peninsula, and is sup-
posed by the natives to be inhabited by every known
and unknown variety of spook. At irregular inter-
vals mysterious lights, apparently similar to those
mentioned, in The Bulletin, appear in the vicinity.
They resemble Chinese lanterns, and are carried
along at high velocity by the wind. A rising light
has a tapering tail below and a falling one displays
a similar tail on top. The natives call these illumi-
nations “penanggal,” and regard them with super-
stitious fear. The theory, a la Malay, is that when
a woman dies in childbed she becomes a penanggal,
and at night her head and a short part of an intes-
tine break away from the grave and set out in search
of the nearest man in order to suck away his blood
like a vampire. The word penanggal means “that
which is detached.” The surrounding country is
marshy, and the part that the spooks come from is
supposed to be an old burial-ground. At any rate,
for sheer weirdness commend me to the performance.
The ordinary will o’ the wisp in England is a feeble,
flickering light, being merely marsh gas, a chemical
compound of carbon and hydrogen. This animal
never rises from the ground more than a few feet.
The penanggal, on the other hand, I have seen drift-
ing over Changkat Asah, which is 700 ft. high. Simi-
lar visitations have been noted at Ballachulish and ,
Glencoe, villages on the south side of the salt-water
Loch Leven, in Argyllshire, Scotland. Here there is
no marsh. Tonald puts down the uncanny phenome-
non to the devilry of a dead laird.
* * # *
“Nell Cook”: Tell “Barcoo” (B. 18/IT7)
that there is a species of nardoo growing on the
Barwon which is quite different from his species.
It resides on a thin greyish-green grass stalk about
two feet high, and the seed is about half the size
of .a wheat grain. Jacky-Jack.v’s grinding mills
were formed of- a slightly-hollowed sandstone and
a flat stone for the top, and they were worked round
and round until the powdered meal was produced.
1 have never seen the flour when it was fit for
baking, and it must be a very laborious task to
gather enough for the purpose. The gin who
showed me the stuff growing and the old mills said
the blacks did not trouble the vegetable except when
they were very hard up.
* * * *
“Spreader” : “Barcoo’s” tale about the 10
sheep on Isis Downs (Q.) holding out five years
without water (B. 25/1 /’l7) is a positive fact. I
happened to be repairing the telephone line con-
necting the head station with the blacks’ camp when
the stragglers were mustered.
“Fiat Lux”: “Two Seas” can prevent his
mare from rearing (B. 28/12/’l6) by fixing a
kieking-strap to the shafts in the usual place, passing
it under instead of over the moke.
“Eddyson”: James George Wjnstanley, of
Catgut, had fallen in defence of his country, and
Andrew fyler, J.P., shire councillor, and a man
of eloquence, was deputed to go out to the widow’s
farm at Cup-cup, and break the distressful news as
NOT DRAWN TO SCALE.
WIFEY: “ Isn't this a duck of a hat, dear ?”
DEAR: "Maybe. But isn’t the size of its bill
a little out of proportion?”
gently as possible. Andrew Tyler, J.P., elaborated
his plan of action during the drive. “It’s a fine
day, Mrs. Winstanley,” said he, “a very fine day.
Such weather should be a comfort to us in the
face of any privation. But that’s .not the point.
You re handling the farm well, ma’am. No man
could handle it better. You’ve had a fine harvest,
they tell me, the cows are in great condition, and
these pigs will be worth two pound a piece to you
in a few months. It should be a great satisfaction
to you, anyway, come what will, to know that so
far as a manager and a master is concerned a man
is superfluous. That is not the point though, either.
The boy r s are well, I trust, Mrs. Winstanley. Three,
you say, and all sturdy and strong. What is a man
more or less with the youngsters coming along like
a house afire? That is not the point, either. They
tell me you can keep a furrow with the best, ma’am,
and handle a horse, and fence, and dig, and milk,
and boss a gang of harvesters. Bless my soul, all
things considered I don’t know whether to condole
or congratulate ! Still, that’s not the point. You’re
a fine woman, Mrs. Winstanley; there’s not your
equal in the district, and if a man is really needed
to lun the place, and you feel lonesome and dis-
satisfied, you will have no trouble in getting a
second. In fact, ma’am, I am, as you know, a
widower myself, doing well too, and with no chil-
dien to hinder me, and I shall be happy, ma’am,
to fill the place left vacant by the recent misfor-
tune.” Tyler was forcibly struck by Mrs. Win-
stanley’s subsequent proceedings; and when he had
time and leisure to consider the developments, he
was sitting up in the dust-bath the hens had made,
with a black eye, a cracked head, and a feeling
that suggested damage to three ribs. Tyler arose,
readjusted his tie, dusted his coat, resumed his hat,
and holding his discolored eye in his right hand,
made a fearful reappearance at the widow’s back
door. “Ma’am,” he faltered, “what I wished you
to understand was the fact that your husband is
dead. “Jimmy dead!” cried Mrs. Winstanley.
She steadied herself. She thought matters over
with great deliberation. Then she approached
lyler. “ 01l > Andrew!” she said and wept. Tyler
had broken it gently.

A Day Shearer”: Let me ask “M.M.”
(B. 18/1/ what a slushy knows about verbal

lfe. Ive found them as good as any other as long

‘Tiaki” : I asked a scientific friend what
caused a whip to crack when it was flicked. He
said it was caused by the air rushing into a
vacuum. Then I told him of the crack which I had
heard when Blossom broke her leg-bone, concealed
in an air-tight skin. He suddenly remembered an
appointment..
“Barcoo”: Remains of Binghi's bora
rings can still be identified in many places on in
the initiation of the young buck into the mysteries
necessary in order to qualify to take a gin T h !
turwans (headmen) were the Grand Masters and the
rings were generally
formed on the crest of
some secluded sandy
knoll surrounded by
dense scrub. Two
stadiums for the per-
formance were scooped
out with shells, nul-
lahs and yam sticks,
the soil beihg removed
to a depth of 30
inches and transported
to the rims of the
excavations and
banked up to form a
screen against unin-
vited observers. i n
diameter the rings
were at least 40 feet
and were placed from
12 to 20 feet apart. A
communication trench
connected the two.
The lubras and picca-
ninnies were allowed
no hand in dressing
the stage for the
oeremony, and were
warned under penal-
ties of frightful pun-
ishment against view-
ing the sacred rites.
Very old bucks placed
at observation posi-
tions kept off intru-
ders with an instru-
ment in the shape of
an oblong drum. This
was made out of dried
marsupial skin tied on
the end of a waddy
with native string and
whirled furiously
round and round. It
created a most deaf-
ening noise. Certain
death would fall upon
any member of a
strange tribe caught prying. The aboriginals
during life had three names bestowed upon them—
rst, the birth-name; next, the “kipper” (youth)
name; the last, when he became a “malava,” had
grown a beard and was allowed to choose a gin.
* * * *
“Gee Gee”: I have often measured ’roo
jumps, and generally they average about 20ft. to 25ft.
The longest leap I ever saw a ’roo do was when bailed
up by the dogs, near an old well. He made a spring
at one of the enemy, landed short and went 125 ft. to
the bottom of the hole.
* * * *
“Gerald Barker”: Here, on the Goldfields
of TV.A., I have had many opportunities of measuring
the jump or stride of the kanga., and this is what I
have learned. On a straight run, without obstacles;
a full-grown animal will make uneven jumps,
approximately measuring 27ft., 12ft., 27ft., 12ft., and
si on. That is, of course, with the dogs in chase.
On parts of the fields we still have the grey and
red varieties, and a blue doe will cover equally as
much ground as an old-man red, although she is
much smaller in size. The pads of the ’roo on these
plains are the tracks into water, and I have always
found that their ordinary travelling stride is 6ft. or
thereabouts. “Two Seas” (B. 4/l/’l7) is quite right
about their dislike of going over a fence; they will
go under every time if possible. As for the brittle-
ness of their bones, that’s quite correct, too. By
the bye, the meat of the red ’roo is far nicer than
the meat of grey ’roo.
* * * *
“Nick Bottom” : Tired of “spotted dawg”
a deputation from the fencing camp approached the
. boss for a change of tucker. Among other things
the spokesman suggested blanc-mange and fruit.
“Very well,” said the boss, “I’ll send you some dried
apricots and a few packets of cornflour.” Then
Piercy chipped in. “To hell with your blanky corn-
flour, ’ said he. “We want bler-monge!”
* * * *
“Ses”: Of all the hatters I have known
none enjoyed his own exclusive company more than
“Solo Joe.” He was boundary-riding, on Whitta-
branah, and his camp was pitched in a spot where
nobody ever passed. He was perfectly content with
his own thoughts and talked to himself by the
hour. Sometimes he would disagree with his own
statements and argue the point, and at other times
he would laugh heartily at some yarn he told him-
self. He hail been enjoying himself in this way for
five months without a break, when some musterers
made camp with him for a night. After tea he
took his accustomed seat at the fire, but when the
visitors got into full swing he left in disgust. “So
much jabberin’ goin’ on here,” he mumbled, “a
man can’t have a word with himself at all.”
THE TEETOTALLER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tom Rogers was the greatest tank
That ever lived at Piper’s.
In fact, he well deserved to rank
Among historic swipers.
And one day when he slumbered in
The bar-room of McGibbon,
Upon his coat, beneath his chin,
We sewed a big blue ribbon.
Tom still was tight when he awoke.
He saw the bright blue favor.
The poor man’s accents when he spoke
Had quite a touching quaver.
The ribbon fascinated him,
He made no move to doff it,
But groaned, and gurgled: “I shay, Jim,
How long ’ave I been off it?”
VICTORIA.

The Inky Way.
“Quarter Single”: An Australian press-
man, Keith A. Murdoch, has done a big
thing—he has secured an interview, for
publication as such, with a British Prime
Minister. It was for the Sydney Sun and
Melbourne Herald, and was about the aims
and objects of the Empire War Council.
Murdoch, who is still in the early thirties,
is the London correspondent to the Aus-
tralian papers which subscribe to the
Times and “United” cable services. Origin-
ally Malvern correspondent for the Age, he
worked his way up the staff until he was
doing Federal “rounds.” Then he accepted
the job, in succession to Campbell Jones,
now the Sun’s managing editor, of Sun
correspondent in Melbourne. When Jones
returned from London to Australia Murdoch
succeeded him again. He thus entered the
heavens whose sun is Northcliffe, _ of the
Times, and when Billy Hughes arrived the
man who was in touch with the Times was
enormously useful. Hence, no doubt, the pre-
sent interview with Lloyd George. Keith is a
son of Scotchbyterian parson P. J. Murdoch
and a nephew of Walter Murdoch, now pro-
fessor of English in Perth Uni., and for-
merly “Elzevir” of the Argus. #
“Warrego Bill”: Anyone can have a
pressman’s job for me. W hen news of the
deluge at Clermont came to Brisbane I was
sent off at an hour’s notice on the 690-mile
trip. It was good going till we got to
Capella, where an optimistic railway official
told us that we could cover the 31 miles to
Clermont by 4 p.m. if we started at 1 p.m.
He was the son of a land agent. It took
us 11 hours to do the distance in pouring
rain. For about 15 miles we walked along
the smashed and twisted railway, and the
rest of the distance was negotiated on two
railway “pumpers.” We had 35 people and a
ton of luggage on those trolleys, and every
creek we had to wade through, carrying
our luggage. In places we travelled down
hill at a break-neck speed over lines which
hung clear over gaping holes. At otheis
we got out and pushed, knee-deep in mud
and "water, and barking our shins at every
step. We got to the end some time near
midnight. Then an unkind postmaster
refused to accept any wires. Next morning
early I lodged 1000 words, but in the
afternoon was told that there was no possible
hope of getting them away from Clermont.
So I walked out to Langton, eight miles, to
borrow a horse to go the other 23 miles to
Gapella. Finding the creeks beyond Lang-
ton impassable I had to turn back, leave
the horse at Langton and return per boot
to Clermont. When 1 arrived there 1 learned
that the messages I had lodged at • > a.m.
had not vet been sent. Next morning a
telegram from the office came sizzling in
asking me if I was dead !
* * * *
Mrs. De Boos, just buried at Euro a.
would have been 100 this year if
she had waited. Her husband who
died 10 years ago, was the Melbourne
pressman who in 1840 wrote up the
first gold discovery at Anderson s C reek. Her
brother helped to produce the first issue of
the Illustrated London News. He last
visited Australia at the age of 84. and did
not long survive the trip. Mrs. De Boos
leaves 21 descendants. The youngest grand-
son, Sergt. W. K. De Boos, and the eldest
great-grandson, Bugler Hoy Emmett, are
helping to “dis” the Huns. *
“Roundhead”: Locked his last forme,
Alfred Henry Mussina, printer and pub-
lisher, who, as proprietor of the Australian
Journal, ran as a serial five times in suc-
cession Marcus Clarke’s “Term of His
Natural Life.” For more than 50 years he
ran his Swanston-street printery, having
begun as a journeyman C>3 years ago. His
grandsons carry on the business. He was
the first in Victoria to use the linotype, and
it was on a linotype that his obituary was
set. The Richmond Free Press, founded by
him in 1871, predeceased him..
“Quarter Single” : Re smart headings (B.
l/2/’l7). An Argus cable sub., dealing
with the exploits of a swindler whose long-
suit was precious stones, and who was known
as “The King of Diamonds,” headed the
announcement of liis conviction, “The King
of Diamonds —Turns Out to Be the Knave. ’
Of unfortunate headings, a well-remembered
one was that used by a Sydney evening paper
when a barber suicided. ‘‘Barber’s Rash
Act” was the caption, but as fat black type
was used the first line consisted of the two
words, “Barber’s Rash.” A Melbourne
Herald heading on the Stamboul Univer-
sity’s nomination of the Kaiser for the
Nobel Peace Prize : “Porte, No Longer Sub-
lime, Descends to the Ridiculous.” An
Argus heading on the arrest of a forger of
cheques—“Cheque and Countercheck.” Same
on a Chow fined for negligently riding a
bike —“A Cycle of Cathay.” When Monty
(trover was on the rounds for the Argus
lie wrote up- a case in which a woman had
made a wager to ride her bike down Collins-
street in rational dress, but failed to keep
the bet. I forget whether his heading got
past the sub.—the late Jack.Nish., It was
“Breeches of Promise.” Another good stroke
which failed to see the light of day was the
caption used by E. N. (“Cabot”) McCulloch,
then of the Age, on the police-court story^of
a youngster who claimed to be a pirate —“A
v-' of Kidd.”
* * * *
“Copy Boy”: An inky wayfarer (B.
1/2/TT) asks for examples of brilliant
headings “actually printed in papers of
standing.” Well, three of the “heads” were
charged with "playing an unlawful game,”
and Sydney Evening Snooze allowed its
police-court minion to exclaim: “Three Up
for Two-Up.” A Melbourne Herald account
of a chess match between Sydney and Mel-
bourne teams, played by telegraph and last-
ing 24 hours, was headed. “Fellows, of In-
finite Chess.” The same paper printed a
par telling how a man was knocked down by
a cart, while pausing to look at the Flin-
ders-street railway clock, under the heading
"Time and Eternity.” A. bloke arrested for
burglary gave his occupation as “musician.
Up went the headline: “Orpheus and His
Loot.” I’m sorry that that was Amurrikan.
The most brilliant headings perpetrated by
reporters often fail to reach the linos. For
example, Monty Grover’s “The Sermon on
the Mount,” attached to an interview with
the rider of a Melbourne Cup winner.
“G.H.” : I' was struck by lightning just
before' I wrote the caption “Submerged
Tenth,” cited by “Sesquipedalian” (B.
1/2/T7). I was walking from headquarters
to the flooded telegraph tent when a flash
of lightning struck my umbrella and knocked
it out of my hand. The press at the time
was booming the camp and making light of
the discomforts of the men ; hence the need
of a headline with a grin in it. When the
trainees read so frequently that they were
having a good time they began to almost
believe it, despite their obvious discomforts.
It had the psychological effect of making
them grin and bear it. The almost naked
“Keystone” band which daily paraded the
lines did much to cheer the men. Colonel
Kirkland handled the brigade splendidly,
despite his harshness and love for discipline.
When the Hughsiliers went into camp I
suggested that it would help the Referendum
if the pleasures of camp life (if any) were
again boosted. I was met with the extraor-
dinary but characteristically military reply
that as compulsory training was the law
there was no need to popularise it. The
Referendum was defeated. The rest speaks
for itself.
* * * *
“Lou Par”.: Adelaide papers make a hobby
of interviewing the hoary pioneers who
thrive so well in South Oss. Apropos, a
yarn is told of the late Surgeon-Major J. M.
Hinton, who had served in the Crimean
War. The old vet. was a regular meal for
The Register, which dished him up twice
a year. The old chap was highly pleased
with the attention for a while. Then the
novelty wore off and he got to loath the
pressmen who shouted in his ear. Conse-
quently when he made his final exit he left
instructions that the press was not to be
told of it until his ashes had been scattered
to the 14 winds. The Register got off its
usual column; but by that time it didn’t
matter. The old man’s ear was in 25
different places.
* * * *
“Boulder Bill” : The WestraMan Worker
is on the lookout for anew editor —the
umteenth —John Hilton having laid down
the pen, after a record run of 3i years,, dur-
ing which he attempted to bring out single-
handed a bright sheet, while trying to sat-
isi'y 2317 people, who, having learned how
to lay bricks and repair boots, are naturally
qualified to decide how a paper should be
run. Tli 1 culmination came with the Con-
scription crisis. The Kalgoorlie Congress,
after three days’ discussion, expressed its
confidence in the Hughes Government, and
because Hilton shaped his policy accord-
ingly a number of Antis proceeded to froth
at the mouth, and Hilton had to go. He
is a life-long democrat who did hard manual
labor for a living up to the age of 30, and
lias since had 20 years’ journalistic experience.
“F.N.T.”: The South-street Society
(Ballarat) has just awarded 35 small cash
prizes as a result of its yearly “literary”
competitions. There were IS subjects, a
somewhat dreary lot as usual —12 essay,
three' story, two critical and one poetry—
and the competitions seem to have been care-
fully avoided by most of Australia’s known
pen-people. Charles Daley, of Caulfield,
heads the list with two firsts and two sec-
onds in the heavy-drauglit class (such mat-
ters as “Influence of Climate on Health and
National Character,” for instance). Alfred
Deakin’s fiver for an “original tale” was
taken by Bessie Threadgill, of the Holy
City, and another £5 for an “Australian
story” was won by a Miss Eisemann, whose
family farms at Bacchus Marsh. There were
17' adjudicators, all but two of them dwelling
in the rich intellectual atmosphere of Bal-
larat district —accountants, sharebrokers,
parsons, a doctor, a printer, a mining engi-
neer, an insurance.-agent and a variety of
people whose claims as critics are not ap-
parent at first glance. The musician who
judged the poems is, however, clearly a per-
son of taste and discernment, for he handed
the first prize to Mrs. M. Forrest, of Brisbane.
* * * *
“Mul'knh”: Here is my parade of prize
telegraphic bulls. Some Bananalanders were
lately mystified by a wire from Victoria say-
ing “Father sailed yesterday.” As they felt
they ought to go and meet the old man
tliev wired south for further particulars* and
found that the original message should have
been “Father died yesterday.” A few
months ago a country newspaper proprietor
thought the end of the war was in sight
at last, when his telegraphed ‘ press con-
tained a statement that the Almighty had
ordered four cruisers. A lam worth
CN.S.W.) man received a wire inquiring
“Have my potatoes left yet?” Being an
intelligent host, he wired back “Your people
still here.” The worst writing I ever saw
led to an astounded city firm receiving an
order for a truck of loose treacle. Boeksalt
was the article required. Telegraph operat-
ing becomes purely mechanical. The average
telegraphist would revive a message announc-
ing the death of the Kaiser and be as aston-
ished as the next reader when he saw it in
the papers next day.
IN OTHER DAYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I used to touch a joyous harp, in golden years and
years agone,
But when I tune the worn strings now, friends tell
me I am “getting on.’’
They mean thereby that I am old, and slow and
tame and dull of ear,
That Life for me should have no joys in Woman,
Wine or even Beer.
One, kinder than the rest, deposed: “He can’t
write stuff —well, just as good" ;
Another, ruder, put it straight : “B'tlosh ! The old
chap never could.”
N.s.w.
M. B.
HIS AMBITION.
[FOR THE BULLEIN.]
I am an editor (ah, me!),
And office troubles turn me grey.
An acolyte I’d rather be,
And swing a censor every day !
VICTORIA.
S. S.

Our blood is composed of
red and white corpuscles
the red to nourish the body,
the white to fight disease. In

or less deficient. Thus the
blood cannot provide suffi-
cient nourishment for the
body. Therefore the face
becomes white and “ pasty ”

“heavy”—and a feeling of
intense weariness pervades

Is it to be- “Fail Columbia” V
The last man is still being looked for ; but
Premier Holman seems to have found AndreAV
Fisher’s last shilling.
Last week the committee of the Henty
(Vic.) Labor League decided that it could
not associate itself with or support the
effort to get volunteers for the Front !‘as it
believes the recruiting campaign is greatly
responsible for the present state of unem-
ployment.” For The Bulletin’s part it
merely makes the Henty P.L.C. a present
of the following appeal of the working
classes of Belgium, and suggests that it
pray to God that, when Australia is over-
run and trampled upon, no foreign labor
league will remember the Henty resolution
and fling it in our teeth
Workers of Australia, —We do not doubt you.
Our cause is yours. If we did not denounce to
you the outrages which menace us, one day you
would reproach us, saying, “You had not the
right to keep silent and to suffer your martyrdom
in silence; if a nation of civilised workers is
reduced to a degree, of servitude, the whole working
IN DEFENCE.
JUDGE: “Prisoner, have you anything further
to add to your defence?”
PRISONER: “Well, y’honor, I should like you
to consider me rotten luck with, me
counsel.”
class is hit. We workers of free Australia would
not have allowed them to commit these outrages.
It is from slavery the modern working class has
come; it must not now g-o back.” Workers of
Australia, in the depths of our distress we rely on
you. Do you act!
Australia’s policy in regard to the
colonies seized from the Germans in the
Pacific —Hans off!
The Commonwealth got its £18,000,000
Avar loan and a good deal besides ; and now
it is time to look ahead and consider what'
is going to happen when the debt falls due.
So far, every Avar loan that has been locally
issued has had attached to it a promise of
repayment in 1925 ; and there is no reason
to expect that in 1925 money will be avail-
able for the mere picking-up. We shall
have to float other war loans yet; and it is
about time Ave followed the British practice
NOTHING DOING.
ACTOR: “ Thought I’d let you know I am
looking for something. Almost anything
would suit' me.”
MANAGER: “A.h, yes, but I have to he more
particular."
and spread the dates when the lOU will fall
due. The British Government hasn’t under-
taken to repay a -bean (apart from a small
quantity of Exchequer bonds) before 1929:
and the bulk hasn’t to be found until 1947,
though the Treasury mostly reserves the
right to pay up sooner. Also, on the prin-
ciple of not leaving all the burden to the
kid, the British Treasury sets aside monthly
one-eighth of 1 per cent, of each loan “to
form a fund to be used for purchasing stock
or bonds for cancellation Avhenever the
market price falls below the issue price.”
Though he is a terribly harassed John Bull
he isn’t so harassed as to forget that he has
to pay off a loan as Avell as raise'it. Which
is probably Avhy certain outrageous Censor-
ship instructions were issued in Australia a
feAv weeks ago—instructions that somebody
in the Senate ought to inquire about.
Russia may shake and France give at the
knees, but Junee (N.S.W.) stands firm
and defies the Prussian. Taking a lead
from the Legislative Assembly, the states-
men Avho make up the Municipal Council
lately passed a resolution in favor of pro-
longing the life of the body, as at present
constituted, till the end of the war on the
ground that a change might be detrimental
to the Allies.
Maoriland’s Military Service Act is
giving some queer results. Shearers
and slaughtermen from Australia put
in a portion of their year in M.L.;
they make a sort of round of it. But
this year the Union officials have
quietly dropped Avord that the absence
of the Australians will greatly Help
their brother-unionists in resisting the
Act; and the hint is being taken
enthusiastically. So the Australian,
having succeeded in killing conscrip-
tion in his own country, is helping to
make it abortive in somebody’s else’s.
Meantime in France
The provisions of Holman’s special Act
have not yet been enforced against the
1.W.W., which continues to hold its meet-
ings in Sydney, Avhile Direct Action makes
its appearance every week in a subdued
whisper. In fact, it is all subdued: with
the shadow of John Hop hanging over it
there is quite a Sunday-school look about
the whole outfit.
Sydney Telegraph lays down the law: —
If the Tudorites agreed... .there would be some
justification for approaching the Imperial Govern-
ment for legislation prolonging the life of Parlia-
ment.
So, if groups of politicians put their heads
together, and after a hole-and-corner con-
fabulation decide to (1) hang on to their
jobs for longer than they were appointed,
and (2) to rob the people of their votes,
the deed can be justified—provided the
thieves don’t fall out over the plunder!
The N. S. Wales lawyer will lose a lot of
work when the new Workmen’s Compensa-
tion Act comes into force on July 1.
The old Employers’ Liability ‘Act
allowed so many exceptions that it
was worth w T hile for the employer or
the ‘insurance co. to fight a claim on
the off-chance that some point
would get home. The new Act
brings in practically every em-
ployee—including clerks, domes-
tic servants and rural workers—and
gives a claim to compensation for any
sort of injury. It will hit some
people hard, of course; but it will
make the question of compensation
less of a gamble, and hurt Costs in
consequence.
It is deplorable that Supreme Court
Judges should have to decide, as Justice
Hodges did iu Melbourne last week, that
women clerks should get less money than
men, because “the minimum wage is fixed
on the assumption that the man maintains
the family, not the woman.” The error
underlying this is at the bottom of one of
Australia’s greatest dangers. Men begin
to think they can he paid according not to
what they earn but to what they want. The
true Labor principle is that the worker shall
have the fruits of his labor —that he shall
get what he earns. The . utterly rotten
practice adopted by his Honor Hodges is
that a woman shall not get what she earns,
and that either the man in the same em-
ployment shall get more than he earns or
the employer shall pocket the surplus earn-
ings of the woman. It is a piece of social
dishonesty that a civilised State ought to be
ashamed of.
After all, Peace without Victory is easy.
It is the sort of black eye you take home
with you after you have been too proud to
fight.
The only difference seems to be that
Cook was ready to follow Hughes and Big-
john to swallow him.
Oratory by Hughes to Melbourne Cham-
ber of Commerce:—
If the population is to be employed the employer
must know where he is.
And what has Hughes been doing to let “the
employer know where he is”? A comparison
of the imports during the last three .years
answers that question. For example, im-
ports of apparel, etc., increased from
£16,199,525 in 1.914 to £22,359.671 in 1916 ;
whisky increased from £798,964 to
£1,201.564 ; cordage and twine from £707,760
to £947,652. The Australian employer in
these industries knows where he is, all
right—he’s in the mud. And the tariff-
sinking Hughes put him there.
