( Proceedings in the Federal Arbitration
Court are only “proceedings” by courtesy
in many. cases. The other day ‘ the High
Court delivered a decision in an Arbitration
matter affecting the Federated Engine-
drivers and Firemen, as plaintiffs, and
the C. 5.11. Co. and others as defendants
4he decision was that, in spite of certain
technical objections raised, a dispute existed
which called for the intervention of- the
Court. The plaint about the alleged dis-
pute was filed on September 10. 191,1.
Patience is its own reward in the Arbitra-
tion Court.
THE ARROGANCE OF THE NEW.
FIRST AMATEUR (to second ditto): “Just
fancy travelling in one of those old-
fashioned dirt-lifters !”
Oh, yes, Australia’s statesmen are helping
Britain all right. Harassed John Bull has
for two long years been turning out trans-
ports, cargo carriers, patrol boats and
vessels of all sorts as fast as his mechanics
can do it; lie has even got his women folk
working 60 hours a week on the job. l’ot it
is a solemn and shameful fact that Aus-
tralia’s Navy Department lias two hopper
barges under construction on the Clyde, at a
cost of £57,000. This while a shipyard well
able to carry out the work is lying compara-
tively idle in Melbourne.
Brookfield, .whose endorsement as Labor
candidate for Cann’s old seat in the N. S.
Wales Assembly was hung up for a while
pending an inquiry as to his connection with
the 1.W.W., was branded before the polling-
day, and won easily. It was ascertained
that Brookfield was not and never had been
a member of the I.W.W. Of course he isn’t
a member of the I.W.W. If he had made
that mistake he couldn’t join the Labor
party. But the first thing he did after the
election was to preside at an I.W.W. meet-
ing, from which he accepted an instruction
to hold up the business of Parliament at
every opportunity until the I.W.W. fire-bugs
are released. It looks as though the Labor
party may be sorry for that endorsement
yet. Not that it made much difference. The
young man whose first plank is to “fight
like hell” for the release of I.W.W. prisoners
would have been elected in any case.
The P.L.L. of N. S. Wales held a sort of
stock-taking recently and the result should
shock the people who imagine that the
Executive had merely made trouble for it-
self. The purpose of the rally was to find
out in time if there was any dissatisfaction,
so that the decks might be cleared before
the coming elections. The 400 delegates
who attended as representatives of 200,000
P.L.L. members gave absolute endorsement
to the Executive, and proceeded to discuss
the measures necessary for hurling ex-
T.abor politicians into political darkness
and securing victory for the machine.
One of the most difficult problems after
the war wall be the woman who stays at
her- new-found work.
THE ANZAC’S REVEILLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Why crossed we the foam
When we worshipped our home,
And with death come to grips
AVith a laugh on our lips,
To be mangled and yet
Breathe not a regret,
But thrill with desire
Leaping through us like fire,
To again challenge Fate
Side by side with a male ?
Are we madmen or what
Who frolic ’mid shot,
AVIIO scaled in the night
Gallipoli’s height,
Where like lions some fell
In a blast as from hell?
Why flocked we to fight
In scorn and delight ?
Because that we saw
Might trampling on Law,
And glutting its greed
AVith an infamous deed!
Because—and thoughts caibe
With a rush as of flame —
Did the tyrants of Might
Crush the warriors of Right,
Australia adored
Would be swept by the sword ;
And our homes, each a shrine
That to us seemed divine,
For which we had toiled,
Burnt, branded, despoiled ;
And our mothers and wives,
AVere they granted their lives,
Stained, beaten, abhorred
And priekt with the sword—
This is why
’Neath an alien sky
Australians fiercely fight and die
In passionate love of Liberty-!
K.S.W. :
ARTHUR BAYLDON.
A BALLADE OF LTTTLE MEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The big guns boom and the shrapnel screams,
And the stench of valiant clay mounts high.
The soaked earth red in the sunlight gleams,
And the wings of the watchers soar and fly.
Here in the shelter of sham and pay,
Blind and impotent, dead to shame,
But never dumb, though with naught to say,
Are the placemen playing the party game.
A Quilpisli figure with deep-laid schemes,
Posing still in the limelight’s ray,
With gags, too ancient for Walter Weems,
Holds the stage for a passing day.
Another, bald as an oak in. May,
Pipes his platitudes, shrill and tame,
Or snaps and snarls in a futile way—
They all are playing the party game.
Leader-writers pen reams and reams,
Pifflers fitfully rant and bray,
And a Tudor speaks with the voice of Jeames,
For he who leads must perforce obey.
Statesmen we looked for —where are they ?
War grows redder with shame and flame —
But the jaws wag ever the Hun to slay—
They all are playing the Party game.
L’Envoi.
Lord, for a leader we humbly pray;
Grant to us one we can so proclaim.
Little men over the landscape stray—
They all are playing the Party game!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

-his navy—and help knock out Hun sub-

The speed-trial fixture recently held at Southport,
Queensland, has- created a very widespread interest
among motorists throughout the whole of Australia.
This took place during the Christmas holidays on
the 11-mile hard sandy beach within a few miles of
Brisbane, and the competitions held there resulted in
the establishment of entirely new records for motor-
car racing over various distances. All previous time
records went by the board, and not only all Aus-
but all other speed records for 4-mile and
mile sprints (except those made in the U.S.A. where
speed racing has become a fine art) were easily
broken. A quite remarkable feature about these
records is the fact that they were established on
practically stock model cars. A Studebaker Six
fitted with racing body and wheels and equipped with
Goodyear Cord Tyres, was driven by Mr. A. Jewell
over a J-mile course in 21 2-5 seconds and over a
mile course in 44 seconds even. The Overland Four,
and not a new car either, also fitted with speed body
and special wheels, likewise shod with Goodyear Cord
Tyres, made a close second by putting up 22 seconds
for the J-mile and 44 3-5 seconds for the one mile.
This ear also recently established a hill-climbing
record for the Mt. Cootha, Q., course, on both occa-
sions being driven by Mr. F. Z. Enger. Though
several makes of tyre3 were tried, in every instance
the fastest times were made on Goodyear Cord Tyres,
which are evidently suited to high speeds', and lose
least power on the road. The doings at this very
successful function will no doubt act as a stimulant
to motor, bodies in other parts of Australia and New
Zealand in the matter of conducting speed trials of
a similar nature. There must be in many parts of
this wide country suitable stretches of beach or
roadway which will allow fast driving without danger
to competitors or onlookers, and we expect at no
distant date to see announcements of some action
being taken. It is a far cry from a natural beach
to a banked and boarded Speedway, such as many
leading cities in America and Europe now boast, but
we have no doubt that ultimately some big structure
of this nature will be erected where motor speed-
contests will be held, to which competitors will flock
from all parts of Australasia.— Advt.

SYDNEY.
SPORTING
NOTIONS
lime was when it seemed that the New-
market Handicap flutter would become an annual
picnic for flashy imported English horses. Sir Foote
ran away with it in 1902 after winning the curiouslv-
conditioned Futurity Stakes at Caulfield. In 1904
Playaway won the Futurity and ran third for the
flutter at Flemington; next year she won the New-
market, anti her stable companion, Lord Ullin’s
Daughter (also imported), followed her home. Since
then no imported horse has been placed in the New-
market, though four of them have captured the
Futurity. This year a very dangerous quartet of
English horses, weighted from Sst. 51b. to 9st. 21b.,
figure among the lot likely to fight it out with
Balarang down the Flemington straight. Whitefield,
llighfield and Polycrates have been backed more or
less, and Lavendo (9st. 21b.) is fairly in the market.
The Lavendo demonstration may be followed by the
backing of some deadly dark ’un in the same inter-
est, but at present it looks as though the winner of
last season’s. Caulfield Cup was a genuine stable fancy
for the coming event. Thus far his notable perform-
ances have been scored in 10- and 12-furlong races ;
lie has not been thought of as a flier that could be
expected to get away with the slickest. But before
his excellence was known to the world Lavendo did
win a couple of . six-furlong sprints from inferior
fields, and there is ijo knowing what he can’t do
when he isn’t suffering from lumbago, or whatever
else -the trouble may be that causes him to walk so
stiffly.
The Newmarket favorite Balarang is
working well. If this nag goes to the post as fit
as he was at Christmas when performing at Rand-
wiek, the reason for expecting him to be beaten will
not be' known to the writer of this paragraph. Any-
how, whatever beats Balarang will have to move at
a slick pace, for he left some very expeditious
animals astern at Randwick with remarkable ease. The
weight may trouble him, but he does not look like
a horse to be bothered much by that. The main
consideration is whether he will hop away quickly
enough. If he. neglects to do so this page may be
losing half a crown.
* * • #
The Oakleigh Plate, which happens at
Caulfield on Saturday, would have been particularly
interesting if Blue Bolt had. been having a go.
But owner Bennett relieved the V.A.T.C. of decid-
ing whether to follow the V.R.C., and scratched
his horse. There used to be a popular belief in
the ability of a smart young ’un to win the Oak-
leigh from older horses; for the 54 furlongs should
be rattled off bv a youngster that "has been able to
win a nursery handicap under heavier weight. But
this page is not so certain about the young-’un
theory this year. There are some slick old ’uns
to deal with. Much depends upon the start and
the position at the home turn. Still, on the pround
that the immature ought to do something Lost
Time may be a likely winner.
Of course, . Lingle is backed for the Austra-
lian Cup. . The son of Linacre is so handsome and
his owner is so intent on winning a big race with
him. And his weight (Bst. 31b.) is only 51b. more
than he carried into fourth place for the Melbourne
Cup. That gives Lingle a chance for the longer-
distance race. But it must be remembered that he
never showed up in the Melbourne Cup until a lot
of the horses were being eased. Lingle’s fourth place
was a good long way back, anyhow.
* * * *
The Sydney books have not been having
too good a season this year, and on Saturday at
Canterbury Park the public took coin out of the
bags after every race but one. When that race was
over there were few tickets offering. Challenge
Cross, Medley and Gai were solidly backed in a field
. .but the Linacre mare Blackacre was abso-
lutely friendless at 15’s and 20’s. A week before,
at Warwick farm, the lady bad been at the wrong
end. This time she came with a great run, and
Challenge Crosse was once more accommodated with
second money. Gai cut the contract in the last
furlong—and Mo rejoiced over a skinner. Heaven
knows some of. the tribe wanted it badly enough.
A few have given up business. Others are in a
parlous state. W hen the tote starts jingling the
casualties among the small fry will be heart-rending.
At Canterbury Park (N.S.W.) on Satur-
day the well-bred three-year-old Bernalda bounded
over six furlongs so speedily that she reached the
judge s box two lengths ahead of the next nag in
the Maiden Handicap. Bernalda had been endeavor-
ing for some considerable time to go faster than
other competitors, but until Saturday was bumping
snags. The young lddy is the pronerty of John Lee°
and when a yearling was purchased for 200 guineas.
Her dam, .Carbelle, gave to the world the good Vic-
torian animal Dame Quickly. Bernalda is in
Burton’s stable, and should be heard of again.
* * * *
Lady Isolt, a winner over six furlongs at
Tattersall’s meeting in December, who captured the
Stewards’ Mile at Canterburv Park (Sydney) on
Saturday, just got up in time to prevent ikev giving
loud, resounding cheers, for she started with the
public laying 5 to 4 on. Young Foley only "managed
to prevent the worst from happening in the last
stride. This mare may be heard of again if there
is not too much weight aboard. There is some rare
galloping blood in her. Her dam was Isolt, a
daughter of Multiform and Bellicent, the latter a
beautiful M.L. mare owned by G. G. Stead, from
Enid by Apremont-Idalia—and Idalia was the dam
of the great Sir Modred.
A speedy two-year-old won its first race
at Canterbury Park on Saturdav. This was Tatyoon
the property of Agav Wynne, one time Postmaster-
General of Australia, and W. Leonard, both of whom
live in Vic. Though there were 17 starters Tatyoon
was an odds-on favorite and careered past the judge
four lengths in front of Malt Maid, with the bit
between his teeth. Tatyoon was solidly backed in
the recent A..J.C. Phillip Stakes, but had no earthly
chance of showing his worth, owing to the dreadful
start in that event. Tatyoon is trained by Tom
Payten at Randwick, and judging by the speed dis-
played on Saturday he may be looked for in the
fighting line later on.
Racing in England is now cut down to
nothing, being allowed at Newmarket only. If this
state of affairs persists much longer it is fair to
count on the importation of numerous young horses
into Australia. A crop of yearlings will be coming-
on for which there can be nothing to do. Most of
thh two-year-olds will be eating their heads off.
And should the food shortage commence to be serious,
the consumption of oats by animals may be looked
on with a sour eye. The Australian-bred horse has
been having a hard struggle lately to keep in front
of strangers, and a few more shiploads are not likely
to make the job any easier. Of course the war has
made no difference to Australian racing yet, but
the chances that men will be rushing in to hand
over big wads for yearlings when peace arrives are
mighty remote.
* * * *
“El Gallo”: Tlie double tote has been
used by plenty more besides the Canterbury Jockey
Club, in Maoriland; but the biggest div. was paid
out by that club’s machine. This was when the
Star Rose-Captain Shannon combination won the
Cup and Stewards’ Handicap, paying over two
thousand lovely quid. Other big divs. were paid
by the Takapuna J.C. (over £900), and over
£7OO (twice) by the Auckland Racing Club.
“Trot’s” suggestion is right (B. l/2/’l7), and his
explanation of the withdrawal of the law allowing
double totes in M.L. is correct. The books obtain
a living (incidentally a fat one) in M.L. solely
through the absence of such a tote.
/‘Carfax”: A Sydney paper, disturbed by
the fact that the inside tote at Randwick is going to
have 40 pay-in windows and 30 pay-out orifices,
arrives at the conclusion that the “economic odds”
are with the machine. Of course they are, but not
because of the number of holes in it for handling
cash. There _ must be more people paying-in than
collecting dividends. If it was the other way about
racing would collapse in a day. As there are about
150 Bags receiving cash in the paddock at Randwick
on a race-day, something ought to be said now about
the “economic odds” prevailing in Israel.
* . * * *
“Merrigal”: Sam Meers, a fine tracker abd
bushman of the Martliaguy, threw in his bridle not
long ago. He was the first white born on the
Oastlereagh (N.S.W.), and was for years a partner
of that ding-dong stockman, Billy Ostler, in Dick’s
Camp, Collie, where young Sir Hercules, Warpaint
(half-brother of Trenton’s grand-dam, Flora Mclvor),
Brunswick (by Kelpie from Bouquet), and Ccerebus
fathered some of the best horses that ever carried a
shoe.
BERT SPARGO, Vic. Featherweight.
Nhill (Vic.) put up a record lately. It
had a race meeting, but no betting. Bookmakers
turned up, but refusing to pav an extra half-guinea
(making the paddock fee £1 11s. 6d..) they packed
up their props and left in a body. The sport
went on without them, but the seven events on
the programme averaged only two runners each.
Ready-money bettors unable to lose their money
were quite grumpy about it, and all opponents of the
tote went home converted.
* * * *
Herb McCoy and Englishman Llew
Edwards 'foree-athered for the fifth time on Satur-
day at Sydney Stadium. There was not much doubt
as to who was master. There is none now.
Edwards is too good for Herbert, and impressed the
tact upon him with great frequency. What hap-
pened can be boiled down to this—that the
Australian got a real good hiding. At times he
sparkled up and did well; but Edwards was after
him all the time on a pair of firmlv-planted,
resolute feet, carrying a wallop in either' fist that
always had the shoulder behind, it. Edwards
fought a hard, purposeful battle and half-way
through McCoy was badly beaten. Yet the end did
not transpire till the eighteenth clash. A left to the
body shook the Victorian to his heels and a right to
the jaw would have finished the matter if another
quick left had not followed for luck.
* * * *
The hopes of Sid Godfrey (N.S.W.) about
annexing the featherweight championship of the
Commonwealth failed to survive an argument with
Bert Spargo at' Melbourne on Saturday. Godfrev
was a worried youth all through, frequent jabs
m the pantry giving rise to a severe attack of.
pessimism. He boxed well, but not hard enough.
Spargo carried too much pep. altogether and
handed it out with either hand. They finished in
the fourteenth round, the Victorian passing ' over
one to midships. The winner, who appears to fight
better in Melbourne than in Sydnev, can now claim
the championship and hang on to'it until someone
tears it away, leaving only memory and bruises
behind.
* * * *
Freddy Enck, of America, pursued a
“watchful waiting” policy for 12 rounds of his
scrap with Hector Melville 'at Brisbane on Satur-
day He took every variety of wallop from Hector
without turning a hair. After this nonsense had
proceeded far enough, Frederic adopted vigorous
measures and settled his opponent in three more
rounds. The crowd, cheerfully inconsistent, had
been hurling jests at the imported article which
it had crowded to see. It reckoned he didn’t know
anything about fighting. Afterwards it pushed off
quickly before anyone found out it had been making
a fool of itself.
* • * . ' * *
There was a whiff of olden days in the
labors of Hansen Carter for Waverley against Sydney
University on Saturday. With the bat he smote 53
(run out) of a total of 113, and behind the sticks
made a catch in addition to permitting only one
Varsity’s 165. Carter wore the gloves with
the 02 and 'O9 Australian Xl’s in England, but
dropped out of “big” cricket when the Board of
Control undertook the management of over-sea
journeyings. The form of many .youthful stick-
keepers has evidently given Carter a prod.
Clarrie Hogue, who did the hat-trick
on Saturday by clean bowling three Middle Harbor
wickets m the first-grade match against North
Sydney, is a son of Jimmy, ex-Minister for Educa-
tion in N. S. Wales. Two brothers are at the
Dront, one being “Trooper Bluegum,” writer of
much good war stuff.
* * * *
“Out” in Melbourne, at the age of 68,
Frank Allan, “the bowler of a century.” Until a
lew months back he bossed the Vermin Destruction
Branch of the Vic. Lands Department'; but cricket
was always his chief interest. Twenty years ago
he had to give up playing the game and take to
bowls, but up to the end he talked, thought,
dreamed and wrote about cricket. He was in the
first Australian Eleven, which went to England
in 18(8. In a match against Noo South next
year he took eight wickets for 20 runs. Grace
reckoned that Allan was one of the best bowlers
he had ever stood up to. In inter-State matches
Allan took /3 wickets at an average cost of 10.26
™ ns > °h e °f his most remarkable exploits being
the bowling of seven successive maidens to H. 11.
Massie, who was among the Ma State’s finest bats-
men. Harry, one of Frank Allan’s sons, has a, Mel-
bourne reputation as a cricketer and baseballer.
* * * *
“Bails”: Frank Allan, “the bowler of a
century,” was a native of Allansford, near Warr-
nanibool (Vic.), and as a boy was much with the
aboriginals on or near his father’s squattage. From
them he learned to throw the boomerang, and to
that fact he always attributed the devil in his bowl-
ing.
* * * *
Hundred or So” : Allan bowled the first
ball in that famous match against the M.C.0.;
and as the betting was 4 to 1 against the
colonials, ’ a loud laugh struck the atmosphere
when Grace smacked him away for 4. When the
next delivery from Allan started the multitude
prepared to laugh some more. But when the ball
fell where Alec Bannerman’s hands happened to
be, the 'grins were promptly removed. Spof. and
Boyle completed the massacre.
* * * *
The cricket ball used in the great M.C.C
match of ’7B, after capturing £7OO for patriotic
funds, has been accepted by the trustees of the
Melbourne National Gallery after a severe mental
struggle. The trustees can only accept gifts of
historical interest” or works of art, and they
have persuaded themselves that the piece of leather
possesses “historical interest.” Before the ’7B
match at Lords English critics made humorous
forecasts about the size of the drubbing the cheeky
colonials were, going to endure. The wicket was
affected by rain and the four innings were over in
a day. At the last knock, the M.C.C. had 23 to
get to tie. Just-dead Frank Allan disposed of
Grace for four, and Spofforth and Boyle had dis-
missed the rest when the score showed 19. Aus-
tralian cricket became famous from that day.
Blackham pounced on the ball which bowled the
last Englishman and has kept it nearly 40 years.
Now it becomes a National Relic.
The famous ’7B team which astonished
England by defeating a picked English team at
Lords in one day consisted of Dave Gregory, two
Bannermans, Spofforth, Garrett and Murdoch
(N.S.W.), Allan (who died last week), Bovle,
Blackham, Bailey, Horan and Midwinter (Via)-
P. R. Le Couteur, in making 133 for
Melbourne University against East whilst on vaca-
tion from Perth lately, showed some of the cricket
which helped to win him the Rhodes scholarship
for Vic. in 1908. Le Couteur was undoubtedly
the most successful to date of all Vic. Rhodes
scholars in Fogland as far as cricket is con-
cerned. He. quickly caught the selectors’ eve at
Oxford, scoring 50 for that Uni. against Surrey.
In 1910, established in the Oxford v. Cambridge
match, he was responsible for the best all-round
performance ever recorded at this fixture, knocking
up 160 in under 31 hours and capturing six
wickets for 20 and five for 46 runs. For Gentle-
men against Players he headed both batting and
bowling figures for the match, scored 73 runs in
65 minutes against M.C.C. at Lords, and cap-
tured 10 Kent wickets for 42. A batsmen with a
hard drive and a late cut and a “googlie” bowler,
Le. Couteur was knocking hard on the inter-State
cricket door when he left for England.
* * # *
“F.M.C.”: Tom Sullivan and his son (a
fine fellow 6ft. high and weighing 13st.) are cer-
tainly prisoners in Germany, as mentioned by The
Bulletin, and, as I know them both well, I’ll tell
you how it came about. Tom, sen., kept a pub
at Putney (London) and, incidentally, trained oars-
men, including Barry. He gave that up and with
young Tom went to Germany to teach athletics.
They were there when the war-dogs were slipped,
and of course were interned. Mrs. Sullivan, who was
with her husband, was subsequently released.
This year the old sports who ran the
Anniversary Regatta on Sydney Harbor had a
special object, in keeping the fixture going.
Through the sinking of some quaint old waterside
annual in England, the Anniversary frolic is now
the hoariest aquatic event in the world, and those
in charge thought it worth a bit to keep the dis-
tinction.

“R.S.”: Tickling trout is an offence in
the eyes of the law, but it is mild sport compared
with jagging,” for which a barbarian was fined
on two charges at the Murchison (Vic.)
Police Court the other day. This vandal had
been fined before for illegal methods at the Wav-
anga Outlet, where fine fish abound. Jagging con-
sists of casting out a line to the end of which a
bunch of largish hooks has been attached. By
drawing in rapidly any fish in close proximity will
be j'agged in more ways than one. It is the
injury done, to fish that are not caught that
makes, the jagger worse than the dynamiter and
fish-poisoner, and he is just as hard to detect. lie
generally carries on his nefarious work in the
close season, too.
* * * *
There was much grumbling this year
amongst the “sports” who turned out to slaughter
black swans at Moulting Lagoon, near Swansea (Tas.),
on Feb. 1, the opening day of the season. Ninety
guns killed only 110 amongst them instead of
between 400 and 500 as usual. It was too dull a
shambles alogether.
* * •* *
The duck-butchery season opens in N. S.
Wales a fortnight earlier than in the more civil-
ised Southern State. The Ma State gunners have a
further pull on the Victorian, as they, can shoot
till the available black-duck species is wiped out.
Under the Vic. law the slaughterman’s bag is
limited to 30 birds, or a few more if it’s quail
or smaller feathered targets. The wisdom of
restricting the number of victims to eacli gunner
is again shown by recent “bags” at Eehuca, on the
N.S.W. and Vie. border. One party got 470 birds.
This waste of good poultry is called “sport.”
* * *
“Artists’ Corps”: The walk of the London
Scottish from London to Brighton (B. 25/l/’l7) at
first sight doesn’t seem much of a performance. • But
the 100 kilties travelled in full-kit marching order
and were the only crowd that kept their numbers
right through. With the “Artists,” I attempted the
same feat, but we lost 20 per cent, of our strength.
Hands Cross Hill settled many and the hill at Craw-
ley others. The London Scottish have long been
reputed the most athletic body of volunteers in the
Empire, and kept their end up in the Olympic
Games last held in England. And when it came
to fighting they performed like champions again.
* * * *
The Vic. Amateur Athletic Assoc, has
planked both feet down and will not sanction any
event until the big contest on the West Front is
decided. Meantime tlie artful pro. is chasing the
quids at Gurney’s running-grounds at Flemington,
where Ikey Mo yells the. odds and the schemers can
catch 50 of the best in a heat. Professional • peds
are better off to-day than they have been for many
years. But the sport is entirely dependent on the
betting, and the moment Ikey is stopped the game
or whatever it is will give up the ghost.
* * * *
Ford, maker of motor-cars and instigator
of the peace mission to Europe, was the first man
on the doormat of the U.S. War Offce with an offer
of the use of his factories, without profit, for war
work. Being in the habit of turning out mechanical
cockroaches by the million, he had a large-sized sug-
gestion to make. He declared that he was prepared
to turn out submersibles 20 feet long, and capable
of being operated by one man, at the rate of a
thousand a day. Apparently Ford has altered his
opinion about the best way to speed up Peace.

Grey Hair.
Lady Gives Simple Home Recipe That
She Used to Darken Her Grey Hair.
For years I tried to restore my grey liair
to its natural color with the prepared dyes
and stains, but none of them gave satisfac-
tion and they were all expensive. I finally
came across a simple recipe which I mixed
at home that gives wonderful results. I
gave the recipe, which is as follows, to a
number of my friends, and they are all
delighted with it. To 7oz. of water add a
small box of Orlex Compound and loz. of
bay rum. These ingredients can be bought
at any chemist’s at very little cost. IJse
every other day until the hair becomes the
required shade. It will not only darken
the grey hair but removes dandruff and acts
as a tonic to the hair. It is not sticky or

The reserves are made up this way: Insur-
ance and repairs fund, etc., £(>S.33l (£4098
added during tlie year) ; equalisation of
dividend fund, £13,478 ; balance available for
equalisation fund, £5lO. It is impossible
to say whether all or any of the money
added to the insurance and repairs fund is
true profit. Probably the management hopes
that a good deal of it will be : it depends to
some extent on the luck the co. has with
whatever "risks the insurance fund covers.
For some years past the luck appears to
have been good. Take as a basis this
comparison :
1905. 1915. 1916.
Capital .. ..£159,228 £300,000 £300,000
Liabilities .. .. 33,356 28,631 20,626
BP,ips . . . . 184,730 248,979 232,170
Wharves, works, etc. 25,765 26,809 26,868
Cash and hook debts 32,435 79,564 67,261
Sundry assets .. . 7,871 64,491 . 90,152
In those 11 years the co. has got in
£.140,772 of capital, and it now has £l3O 293
more assets (net) apart from ships and
wharves and works. How much it has actu-
ally put into shins, wharves, etc., this paper
cannot say. The assets are now earning
an average of about £26,000 a year as
against an average of about £16,000 10 or
12 years ago, and that looks as though the
additions have been very substantial. The
“other assets” mentioned in the table con-
sist partly of coal and stores but mostly of
unspecified items (£<1,772) ; and that
doesn’t give investors much enlightenment.
All that one can say for certain is that,
after providing for all additions to fleet,
wharves and works, in 11 years £130,293
net assets have been accumulated. Ifi other
words, though during that period £140,772
of new capital lias been got in, all but
£30,479 of the added book value of the ships,
wharves, etc., has been provided out of
profits in excess of the dividend. And pro-
spects are good; for, though expenses are
much higher, there is perhaps no part of
N. _B. Wales where the settlers are more
solidly prosperous and the increases more
easily passed on.

This co. continues to turn out the light when
the time comes to show its figures. Its
I’rofit and Loss account is a mere statement
that the total revenue is so much and the
total outgoings, including “manufacture, dis-
tribution of gas, rents, rates and taxes,
renewals, reserve, management, etc.,” so
much. Under the Gas Act passed three or
foui' years ago two or three N. S. Wales c-os.
must present their accounts in a prescribed
form. For some obscure reason or no reason
at all this one was exempted. Hence it may
he-stuffing profit away, employing it in pay-
ing for extensions, or, on the other hand, it
may be writing off too little to cover depre-
ciation. In the one case its profits would
be understated and in the other overstated.
It says itself that the coal strike and a new
wages award have cut down profits so that
“the directors have no alternative but to
reduce the dividend for the six months to
December 31 to 3 p.c.,” making. 7 p.c. for
the year. But as some internal reserves
were capitalised a few years ago (that is to
say, some profits which had, not been dis-
closed) the return is still really more than
7 p.c.—about 8i- p.c., in fact. So far as the
balance-sheet figures are worth anything,
here is their tale for 11 years:—
1905. 1910. 1915. 1916.
Capital.. ..£15,000 £37,475 £63,750 £63,750
Liabilities . . 4,141 5,680 17,924 21,370
Fixed assets . . 19,598 44,094 82,359 87,941
Other assets . . 1,272 4,346 4,746 3 J 36
Sales of gas, etc. 5,246 11,956 23,193 24,221
Since 1913 the income has increased by
about one-third; and it will, on present
appearances, continue to grow, for the co.’s
field is a large and surely progressive one.
The liabilities are mostly what are described
as “loan capital,” which is as vague as the
rest of these totally unsatisfactory accounts.
* * *
Round Jlill (Tas.) has to make a call of
sixpence to finish installing the machinery.
The original estimate was £10,500; already
about £13,000 has been spent. The late
floods burst the dam and washed an engine
which was being carted to the mine into'the
bed of the creek. It all means a delay of a
couple of month’s.
From a digger at Lakekamu, in the
innards of Papua :
There is a certain, amount of osmiridium in Lake-
kamu gold; and although there is a common idea
that it is twice-as valuable as gold itself, we get
nothing for it, whereas we do get returns for any
silver contents. There seems something wrong here.
“Platman” : The N. S. Wales Mines Dept,
could with advantage follow the example of
Queensland and publish a monthly Mining
.Tournat, for the guidance of prospectors and
the public generally. It is only when one
crosses the border that one realises how
valuable the Queensland Journal is.

Don’t Have Grey Hair.
A simple, old-fashioned, home-made recipe will
make the greyness disappear.
G-rey hair is often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often downright injurious. Few people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily
made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a natural color in a perfectly harmless
manner. You have only to get two ounces
of tammalite concentrate from your chemist
and mix it with three ounces of bay rum to
prove this. Apply this simple and harmless
lotion for a few nights to the hair with a
small sponge and the greyness will gradually
disappear. The lotion is neither sticky nor
greasy, and has been proved over and over
again for generations past by those in posses-
sion of the formula.— Advt.

THE SECRET OF A
GOOD COMPLEXION.
By MME. ST. CLAIRE.
Any woman nowadays may quickly reju-
venate her complexion at home by a safe and
painless process of gentle absorption. The
days of expensive and dangerous “operations”
are past, and each woman can be her own
beauty doctor. It has been discovered that
ordinary mercolised wax. applied like cold
cream, at night, will cause the thin veil of
lifeless, sallow or blotchy cuticle to gradually
and gently flake off in invisible, flour-like
particles, revealing the fresh, vigorous and
beautiful young skin underneath. Mercolised
wax absorbs only the unhealthy and un-
sightly outer film, and does not affect the
healthy tissue at all.
The process defies detection, and unveils
a lasting and beautiful complexion, which *««,
of course, a perfectly natural one. For tnis
purpose thousands of women are now using
mercolised wax, which may be obtained at
any chemist's. Naturally the process also
removes all such surface blemishes as
freckles, blotches, pimples, tan, liver spots,
etc. It makes the face look years younger
and prettier, and renders the use of face
creams unnecessary.
Another valuable piece of information,
which many women will appreciate, concerns
the success of the new treatment for super-
fluous hair. A remedy for permanently erad-
icating this ugly form of facial disfigurement
now seems to have been found. It is simpli-
city itself. About one ounce of ordinary pure
powdered pheminol, which can be obtained
at any chemist’s, is all that is heeded to put
one in the way of completely ridding oneself
of superfluous hair. When applied to the
obsoxious growth this causes it to immedi-
ately disappear, and the continuance of the
recommended treatment is said to finally
destroy the hair roots, so that there will be
no renewal of the growth. 15

“Murltah”: Why can't the N. S. Wales
railways light their carriages with electricity
instead of the dim, leaking, stinking, flicker-
ing gas? With electricity the first cost, of
installation, is practically the last. The
motive power is got for nothing, a belt from
one of the carriage axles being used to run a
small dynamo. The current from this
charges a set of accumulators, from which a
steady, constant light is obtained. The only
attention then needed is a drop of distilled
Av..ter in the accumulators every month or
so, and an occasional squirt of oil on the
bearings of the dynamo. Further, the
current can also be used to whirl fans, a
fragment of civilisation not yet known in
the Ma State. If some improvement isn’t
made soon I'm going to. buy a storm-lantern
and hang it on the hatrack.

For punctures — ; self-cure patches and patching solution for
quick and easy repairs.
For blow-outs —inside and outside patches to enable you to
get home without running Hat.
For tread cuts —tyre putty, to prevent sand and water from
entering and cutting and rotting the fabric.
For tubes —French talc to dust them with before placing in
the casing, and new Valve insides for replacements.
For general utility purposes, a 2oz. roll of insulated b riction
Repair T ape.
And last, but most important of all, a tyre tester, to tell you
when you have the proper inflation.
Motorists are rapidly learning that a small sum put into a
Goodyear Tyre-Saver Kit proves a profitable investment.
In it is everything needed to restore to running condition a
tyre that has suffered a puncture or blow-out.
With this kit in your car you need never run on a flat tyre,
and damage casing and. tube beyond repair.
It prolongs the life of your tyres. It saves you time, trouble
and money.
Get a Goodyear Tyre-Saver Kit the next time you stop) for
petrol. It will help) you to get longer mileage from
your tyres no matter of what make.

IN
BANANA LAND
A deputation to Minister Hardacre the
other day protested vehemently against the
use of patriotic lessons and poems in the
School Paper; also against the frequent
eulogistic references to the navy, Victoria
Cross winners, etc. One deputationist said
he would, prefer to see the leaders of
the industrial movement praisefully men-
tioned ; another would rather see a child
raise its hat to the memory of the recently-
dead Labor worker Mrs. Miller than to
the memory of Nelson or Wellington. These
small-minded Laborites can never see any
farther than the dust-tin in their own back-
yards—where, by the way, all Labor-leaders
are eventually cast.
“C.G.0.”: The absurd practice of drag-
ging all products to one congested port has
found a new supporter in the Hughes Gov-
ernment, which, under the wool-appraise-
ment scheme, insists that all wool for the
Imperial Government must be shipped from
the capital cities of the various States. This
prevents Rockhampton from receiving the
Central Queensland clip, whilst the extra
haulage and inconvenience is estimated to
represent a loss of approximately £40,000
to the growers. The Rockhampton Cham-
ber of Commerce protested to the Federal
authorities the other day, and the astound-
ing reply came that “as all the other States
have to ship their wool from their capital
cities, no exception can be made in the case
of Queensland.” A brilliant piece of logic
from a Government that is busy exhorting
everybody to practise economy.
A vote of the Bananaland caucus has
given Bill Hamilton the job of President of
the Leg. Council in succession to Arthur
Morgan, deceased. Hamilton has been
representative of the Government in the
House ever since the Ryan party took office,
and liis job has not been a rosy one. Ilis
new billet, however, carries a nice little
screw of £lOOO a ‘year and is the softest snap
in Queensland politics. Alf .Tones, the
young Maryborough representative, steps
into Hamilton’s shoes in the Cabinet and
in the Council. He has won on sheer merit,
and nobody begrudges him the lift.
The area selected in Queensland in 1915
amounted to 7,295,085 acres; in 1910,
4J897.259 acres—a falling-off of 2,398,420
acres. That is an indication of how hard
the man on the land is being hit. While
pundits are advising cultivators to go in
for sisal- and cotton-growing, the sugar in-
dustry is practically paralysed, and in some
districts is in danger of being wiped out
altogether. Apparently Bananaland can't
hold on to the primary industries she
already possesses—to say nothing of start-
ing new ones.
“Ah Ivee” : A further instance of animal
sagacity. During the Clermont flood a
horse waded past the Salvation Army Bar-
racks and Olsson’s two-storeyed cordial fac-
tory-—both subsequently swept away—
crossed the street and entered the
Grand Hotel. The ground floor being
flooded, he walked of his own accord half-
way up the stairs. Being then encouraged
by the refugees in the hotel, he continued
to the top, entered one of the rooms, and
remained there quietly until danger was
past, when lie was helped down with ropes.
UNNATURAL BEHAVIOR.
HINNESSY : “Phwat’s up wid Regan that lie don't spane wid you now?”
H OGAN: "Sure, ’tis a bit of a coolness between us since Oi dropped half a ton av
potatoes on the top av him. (Reflectively) He's a quare feller, is Regan."
“Clario” : Concerning the invasion of
Bananaland by foreigners. Anybody desiring
to become a linguist should go to some of
the mining centres near Gloncurry. Every
nationality is arriving in bunches ; and very-
few can speak enough English to make them-
selves understood. There is an element of
danger in employing many of these men
underground; every order elicits the one
reply from most of them —everything is “All
righto.” “One mine one language” threatens
to become the slogan of the miners’ unions
before long.
“F. 8.”; “Boro-Belle” (B. 18/1/T7)
remarks that Bananaland wastes immense
quantities of two products—molasses and
megass. Let me tell “Boro-Belle” that in
Bananaland we do nothing by halves, and
that, instead of contenting ourselves with
wasting two paltry by-products, we are at
present throwing away the whole sugar in-
dustry. Ask Mackay, Bundaberg and the
Herbert.
A man who was found drowned in a stock-
watering trough near Brisbane the other
day was really too considerate a gentleman
to be wasted. lie left a note of apology ;
he was sorry to use the trough, but it needed
cleaning in any case, he said, so the little
addition of his corpse didn’t matter.
“Dry Shave”: The idea that goods that
have been bumped about a lot in a long
journey are better than the local article
seems to have got even a firmer hold on
Queensland than on the South. And when
the northerner patronises the Australian
product, lie insists on a Southern label. He
drags his beer, his boots, his biscuits and
every other thing from as far away as prac-
ticable, and assumes an air of profundity
as he tells you they are better than they
make down at ’Gabba. There’s a manufacturer
in Queensland turning out marmalade for a
Sydney firm which puts it on the market
under its own label. The same marmalade
is sent out under the maker’s name as well,
and is priced in Brisbane at twopence less
than its twin’ brother. Mrs, Brisbane won’t
have “that local rubbish,” preferring to pay
the extra 2d. for an imaginary sea-trip. It’s
mostly the manufacturers’ fault, of course.
But they may shake themselves one of these
days, and give their goods a push. They
may even tell people they make goods.
Meanwhile they are content to let the re-
tailer do the pushing.
Independent investigations into the gasti-o-
intestinal disease known as sprue haw been
made at the Institute of Tropical Medicine
at Townsville. The results are embodied in
a paper by Brienl and Priestley, published
in the Medical Journal of Australia.
This disease is endemic in the coastal dis-
tricts from Aiaekay to Cairns, and is preva-
lent in China, .lava, India, Ceylon and Porto
Rico. It still baffles the investigators, and
the treatment is more or less empirical. Its
causation cannot be fixed, but it seems to be
fairly well established that anyone who
develops it is better in bed in a hospital
than anywhere else. Incidentally, efforts to
reproduce disease analogous to sprue in
laboratory animals have not been successful.
The Chap Who Stays at ’Ome.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“If tlie staff of officials waiting at home broke
down what would become of those actually at the
Front?”- — Assistant Minister for Defence Smith at a
picnic of Defence Department soldiers.
I? the chap as stays at ’ome broke down
I pity them as battle in the trenches;
There’d be trouble in the mud
An’ a dfefful loss o’ blood
An’ a-weepin’ with the sweethearts an’the wenches ;
There’d be riot ’ere for certain in the town
If the chap as stays at ’ome broke down.
If the chap as stays at ’oine broke down
, The news ’d straight be cabled to the Kaiser ;
He’d say to Fallcenhayn:
“Cheer up, me boy, again,
I guess tliis news ’ll come as a surpriser;
There’ll be Anzaes on the rampage an’ Hie frown
When they know the chap at ’ome’s broke down.”
It' the chap as stays at ’ome breaks down
Cord ’elp the coves a-fightm’ in the Lowland.
Fer, see, ’twas only scare
As made us send ’em there —
We didn’t want to be. the same as Poland;
The prospects of the Anzaes would be brown
It the chap as stays at ’ome broke down.
If the chap as stays at ’ome broke down
The girls would all be dumpy on the Sunday;
The picnics would be off,
The gutter-press would scoff,
An’ who would measure ribbon on the Monday ?
Yes, who would face the counter an’ the gown
if the chap as stays at ’ome broke down ?
If the chap as stays at ’ome broke down
Yer might as well shut up yev mad recruitin’;
Fer if we wasn't here
To spur ’em on an’ cheer
They’d never go to face the bloomin’ shootin’ ;
You’d better go (ari take yer dog) to drown
If the c-hap as stays at ’ome breaks down.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 10, 1917.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
This week we laid down our knitting-
needles to give the swimming girls from the
South a good time before they return to
their needles. The official welcome was, of
course, the business of the Q.L.A.S.A. Then
the Commercial Ladies’ Club gave them a
night out, which was followed by an evening
on the river with the Valley Cliib girls. On
Wednesday they were motored to the pine-
apple farms at Nudgee. and next afternoon
“Mick" Ivirwin, M.L.A., came to the rescue,
and benzined them up to One Tree Hill, after-
Mrs. T. J. Ryan had ballasted them with
tea and crumpets at Parliament House.
Mesdames E. G. Theodore, W. Lennon,
Hardacre and W. Hamilton assisted in tin-
good work at this last-mentioned soiree.
The tea-tables were set on the cool, wide
balcony from which opens a lovely view of
blue hills and sparkling river.
Lieut. Malcolm McGliie, of ours, has just
won the Military Cross. Ilis brother, Lieut.
Norman McGliie, once associate to Griffith.
C.,T., is on his way back severely wounded.
Another bird for the Flying Corps is Fred
Small, who joined the 9th Battalion as
a private and left Gallipoli, wounded, as a
captain, after seven months’ of Abdul. On
reaching Australia lie was not convinced
when several military doctors told him that
he was unfit for further service. So he
floated back to London and pushed into the
Flying Corps. Capt. Trouton, a well-
known Sydneysider, is this Anzac’s grandpa
on the maternal side.
A Bananaland Light Horseman some-
where west of Suez wrote home recently
that some brasshat had decided that the
L.H. needed scouring. So all clothing,
blankets and lace ruffles were steamed for
a couple of hours. That settled them. Then
the troopers were lined up and drafted
through a sort of slieep-dip. And that
nearly settled them, for the acid was so
strong that some of them emerged half-
skinned.
Friday was another day out for the badge-
sellers. The Red Gross Society made a suit-
able pirate crew, and the plunder went to
the Clermont Relief Fund.
Dr. Arthur, M.L.A. of your State, is in
town. I don’t know what his fad is for the
moment, but if it’s more daylight saving
you’ll never see him again this side of
Jordan.
■ On Tuesday afternoon the Coo-ee Cafe
was opened, and is being run by the Com-
forts Committees of the sth L.H. Regiment,
the 9th and 49tli Battalions, and the 9th
Artillery Brigade. Brisk business.
The other week I told you of the death of
Mrs. Jordan, of Manar station. Well, some-
body else tells me that she was not once a
Mrs. Deuchar; since marriage she was
always a Jordan. But she was a niece of
old pioneer Deuchar. Incidentally, the lat-
ter’s widow is still alive and hearty in
Brisbane at the age of 80. She is also widow
of late Telegraph Superintendent Wilson.
Mrs. Charles Campbell Riley, who died
of typhoid in Toowoomba lately, was one
of three beautiful sisters of Mr. “Coogee”
Walsh, manager for Dalgety and Co. in
Brisbane. Leila, the eldest, widow of the
late Dr. Huxtable, resides with her family
at Chatswood, North Sydney; Hilda,' the
youngest of the trio, is the wife of a doctor
in Toowoomba. “Charlie” Campbell Riley,
who married Kathleen, belongs to a very
well-known family in N. S. Wales. His
cousin, the late W. R. Campbell, owned
Warialda, a station on the Gwydir, which
electorate he represented for a considerable
period. His younger brother, Colonel Sel-
wyn Campbell, the owner of Cavan station
in N. S. Wales, was A.D.C. to John Madden
during several terms of Acting-Governor-
ship. Campbell Riley was the only son of
Charles Riley, barrister-at-law in Sydney
in the early days, and first Master in Equity
in X. S. Wales.
Tours affectionately,

REMARKABLE
NEW DISCOVERY
Grey Hair
Naturally Restored by
Simple, Harmless Method.
It is a Great Specific for Grey, Faded and
Bleached Hair, which fully satisfies many
thousands. It will satisfy you. Try it once
and be satisfied of its absolute efficacy.
m
\T,
NO DYES OR STAINS.
MY GENUINE SPECIFIC IS NOT A DYE OR.
STAIN. It is almost as clear as water, in fact,
many persons have remarked that it is truly won-
derful to think that a preparation that does not
even stain a sheet of white paper will restove grey
hair to its natural color. It is a great mistake to
apply a preparation such as an ordinary dye that
wall darken the hair immediately. No self-respect-
ing person would think of adopting such a method ,
first, because the sudden change attracts attention
and ridicule on the part of acquaintances. Second,
because the coloring is almost exactly the same as
if ordinary ink were used, and the active chemical
agents in' such dyes are nearly the same as are
employed to give the color and body to writing inks.
Third, the stain is only very temporary and it
cannot be evenlv applied, therefore the darkening
is in patches. Moreover, the scalp is likely to be-
come discolored. Fourth, these dyes injure the hair
shafts, and cause the hair to gradually fall away.
Fifth, the dark color is not natural, it looks as if
the person had applied writing fluid or stove polish.
Sixth, when the hair is moist through perspiration
or wetting the color will run. Imagine the horror
of blacking chair backs, pillows, etc.
A SIMPLE EXPERIMENT.
Take a post-card and put it over the right
side of the line through the centre of the
face. The left side shows a man looking
between 50 and GO years of age. Now cover
the left side and note the remarkable trans-
formation. It shows a man of between 25
and 35 and not a day older.
I AM A WOMAN who belongs to a prematurely
grey-haired family. My father was grey before he
was 30, and two of my brothers as well as- my sister
became grey early in life. Before the age of 25 I
developed grey hairs, which became quite embarras-
sing to me socially and also in business. I LOST
TIIHEE GOOD POSITIONS for no other reason than
my fading hair. The greyness increased with alarm-
ing rapidity, and at 28 1 LOOKED LIKE A WOMAN'
OF -15. One day I was seriously thinking of buying
one of the many. Grey Hair Restorers that are
extensively advertised, when I casually mentioned
the subject- to an acquaintance—a scientific man
and, as I afterwards learnt, a great student of
the Chemistry of the Hair. He strongly advised,
me to have nothing whatever to do with these con-
coctions. He pointed out that SUCH PREPARA-
TIONS EVENTUALLY RUINED THE HAIR, and in
the meantime rarely deceived even the casual observer.
He suggested that I try' a formula that he had per-
fected after a considerable time, which, by a cer-
tain chemical action on the pigmentary coloring
cells restored the hair to its normal color. In reply
to my inquiries, he assured me that the formula
was not only absolutely harmless, and would develop
A STRONG'AKD BEAUTIFUL GROWTH OF IIAIII.
My friend wrote out his prescription, which I hail
made up, and after carefully. following- the instruc-

“Trot” : Henry Bracy spent a lot of time
dressing himself. He was always the mum-
mer, and the varnish, "though thin, lasted
well. One day in Chchurch (M.L.), where
Henry was taking the sparkling eyes, some-
one arranged a trip on the river. Henry
turned up at the boatshed brand-new from
head to foot —flannels, straw hat and every-
thing down to a nautical air. After he had
grouped himself as a decorative effect at
the bow, some of the girls toyed with the
oars and George Lauri signed on as cox-
swain. So the skiff drifted down the stream,
like a painted boat on a painted stage.
Thereabouts the willows weep gracefully
over the water, and Lauri’s seamanship was
put to a severe test. Bounding a bend
George ran the craft under a trailing bough,
and one of the branches, dragged from the
water, flicked Henry’s hat and sprinkled
his blazer. Shudderingly he wiped the
offending spots away, crouched down in the
bottom of the boat and turned a pained
expression aft. “George,” he said, “you
have spoiled the whole day.” And George
had, too. Bracy was crushed. He sang
that night with a wounded soul.
“Harrison O.”: After six years of life
Melbourne Repertory Theatre is in danger
of becoming comatose. Performances which
Avere .to have been given in December had
to be abandoned, and director Gregan
McMahon, seeing no chance of doing any-
thing in the repertory line for 12 months
at least, contemplates returning to the
commercial theatre for that period. If this
happens, all lovers of intelligent drama will
have cause for regret. Had the Repertory
Theatre received support from those to
whom it Avas entitled to look for it, its
affairs Avould not be in their present unsatis-
factory state. When the theatre started
400 citizens who yearned for some-
thing better than the prevailing theatrical
tripe guaranteed McMahon a guinea a year
each, Avhich enabled him to go,ahead. Only
a few of the original subscribers remain,
although the total number has somewhat
increased. But the majority now pay their
guineas for the privilege of belonging to the
Repertory Club, which Avas not in existence
at tho> start, and which, although it devotes
occasional evenipgs to lectures on the drama
by university and other highbrows, is largely
an afternoon-tea and twaddle concern. Once
the theatre could rely on the attendance of
a large number of subscribers and their best
girls, but lately only about 20 per cent,
have tjirned up, so that there has been a
stpady shrinkage in the box-office receipts;
for many of the 400 members of the Club
belong to the corseted sex and are no more
interested in literary drama than coavs are
in theology. The original purpose of the
theatre has been lost sight of, and the Club
is like half a dozen highly decorous social
institutions of the kind. Unless the reper-
tory movement is to be sacrificed to tea
and scones, it is up to supporters of literary
drama to get a move on.
“Sardonyx” : The crux of the “gag” ques-
tion appears to be that Avhile interpolation
may sometimes considerably help the “go” of
a piece, it may, on the other hand, be mere
concession to the bad taste of those Avho can
appreciate only what Ave know as funny-
business. Cibber’s “Richard III.” is cited
by “0.K.” (B. l/2/’l7) as still holding the
stage; but Cibber, notwithstanding the
sneers of Pope, had a fine dramatic instinct
and a keen sense of stage effect. He is his
own justification, which is more than can
be said on behalf of Wilkie, Avho defends his
interpolations by saying that he doesn’t
cater for dryasdusts. "Nature’s getting on!”
gibed Whistler, when somebody said that a
painting of his was “almost Nature.” Gold-
smith, too, must be getting on Avhen he can
be mangled into popularity.
“Van Eck”: Latest Australian to use a
dressing-room on Broad Avay is Dorothy
Gumming. On her arrival in the land of
the wooden nutmeg she Avas engaged to
play opposite James Power and fill in a
part for a Famous Players Co. film.
Ada Guildford, Avho used to be Charles
Holloway’s leading lady, and more lately
served under the blood-red banner of Bilan-
derson, has left for the star-spangled repub-
lic to try to make dollars by grease-painting.
Violet Paget (Mrs. Hugh Buckler) is now
in London, where sh% elects to be known as
“tjie popular " Australian actress.” Another
indication that this country’s name counts
for something these times. When she came
here some years back, with “The Night of
the Party,” she was “the talented English
actress.”
“M.P.A.”: Gaston Mervale is said (B.
18/1/T7) to be a lover of the chaotic. This
can be substantiated without the slightest
difficulty. Several years ago, when Mervale
was movie-making near Sydney, he had a
bush-homestead incident to depict. He
carted a bunch of people out into the hills
at the back of Manly, in a property stage-
coach, and planted them down at a little
wooden cottage occupied by a nice old lady
and her family. When the movie people
detached themselves from the coach they
couldn’t see-any dressing tents; so they bore
down on Mervale Avith : “Where do Ave dress,
Mr. Mervale?” The unhappy Mervale, Avho
didn’t knoAV, waited on the little lady of the
cottage and got her to AvitlidraAV from part
of her house and allow the ladies to use it.
The little lady was next asked to allow the
gentlemen to dress; eventually, the party
taking complete possession, the family Avent
out and sat on boxes. When the picturing
commenced the furniture of the little home
was soon all in use as different “props” were
required. Mervale thundered out directions
and rushed about at speed limit. His
thunderings drove the curate away. At last
the little lady became so overpowered that
she decided to order the Avhole shoAV off the
premises. It was her first experience of
pros. “Oh, what a terrible man!” she
moaned. Godfrey Cass, Avho Avas standing
near, saved the situation. “Ah, madam,”
Cass said, “he’s a terrible man, but awfully
good to his mother.” “Well, sir, if he’s good
to his mother I can forgive him all.” she
replied, and made tea for the entire show.

Business, Robbery, etc
“Viator": Innocents ask, “Why plant
potatoes in English parksV” Because the
spud is no traveller. The refrigerator can’t
persuade Murphy to keep. Queen Alexandra
once received a lovely hunch of fresh roses
in ice from Maoriland; and poultry, eggs,
peaches and everything else that can be
suggested may be transported across the
world. But the spud won’t travel in a state
of suspended deadness for anybody.
“Fax”: A Melbourne Solicitor (and barris-
ter) lately charged his clerk with embezzle-
ment. Before thv case was heard the coun-
sel for the lawyer-prosecutor asked permis-
sion to withdraw the charge on the ground
that there was “nothing to sustain it.” The
lawyer-prosecutor admitted in evidence that
he paid bis clerk £1 a week. Finally the
Bench decided that accused had done no
more than he was entitled to do, and the
charge of embezzlement could not have been
the right charge, anyhow, and there was no
case. So the learned Gent. One, who sells
legal advice for a living, had brought his
own £l-a-week clerk into a police court on a
charge which, having nothing to sustain it,
was contemptuously blown out. Altogether
what a reflection on the privileged legal
brotherhood! And what a warning to the
public that lives in danger of being inveigled
into legal difficulties !
* * * *
“D.F.”: You never know your luck.
Forty or 50 years ago a citizen of Bagdad
(Tas.) had a bit of poor, stony, unprofitable
ground in a gully. A convenient cloudburst
came along and the resulting flood brought
down a fine lot of fat alluvial and covered
his patch with it, and ever since he has been
growing fine crops of vegetables. But a
still bigger cloudburst the other night was
followed by a flood that took away the
deposit with compound interest, and left
him nothing but bedrock. They picked up
the carrots and parsnips 10 miles down the
valley.
* * * *
“S.S.” : lie came to our door with a limp,
a hollow cough and an expression of im-
measurable woe. He wanted money (or
equivalents) to save his life, his wife’s life,
and the lives of his three children. “I’m
wastin’ away, boss,” he said, and coughed
his hollowest. “There’s me doctor’s stiffkit.”
The words were faintly legible. “This fellow
is a confirmed waster. —Henry D , M.D.”
Another young Australian, H. F. Godfree,
a Ballarat railway employee, is the in-
ventor of an automatic recorder of telephone
calls, which does all it is asked and “works
like a charm,” as the S.M. at Ballarat re-
ports. It has been in operation there for
18 months; but none of the Melbourne offi-
cials seem to know anything about it. Still
there is hope: P.M.G. Webster has knocked
off excusing the' failures of the manual
recording system to “call for a report.”
South Aus. claims to have beaten the
record for quantity of wheat crammed into
one Outgoing ship—4ol,ooo bushels. The
best the others States can claim, according
to the Adelaide records, is 340,000 (in 1914)
from Sydney and 364,000 (throe months
ago) from Melbourne.
“Chinky” : Representatives of the leading
English seed-distfibutories have beep travel-
ling about seeking fresh growers to take the
place of the Huns and their pugnacious
neighbors. American ‘seedsmen also are look-
ing further afield for their warm-country
stock. In certain lines Maoriland growers
have been building up a reputation, especi-
ally for market gardeners’ and stock-
raisers’ supplies. Many varieties hitherto
retailed as the best German will in the near
future bear the Australian brand —-and a
rattling good brand, too. as experiments
have already proved. The sweet-pea seed
trade is a valuable one, and the monopoly,
formerly held in certain Yankee quarters
will be nipped by Australian raisers who are
coming forward w T ith both old and new
kinds. Watch for ’'em! 1 Ask for ’em! And
shun like poison the house which asperses
them!
Not all Australians miss the ’bus. A few
.years ago a preserving co. tacked a modest
sign upon a shed at Stanmore (Sydney). It
worked its passage slowly, changed itself
from a proprietary to a limited co. for the
purpose of getting capital, and ordered up-
to-date machinery. Then the war came ;
and the ‘ concern doubled its plant and
plunged for army contracts. In 1915 it
supplied the War Office with 700.0001 b. of
jam, in 1916 with 4,000.0001 b, and by June
this year the output will be 4,500,0001 b
good business for the co., its employees,
other cos.’ employees, the man on the land,
the soldiers at the Front who havq the
State’s guarantee of pure food, and (if you
don’t mind it*' mentioned ) Australia.
DODGING PROBATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ike Oofbloom had been very rich.
He lay a-bed as grey as ash,
And gulped the little pills on which
They took off ten per cent, for cash.
The doctors said his day was o’er,
But Oofbloom still declined to shift.
His wealth he’d cut up long before
’Tween sons and daughters half a score,
With sundry little deeds of gift.
“Your fight is worse than useless, man,”
The doctor said. “In pain you lie,
Wiien if you will submit you can
Turn calmly to the wall and die.”
But Ike lived, though against the grain
Were doctors’ fees. What he was giving
To nurses caused an anguished strain,
And oft he’d bitterly complain
About the shameless cost of living.
Then came a day when Qofbloom sent
To have a clock placed bv his side,
And at the stroke of one, content,
The good old fellow smiled and died.
’Twas found then by a lawyer wise,
Of whom Ike’s eldest son inquired,
The time required to legalise
The deeds of gift, to his surprise,
Had simultaneously expired 1

Red-Gold.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAD nothing to do but to carol and
whistle,
With flowers all around me and grass
to the knees,
The heart of me light as the down of the
thistle
That hastens and halts at the will of the
breeze.
And the sheelings were many, and many the
dairies
That gave me hearth-welcome and new milk
to drink ;
How could it be else with a friend of the
Fairies
Who had them anigli him all day, do you
think ?
It was they who would sit at my feet full
of stories,
Or sing the sweet songs that enchant and
enchain,
Of dead and gone doings and long-ago
glories
That gleam a gold vision through grey, veil-
ing rain.
‘Twas the Good Folk that filled me with
dreams and with fancies,
I sitting at dewfall with twilight around ;
And some were like tulips and some were
like pansies,
And some sashed with crimson, and some
greyly gowned.
It was they who could raise from old ruins
mossed over
Grey castles with perilous towers at a
breath ;
Or call from smooth mounds and their
mantles of clover
Tall knights armed and armored to fight to
the death.
’Neath the spell of their 'weaving quaint cos-
tumes and manners, *
Old banquets and battles would rise once
again ;
And the mists on the hills would be Meave
with her banners,
And the keening of Deirdhre would rise
from the glen.
It was they who could ’wilder the senses
and dazzle,
And make the green sycamore sing like a
lyre,
And set the far sea-edge ablaze with Ily-
Brasil —•
Its domes and its turrets of gold and of
fire.
With the sand for my couch and a rock
for my pillow,
I’d lie, by the spell of their magic made
mute,
And watch a faun leaping from billow to
billow,
With ever a black hound in silent pursuit.
As the song of the sea and the forest-way
varies,
Now louder, now lower, from dawn-flush to
dark,
So echoed and echoed the song of the
Fairies,
At times like a linnet, at times like a lark.
And one was a lily and one was a daisy,
And one was a cricket, and one was a bird ;
And they taught me the love-speech that'
sent the girls crazy
With a kiss and a coo peeping out of each
word.
’Tis the way with old Time that he marks
on his dial
A moment that fills us with glory or shame,
And the shadow came round to the hour of
my trial,
And ’tis weak as thin water I proved when
it came.
For as I sat courting the Fairies one morn-
ing.
Red-gold, the loud wanton, came by with
a song,
And, all the sweet words of the fairy-
throng scorning,
I thrust their hands from me and broke from
the throng.
At the heels of Red-Gold was a rabble that
muttered
And cackled and struggled and followed like
geese;
But ah for the Fairies a farewell they
fluttered,
Their little hands trembling like flowers in
the breeze.
I am one with that rabble that gathers and
thickens,
And fouls the sweet air with its lust and
its l^es;
And the heart of me sighs, and the soul of
me sickens,
For a touch of her hand and a look of her
eyes.
Though the skylark should sing ’tisn’t I
that would hark it,
With the big lust upon me, the hot, choking
lust; •
The fairy cries cease, and I’m off to the
market
To mix with the pigs and to roll in the
dust.
Ah, the black curse upon her and all her
devices,
Her smiles and her wiles, making hot, mak-
ing cold ;
Her gifts that men die for, her glow that
entices—
Bad cess to the big wanton eyes of Red-
Gold!
There are times when the thirst in my
throat makes it brittle,
And the pace grows too hurried, too fevered
to last,
And the heart of me asks to be rested a
little,
That I sit on the roadside and think of the
past.
Yes, I think of the twilight, the lakes and
the mountains,
The grass underfoot and the clean skies
above,
And my soul breaks its seals at that moment
and fountains
With the waters of sweetness and beauty
and love.
And I cry: ‘‘l was free of the sheetings and
dairies,
Hearth-welcome and greeted by young and
by old,
A singer of songs and a friend of the
Fairies,
And lost all these things for the sake of
Red-Gold !”

RODERIC QUINN.
The Saviour.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
YDNEY Domain is one thing on a
k 5 weekday, another thing on a Sunday.
Always, on a sunny day, there are the
avenues of dark trees with the trellised
shade beneath them; margins of shadow
around the wide stretches of sunlit grass;
cool-looking daubs of shade in seas of vivid
color. But on Sunday there are the massed
crowds that represent all the struggling
ideas, fancies and desires of humanity,
mingled in a discord which somehow, if you
note it long enough, achieves a harmony.
There is the big crowd round the 1.W.W.,
where a band sometimes plays, listening to
the perfervid eloquence of Donald Grant, its
most popular orator,, under sentence of death
because of the. consumption that holds him,
but afire with the idea that he is doing some-
thing for the advancement of his fellows.
There are the little crowds on the outskirts
hotly debating side issues. There are the
Socialists, quieter, more logical, less popular
because their utterances lack the stinging
sensationalism of the speakers in the other
crowd; the Christian Israelites with queer
ideas and an ill-painted banner and a
megaphone to make their statements heard ;
in the midst of all, the Peanut King, who
is willing to discuss any subject which will
draw a crowd; and children, and flappers,
and lascars and sundries. These last
include British-Israelites, Theosophists,
Christadelphians, Agnostics, invalids in
bathchairs preaching a humble charity; the
True Churchites, the Spiritualists, a shabby
orator who declares that the cultivation of
will power is the secret of success, and tells
how it can be done: scores of different ideas
promulgated side by side. And there is the
procession of vehicles on the road; the
pleasant sunlit views, and an atmosphere
of lazy, glorious contentment, spiced with
a vagutvscent of flowers.
The man beside me on the seat was get-
ting old : his face had the look of a philo-
sopher.
“I’ve never missed a Sunday in me life,
barring it \yas wet,” he said. “I know ’em
all; but I don’t listen to ’em much for the
lust few years. I just come an’ look on.”
“How’s that?” I asked.
“Well, I haven’t, since the meetings of
the Disciples broke up. It somehow broke
me up, too,” he said. “I was a Disciple.’ 1
“Were you!” I exclaimed.
lie looked quite gloomy.
“You remember Julius,” he said. “Oh, I
forgot you hadn’t been here much. Julius
was the Saviour. First time any of us met
him he just blew into Sydney—if I ought to
say ‘blew in’ about him” —his voice ex-
pressed a sort of reverential apology—“and
came up to the Tree of Knowledge. You
know it?” he asked.
“I’ve seen it.”
“That’s right. I’ve heard some fine dis-
cussions there. There’s men like Curtin, the
anarchist. He’s a clever fellow, educated at
Cambridge, and was a professor at one of
the universities till he spoke his opinions
too loud. He was offered a job at a fiver
a week, but he turned that money down;
he said it was more than his share. There’s
a group of five of them with him, all anar-
chists, and they live up to it.
“You’d hear more of Karl Marx an’ all
the rest of ’em from that lot in half an hour
than you’d hear in most places in a month ;
but religion wasn’t usually mentioned, unless
it was to show it w r as all wrong, and just
an invention of the ruling classes—until
Julius came along. He was all shabby, and
his boots were qut, an’ his hair wanted cut-
ting ; but there was something in his face
that made him look remarkable. 'He plunged
right into an argument, taking the men
right up on their dogmatic words about how
the world was to be put right, and he
started to talk religion—or what religion
might be. And lie talked it so well that
he pretty well outed the hard cases who
usually had an answer for everything.
“When it was getting tea-time old Per-
kins, who keeps a little grocery, but nearly
always leaves his wife in charge while he
gets down to the Tree, asks the newcomer
where lie’s stopping ; and learning he hasn’t
fixed anywhere, asks him' home.
“Some of us dropped in to Perkins’s after
tea; and there, sitting in the kitchen, with
the light shining on his white, gentle face,
and his big eyes glowing out of it, he im-
pressed us even more. What was most curi-
ous was the way Perkins’s wife, who usually
didn’t forget that she had a tongue, just
sat an’ listened and said nothing.
“We picked out, some way, that Julius
had come from somewhere out in the coun-
try, where he lived all alone with .an old
man. He didn’t know whether the old
fellow was his father, but when he died
Julius felt a longing to come to Sydney and
say to the people what he felt he must say.
“But he didn’t talk much about himself:
he spoke mostly of religion, and it was the
first time most of us had heard it spoken
about in any but an irreligious way.
“Perkins used to bring him down to the
tree every day and on Sundays we took him
to the Dom.
“One day Hilder, a man who had always
been bitter against religion, without being
able to say why, except that he disliked it,
drew three of us aside as we were leaving
the Dom. and says:
“ ‘Who is he?’
“ ‘Who?’ I says.
“ ‘Why, Julius! Is he the Saviour?’
“The idea staggered me; but almost im-
mediately it didn’t seem so ridiculous.
“ ‘He hasn’t said so,’ I answered.
“ ‘But he has been led by some power
which has brought him down to Sydney—
and hear how he speaks! Perhaps he has
not yet been given to know about his own
mission.’

“ ‘Nonsense,’ said Stewart, who is very
religious. ‘He is merely a clever man, well
informed by a lot of reading.’ , ,
“But Milder says: ‘l’m going to ask him,
and going after Julius he says: ‘Are you the
Saviour?’
“Julius just looked at him as if he did not
understand, and then says slowly, ‘I do not
know.’ •
“After a time we all began to think of
him as the Saviour. We didn’t say it out,
of course, because we- thought the proper
time would come when he’d say it himself.
He never drank; he never smoked ; he ate
less and less, and the less lie ate the more •
powerful he seemed able to preach. Then,
one day an old woman, hobbling with rheu-
matics, held his hand, and cried out that
she was cured. She was, too ; and although
she used to get the pain again, he could
cure her in the same way. And there were
other things.
“At last —this was after about eighteen
months —he seemed to be troubled in spirit.
He didn’t hold the meetings in the Dom., but
told us that he wanted to be alone. A lot
of us thought that a big revelation was com-
ing. And it did come.
“He re-started the Domain meetings; but
one Sunday, when we were all gathered
waiting for him, he did not come. Perkins
said he’d not been home.
“As we were waiting, talking about his
non-appearance, Miss Tomkins comes hurry-
ing up, with her baby in her arms—it had
been born some months. She was crying.
“ ‘lt’s my fault,’ she said. ‘I got an
order against him —and the police have
arrested him.’
“It staggered us to hear her, but she
hurried on with her explanation. She had
taken out an order against him in the
Children’s Court for herself and the baby ;
and he, not doing any work, but getting only
his clothes, food and lodging from us, had
not been able to pay. That was the time
he appeared troubled and knocked off the
meetings. He’d been up before the Court
for disobeying the order, and committed to
prison, but she had pleaded him off; but
this time she had let him go.
“‘I only did it because I thought it
would make him get work, and then we
could be married,’ she sobbed. ‘But, oh,
I’m sorry.’
“When he,came out, a few of us went to
meet him, at his request, at Perkins’s. Mary
was there, and the baby ; and when we saw
him w r e couldn’t say some of the hard things
we meant to say!
“‘I have been a fraud,’ he said, ‘but I
swear I did not mean to be one. First I
only thought I had a mission to preach to
.people, but when I saw what you thought
of me I fancied I was really what you
thought. You deluded me, and I deluded
myself and you in turn. Even when
Mary”

because of what I imagined—in doing a
wrong. My idleness itself was a wrong.
M.v wife” — :
