there, talking about mesmeric, power—he
came back to the Dom. after some months:
some of these men who can talk can’t help
it. They must talk somehow. That was all
it was: a young man brought up lonely,
with queer fancies and much religions read-
ing, and no taste for beer, tobacco and other
stabilities.
“But I don’t care to listen to the speakers
since then,” he concluded. “I just look on.”
HAMER.
Blackbirding.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was a golden Spring morning, and the
villagers of Moviavi basked on the
shady verandahs of their umber-tinted
sago-huts, intoxicated by the fragrance of
the atmosphere.
Tall cocoanut palms threw patches of
shade on the shimmering sand, where bronze
girls, garlanded with frangipanni and hibis-
cus, la.\ languorously watching brown
urchins at play! In the midst of them, pigs
and dogs were asprawl. And here we were in-
truding with boxes of shoddy trade to per-
suade the happy-go-lucky Adams and Eves
to leave their Eden and work. 1 was on the
genial Samoan pastor’s verandah, pondering,
as I drank the young cocoanut’s sweetness
lie brought me, the most likely blandish-
ments to beguile them away.
In the yard below capered the pastor’s
flock. They had been at school on his veran-
dah, chanting multiplication tables to San-
key and Moody tunes, and they grinned a
unanimous -vote of thanks when my arrival
put an end to their educational troubles for
the day.
After a game of cricket, with a cocoanut
for a ball and a sapling for a bat, they were
dismissed by their master, but still lingered
laughingly.
•“Sir,” said the pastor, “what you think my
school? I think my school the very good school.
Altogether the native very lazy peoples.
Sometimes no lika the schoola, very wild the
schools. But no matter; I teach gooda the
letter, the number, the mappa. I the very
kind man, all the sama white man. Sir, you
very much love-a to me; I the very much
love-a to you; my wife a the very big love-a
to you”—
Presently the pastor’s pretty wife shyly
appeared. “Ah.” she said, “do you like New
Guinea? For me I no like. Too much the
fever; I very sick the fever. Too much the
mosquito; he very no good. All the time
the medicine, the quinine, very bada th’
tasta. Very dirty the native ; too much thief
the people • very bad man the people.”
No doifbt the lady could have continued
the denunciation for a week, but her grubby
native kitchen assistants rushed out to in-
form her that the pullets had been caught
and the yams and sweet potatoes peeled.
Said the boy: “Huh, tuubada! That time
I bin work along Laloki. X bin savvy you.
That time you boss alonga wharfa, eh?”
I gave him a stick of tobacco.
“Wife belong you stop here] Eka?” I
inquired.
"Huh tuubada! That time I bin come
back village* close behind wife belong me
she bin die.” '
“Huh! You got plenty rice, singlet,
tobacco, knifa, ’n’ altogether something along
boxa belong you?”
“Altogether everything I catchem along
store.”
“You savvy me good,” I proceed. “This
time I like plenty boy. he come make big-
feller garden close up Ela [Port Moresby].
I think more better s’pose you come work.”
“Huh! True you gottem plenty rice,
plenty bullamakow [meat], plenty tea,
plenty sugar along village, eh?”
“Jah !” says another. “New Guinea boy
he no gottem bullamakow, he no gottem
rice, kuku, damience [salt]. But he gottem
plenty megani [wallaby], kokoroko [casso-
wary]. quarumi [fish], malm [yarns],
taitu [sweet potatoes], beko [bananas],
wi-tvi [mangoes]; ’n’ he no’ gottem hard
feller worka.”
“Jah!” I argue. “S’pose you come, you
look out along altogether boys, allasame
boss. One moon, one pound.”
This proposition made brown brother
ponder. So I play another trump. “Huh,
Eka, what for you no got good-feller eye?
My word, this time I come along village
belong you, I see kikeni [girls] he no got-
ten! reva-reva \ tattooing prized by natives
as is jewellery]. What for you stop here?
Buyem girl allasame kikeni along Ela, ’n’
Gaili, ’n’ Hula, ’n’ altogether everywhere
he gottem No. 1 reva-reva. Face, too, he
very good. My word, I think eye belong
you he no good.”
“Jah! Finish, taubada. Night-time I
yarna along boys. S’pose some feller -he
like go worka -along you; me, very good.
No good you talk too strong this time;
by-’n’ by boy he too much fright, lie no come.
Night-time I make talka.”
In. the expectation of success I distribute
snips of twist tobacco.
Just as I clambered into my hammock
that night my head-boy came.
“I think we get plenty boy to-morrow,
taubada. I bin hearem Eka ’ltd people talk.
Some young boys he like go work. Old man
he no like boy go work. He say altogether
white man no. good; all-a time fight along
New Guinea boy. Then Eka he talk this
fashion: ‘Huh! What for you, old man, talk
allasame? You no savvy work along white
man. I bin work along white man. True,
some feller white men too much wild ’n’
fight New Guinea boy. That time I go
work, I savvy, fashion along this feller
white man: he no fight along boy; boy
belong him catchem plenty kai-kai’ [food].
I hearem Eka talk that fashion.”
When I woke at daylight it was to hear :
“Eka he bin bring ten feller boy he like
go work. Eka he say more better you givem
presents quick.” And presently I was giv-
ing each of these ten boys two yards of
prints, some singlets, five sticks twist
tobacco, belt, pouch, knife and some beads.
As they tugged one another into the singlets
and put on the gaudy wraps they positively
swelled with conceit, and to show the
thoroughness of their emancipation into the
ranks of working men. all had clipped their
mop's of reel hair. When they had eaten
as much rice and .meat as they could, I and
the gorgeous outfit strutted into the village.
Natives rushed jabbering in all directions.
The parents-of the recruits clamored; but
the boys were as determined to go away
and earn themselves trade treasures as I
was that they should go.
During a lull in the hullabaloo Eka
struggled through the throng and- said : “My
word, taubada, you-me catchem plenty more
boy this time. You go up along dulm
[men’s lodge], gettem plenty presents ready,
so we givem along boy very quick when I
bringem.”
Half an hour later there dime the patter
of hurrying feet through-the gloomy dubu
behind me. It was Eka, with twenty
excited boys. “Iluh, givem present quick.
taubada. Father belong him, s’pose he savvy
boy go work, he come stop ’im quick feller.”
And as fast as I could‘give the things they
snatched them. The wearing of these pre-
sents is regarded by the unsophisticated
natives as the ratification of their agree?
ments with the donor.
Hardly had the second lot of recruits
received the presents than fathers, mothers,
aunts, sisters and dogs rushed vociferously
pell mell at us. The din was demoniacal,
and only eased when they learned that I’d
sent for a hundred pounds of rice as their
share of the business. Meanwhile my own
boy brought word that he had fifteen recruits
hiding at the mission anxiously awaiting
me, find whilst lie remained to distribute
rice to relatives of the thirty recruits I
already had, Eka and myself fixed the other
fifteen. When they ran down into Moviavi
the excitement went to delirium, till it
looked as though the villagers might tear
the clothes off the recruits. Then Eka,
elated and all as he was by the success of
our -joint strategy, made this suggestion : “I
think more better you-me takem altogether
boys along river, quick feller. Close up
people,he wild: s’pose you-me no go quick,
some boy he fright too much, he cluickem
away clothes ’n’ run away inside bush.”
So as calmly but quick-ly as it could be
managed the forty-five recruits were got on
to canoes whilst a thousand and more of
their relatives stood jabbering angrily or
sobbing bitterly along the river bank. Just
as we got well under-way out on the stream
a canoe with eight more boys sped out of a
creek and joined iis, .and as we raced away
at top speed that horde of people and dogs
made a veritable pandemonium with their
yells and threats and wails and yowls.
Native-labor recruiting is still an exciting
game; but what must it have been in the
“blackbirding” schooner of forty years ago!
R. A. GOODYEAR.

There are Three Marked
Advantages in the Use
of these Dies.
The Russell Opening Dies.
The Dies are opened or closed by a single movement of the lever on the side of
the stock. When a thread has been cut to the required length, the operator
pushes the lever, thus throwing the Dies open, clear of the thread, and the whole
tool is quickly lifted off the finished work. When free of the work, the reversing
of the lever closes the Dies exactly to size again, ready to cut another thread.
ADVANTAGES: —-
(1) The time usually wasted in running back over the finished thread is saved
—about one-third of the total time.
(2) The marring or stripping of the threads, caused by chips crowding into
the Die teeth in turning back, is avoided.
(3) The life of the Die is almost doubled, as turning back over the thread in the
old way causes much wear of the Dies.
